UNDER THE GUMTREE
Art versus Artists.
IN the High Court oi
Human Energy : Art,
plaintiff ; nineteen-
dozen - or - thereabouts
Australian painters, defend-
ants. A jury of the many-
headed Public was em-
panelled, and sworn to try
the case faithfully according
to its best brains and in-
stincts.
The plaintiff’s plea set forth
that she was a lady of good
repute occasionally resident
in Sydney. She alleged that
on many occasions and with
various pretexts during recent
months the defendants and each of them
had privily lain in wait for her and assailed
her, and that they or some of them had
grievously maltreated her. Loosening her
robe, she exhibited to the jury numerous
wounds and bruises which she declared had
been caused by defendants when constructing
certain objects which they called “pic-
tures.” She prayed that the defendants, or
some of them, should be punished for a
warning to evil-doers ; and, alleging that
the defendants, or divers of them, had pro-
claimed a desire and intention of repeating
their assaults upon convenient opportunity,
she demanded the protection of the honour-
able Court.
The defendants’ plea admitted that they
had commerced with the plaintiff, but denied
that they had acted unkindly or cruelly
towards her. It was further set forth on
their behalf that Art was no longer a person
of good repute and character, and that in
Australia in particular she had become no
better than a common woman. And they
pled that even if the assaults had been com-
mitted as alleged* they were such as were
condoned by her evil fame, and were the
ordinary and natural consequence of her
calling, which placed her beyond the pale of
the justice of the Court,
.Evidence was given on the part of the
plaintiff by many witnesses who had known
her in Europe and elsewhere, and who tes-
tified ttiat from very ancient times she had
been regarded as especially worthy of honour
and esteem.
The defendants called, among others, a
member of the staff of a respectable news-
paper. This witness, being duly sworn,
deposed that he was of great experience and
authority in matters connected with kennels
and with poultry, and was commonly known
as the dog-reporter. He declared that his
newspaper having been temporarily be-
reaved of its critic of paintings, he had
been commissioned to inspect and report
upon the pictures which defendants had
gathered together. He affirmed upon oath
that many of the said pictures were emin-
ftntly tactful, and were calctilated to reflect
the highest credit upon the plaintiff. No-
thing, in his opinion, could be better
adapted than such pictures to improve the
breed of dogs, and only a mixture of which
he possessed the knowledge would more
signally increase the fertility of poultry.
Questioned, as to the character of this
mixture, the witness respectfully declined
to communicate its ingredients, on the
ground-that they were a trade secret. He
said, however, that it included linseed. He
understood that many of the defendants’
pictures also included linseed.
Questioned as to what he meant by a
“ tactful ” picture, the witness said he did
not know; but he had been impressed by
another critic’s use of the epithet, and had
resolved to incorporate it in his evidence.
The .Court having summed up, the jury
found a, verdict of Guilty,” with a recom-
mendation of mercy on the ground of the
youth and innocence of many of the defen-
dants. ’
The sentence of the Court was that, seeing
the defendants were divided into two jealous
and hostile societies, there should be a public
admission by each society of the infinite
superiority of the other society in every
respect; and all the unsold pictures in the
exhibition of each society should be forth-
with bought by the other society and hung
permanently in its rooms.
The defendants, who appeared thunder-
struck at this judgment, were removed in
custody, weeping very bitterly.
Struggles veksus Results.
This is a dream, of course ; but it ought
not be all a dream. The critic of local
art is year after year confronted with a mass
of tyros’ work, students’ work, pupils’ work
—solemnly exhibited in the sacred name of
Art, claiming admiration and begging pur-
chase. And there are very few of the artists
who are not convinced that they hold a spark
of, the sacred fire, while as a matter of honest
fact many of them would do much more ser-
vice to the community by continuously sawing
wood. They have not an artist’s brains, and
their immature work will never mature on this
earth. If you look at results , and measure
results by the best —or even the second-best
that has been accomplished else where,
there is little for it but wholesale con-
demnation. Then the artists, who come
next to musicians in self-conceit (the poets
are a good third) denounce your stupidity
and refuse to take your drinks, or give them”.
On the other hand, if you look at the
struggles; if you remember how much
nearly every “picture” has cost its maker
in effort and aspiration ; how conscientiously
the incapable work has been done ; how all-
but-impossible it is to make a living in Aus-
tralia by painting, and what a poor living it
is when made, you have not the heart for
wholesale condemnation. This man has
shown you his broken boots; and told you
he can t afford a better pair ; this woman
supports her mother on the proceeds
°* a ler rare sales, and from the
profits of her occasional pupils, and
though you realise that at best she is
a manufacturer of pretty sketches, you can-
not tell her, at all events, to go and saw
wood—or even mind babies.
Let us try and steer a middle course, and
gloss the truth as kindly as critical con-
sciences permit.
The Beastly Other Society.
. Nearly everybody knows that there are
m Sydney two rival societies—“ The Art
Society ’ and “The Society of Artists,” who
glare at each other’s pictures with a green
eye and a jaundiced mouth. (This is no
figure of speech ; the mouth glares as well
as the eye ; they glare all over.) The rivalry
is made worse by the fact that the Society
of Artists is an offshoot from the older
society, and a disgustingly energetic off-
shoot. The old society lives in a rut of
landscape— it exhibits much good painting,
and few good pictures. Bound at the
Society of Artists’ there is plenty of blur-
blotch-and-splotch, and sufficient pink-and-
green ecstasy and lank, long maiden, but
there is exuberant life. The life warms
you : the life-in-death is tame. If you con-
sider : one is the Art Society—which sounds
a little vague and abstract and ponderous :
the other is the Society of Artists—which
conveys a sense of personality and indivi-
duality and humanity. There is a good deal
in a name.
Naturally enough, the Sydney air is thick
with remarks in cadmium and innuendoes in
gamboge. But the Sydney Society of Artists
was at least magnanimous, when an alarm of
fire was raised on its premises on judging-
day, and smoke came up through the floor :
it hardly waited a moment before rushing
round next door to acquaint the Art Society
of the impending conflagration. Art Soc.
President Lister led a retreat in good order.
Some of his henchmen wanted to remove
their pictures. “Not on any account,” he
waved. “Let ’em burn ! Best thing can
happen us. They’re insured full value.”
And vistas of trips to Paris flashed
before the palette-men filing out. They
didn’t come off: they never do. The fire
was easily beaten ; and the artists sadly
resigned themselves to the familiar telescope-
search for a purchaser on the N.W. horizon.
Then came the Minns mixture. Artist
Minns sent pictures from London to the
Society of Artists. When that body woke to
the knowledge, they heard—flame and furies !
—that Minus’s pictures were hung in the
rival gallery. Round rushed an impetuous
deputation. “ Give us our pictures ! ” The
Art Society was as bland and dignified as
nine dead Pharaohs. “ With pleasure, if you
show us Mr. Minns’s authority.” That
staggered the deputation : it hadn’t any
authority. ‘‘ A little cable, now ? : ’ suavely
suggested the Art Society. The Society
of Artists shook its blond locks, and retired
crestfallen. There was indignant hurrying
to and fro till a letter was produced which
made the Art Society disengage reluctant
claws from the booty. Then their cynic
rubbed it in: “Picture-framer’s mistake.
Sent to the wrong shop. Very natural.
Old-established tradesman. He never heard
of the new Society ! ” Death and desola-
tion !
The Picture op the Year.
The Society of Artists has the more inter-
esting show, and it has the picture of the
year—G. W. Lambert’s “ Across the Black'
soil Plains.” This is something like a pic-
ture. It is a large canvas, not a small
sketch ; and the same work put into a small
sketch would not be nearly so impressive.
Other things equal, space counts : a good big
thing is out-of-proportion better than a good
little thing. The subject is eminently Aus-
tralian and well chosen—a long team of horses
straining to pull a load of wool through a
soft patch every horse a study, and
“you can see the old gray leader
with his wise head turned aside ” in
a fashion to gladden the heart of poet Ogilvie.
That gray leader is one of the things in* the
picture which bear thinking over : he is at a
standstill while all the others are moving, as
if the team had stuck and the renewed
motion of the shatters had not reached him.
As it is a picture of motion, it would surely
have been better painted a minute later on,
when all the horses were moving. Or
would it have been better to leave
him out altogether ? And the horses
are a little too individual, as if every
one had been painted by himself, and
not as a member of the team. And, if it is as
bright a day as the heavy shadows under the
horses indicate, the sky is not bright enough.
But the vigour and sweep of the whole, the
way in which the load of wool comes round
the corner, the effective drawing and com-
position, put to shame such minutiae of
peddling criticism. The picture challenges
competition as the best in its kind which
has been painted in Australia.
Two Good Level Workmen.
Lambert’s picture the key of the sho
but on the same ring hang an uncommon'y
level lot of pictures by that laborious anc
conscientious artist, Tom Roberts —a m
with ideas and capacity to paint the
There is, his “ Circe,” a half-length fema e
figure with nudity accentuated by a veil o: :
gauze, the regal type of the triumphant fles 1.
Suggestions of victims fill the picture,
bowed old head and a fascinated young on
and clutching, despairing hands. Yet I mus ;
say that I see the idea without bein
dominated ; it does not master me and co
vince me : all the apparatus of conviction
there, tnd I see only the apparatus. “C :
course, ” said another artist, 4 ‘ I can pain;
pictures ; but I cannot give you brains to u
derstand them. ” And there we are. But I u
derstand enough to praise the way in whic 1
Tomßoberts has tried to paint and has paintec.
His “Dorota,” a wan woman with a ribbo
of blood in her hair ,* the flesh-painting o:
the portrait of Mrs. W. A. B. Greaves; tl
cleverness of the portrait of Mrs. St. Yin
cent Welch ; the characteristic expressio
caught in the portrait of Alex. Mon
gomery ; —these are some of the things on
remembers.
John LongstafF recalls his “ Lady i
Black ” with a similar portrait, “ After th
Rehearsal ” —full of charm, but not so haunt-
ing as the other. His sketch portraits o :
Julian Ashton and G. W. Lambert ar
masterly.
Successes and
Sid Long fails this year; so does Harr
Garlick. The former’s chief picture, callec
“Pastorale,” is kin with the condemnec
“Pan”—a colour-mixture with no discern
ible intellectual interest or imaginativ
quality. The blended tints have a certai
decorative value like that which makes
nurse-girl call to her charge to “ See th
pretty rainbow ! ” but there is nothing else
There is a suggestion of poetry in the lonely
figure “ Sadder than a Single Star ; ” but a
a rule Mr. Long seems to enjoy emotion
which he has no power to put into
his paintings. Mr. Garlick carries im
presaionism to the point of absurdity
There is still a suggestion of character anc
feeling in his horse-paintings, but some o: :
the animals have stepped right out of th
Noah’s Ark of the toyshops.
Sidney Cocks’s Avater-colours are among
the most charming pictures on show. Thev
have genuine feeling and truth : shoulc.
he not try a bigger canvas 1
Julian Ashton has a large picture callec
“ Dawn,” where a young girl sits up in bed
startled by the young day. Here again yoi
see quite plainly what the artist has meant
to convey, and see quite plainly that he has
conveyed nothing of his meaning. Howard
Ashton’s “Funeral March” is an array o'
clothes prop girls—nearly all on the same
model and nearly all expressionless. An
exception is the last girl, with a Rossettian
turn of head—
Ah, but the back look, lingering,
For old sake’s sake.
The rest of the picture is palette.
Dattilo Rubbo’s “ Veteran ” is an admir-
able study of an old soldier : is the muscle
from cheek to chin too prominent ? Miss
Meston paints grapes with the bloom on
them—real grapes : her portrait of Prof.
Stuart is a failure. When will she
paint such another portrait as that of
“ The Moderator”? Signor Nerli’s sketch of
a Bohemian is one of the brilliant pictures of
the show : it warms you like wine. P. Van
der Velden’s large character picture, “ The
Loser Pays,” spends much labor and talent
upon a subject which necessarily seems
somewhat alien to local eyes. Yet it is one of
the pictures worth looking at longest.
Arthur Streeton is seen in process of
being revolutionised, and to no particular
advantage. Florence Fuller’s “Molly” ik
an Australian girl I love. Walter Withers
and J. S. Watkins are to be mentioned with
some commendation ; there are some fine
examples of black-and-white work ; I would
like to own Fred Leist’s sketch, “ Bother the
Rain ! ” and this whole business of skim-
mingthrough a catalogue of 252 exhibits, and
attaching snippets of comment to pictures
which should be fully described—passing
over ever so many promising performances
without a word—is thoroughly unsatisfactory.
And still (I expose my flank to a hostile epi-
gram) the Art Society is waiting.
Leagues oe Landscapes.
The impression given you by a visit to the
Art Society’s show is that it is an artist
business to paint landscape a. d nmhiim bu
landscape, Three or four of. the socfetv
strongest members, like W. VV Lister an
W ' -i C, r n lgUe '' ifc ’ lrd } nt noth ing else; an
pupils follow in their train until one’s sou
abhorreth landscapes. Albert Hanson
‘ JNarrabeen ” has Australian atmosphere
anyway; and A. H. Pullwood’s “Lake Waka
tipu is attractive ; and Lister’s and Pigue
“it s pictures are painted with much technica
skill ; if they could be set apart by them
selves, or with only a few others, one wouli
find leisure and energy to admire them am
state the grounds of admiration.
Mary Stoddard’s large portraits are inferio
to her miniatures, which are really beau
tiful in their delicacy and skill. There is
line sweep and some suggestion in the
shrouded figure which threatens—or draws
back from ?—the distant city in Joseph
Gaut s picture ; but one is not very clea
what it is all about, J. A. Bennett paints
ceitain rocks, with a glimpse of sea beyond
and paints them well. W. A. Moir’s “Fish
mg Boats off Barranjoey ” seemed to me
one of the freshest and most natural picturei
in the show.
Alfred Coffey is represented by severa
excellent paintings from Maoriland scenery,
which one likes the more the more one looks
at them. H. Moore-Jones’s picture entitlec
“The Spirit of True Love Leadeth Up-
ward ” is an interesting attempt spoilt by the
title and the commonplace sentiment. Little
Eva dying in the limelight is own sister to
the upward-pointing maiden. Joe Wolinski’s
“Interior” shows careful work and gooc
work on a subject that is artistically amon|
the subjects least worth painting.
Frank Mahony’s “Indifferent Neigh-
bours ” is another picture in which horses
are painted about as well as they can
painted ; amd the picture ends at that.
His portraits are very promising. There
are a large- number of other pictures
of technical interest and achievement and
with no particular artistic significance. The
black - and - white section is particularly
strong.
The sculpture section includes models for
busts of Cardinal Moran and Victor Daley,
by Nelson Illingworth, which show uncom-
mon power. Mr. Illingworth is decidedly to
be congratulated. The Daley model is a
revelation. Daley in real life leaves upon
you the impression of a short, ruddy, genial
man with a billycock hat and a beaming eye
like Lesbia’s. Illingworth’s model wines
out all the distinctive colouring, and you see
a shrewd, cynical fellow with a good fore-
head and strong features—another man alto-
gether. It’s most interesting ; and line-for-
line true.
There are ever so many pictures which
cannot be mentioned : the public is exhorted
to go and see them : they are well worth
seeing—for various reasons. And if the
public will buy, so much (he better : there
are some pictures which deserve to he
bought. This is “ Finis ” ; and readers may
ejaculate their own “ Thank Art ! ” by way
of echo.
The Art fellow.
Bookfellow's Competitions.
VIII.—WANTED—A SPRING POEM.
Subject—Spring- in Australia or Australasia, or
any aspect of it. Length—24 lines or less ; metre
and verses as you please. Competition closes
September 19. Prize £1 Is.; payment at space
rates for all verses used.
Stray verses.—By W. T. Peters (Amer.)—
Before I looked upon the face of Grief,
Yea, long before Love ever came to bring it,
I wrote a little song of Love and Grief—
But it was Love and Grief taught me to sing it.
By Rupert Hughes (Amer.) —
There is a welcome in the morning mood of everything,
And in our ears our very blood
hums madrigals to Spring,
The dawn-eyed, sunset-mouthed, noon-bosomed,
laughter-luring Spring.
Reviewing the N.S.W. “Historical Records”
Loud. A tkeuteuM says : -'
We were agreeab'v surprised at the determination of
the N.S.W. Govt, to puhiish these volumes. Their recent
'action, about ten years ago, in destroying all the criminal
calendars, indicated a morbid anxiety to conceal the
details of the lives of many of the original inhabitants,
and was, in our opinion, a mistaken policy. Doubtless it
was caused by a sympathy with the more immediate
descendants of the convicts. After ail, New South Wales
and Tasmania are not the only places whQse origins .are
tainted. A larger number of convicts were transported
to our American colonies than ever were sent to Aus-
tralia; ygt who thinks of referring to it? “Sic fortis
Etruria crevit scilicet et rerum facta est pulcherrima
Roma.” The greatest empire in the world was originally
a settlement of outlaws.
And the Athemeurn might have added that the
genesis of the “true-born Englishman” is as
scandalous as anybody’s.

ECIAL NOTICE
THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCI ETY is prepared to LEND in any amounts,
at lowest current rates, upon FREEHOLD and LEASEHOLD CITY and SUBURBAN PROPERTIES ; on
FREEHOLD and CONDITIONALLY-PURCHASED PASTORAL and FARMING LANDS.
LOANS can be arranged either for a fixed term, or repayable by instalments of principal and interest
extending over long periods.
Arrangements have been made, whereby valuations for country properties can be made for a very mode-
rate fee, to be paid as evidence of bona fides on the part of the applicant.
The legal expenses in connection with mortgages are also on a specially low scale.
The Society is also prepared to entertain proposals for purchasing Reversionary Interests in Estates, whether
in this colony or elsewhere, or to lend upon the security of the same on liberal terms.
Forms of proposal may be obtained on application at the Head Office, or any District Office of the Society.
Head Office, 87 Pitt-st., Sydney.
RICHARD TEECE, General Manager.
ROBERT B. CAMERON, Secretary.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When a burglar breaks into a bank he
should not necessarily be accused of seeking to rob.
He may be only looking for a “ sphere of in-
fluence,’’ just as if he were a great Power.—
American.
Some people have a painful respect for the
law. A man rushed into Carlton (Melb.) police-
station the other midnight with the news that
his 17-year-old daughter had given birth to a
child, and hidden the body in a wardrobe at his
house.
The Test of Love.
“ Two I love, and two love me —
Blue-eyed Jean and brown-eyed Kate.
Which loves fondest ? (Her I wed.)
Prithee show me, Mistress Fate.”
Strolling by a restless stream,
Watched by twain, he thinks it out,
Heeding not the crumbling bank.
Hark ! —a plunge ! —a strangled shout!
Beating down the swollen stream,
Still for help he vainly calls.
Two have seen his danger. Ah !
Blue-eyes gasps his name, and falls.
Brown-eyes, with her trembling limbs
By her dauntless spirit stayed,
Flies across the reeling fields,
Brings her dying lover aid ;
Helps to hold the quivering rope,
Speeds the cry that bids him cheer,
Pulls with all her woman’s strength,
Sobs to see him fainting near.
Then, with lips to cold lips pressed,
Gives him of her laboring breath ;
Draws him, with her failing strength,
From that other river—Death.
Wedding bells ! With heart elate
Walks he, while his choice confessed
Paces faltering at his side—
She who proved she loved him best.
Waiting crowds admiring gaze
At her cheek, so soft and pale ;
While, with love and wonder blent,
Shine her blue eyes through the veil!
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
A Woman’s Farewell.
So with this farewell kiss I taste at last
The all of life; the future and the past
Upon your dear lips dwell.
L >ve will not come again, though I implore;
And in my heart a twilight evermore—
Farewell!
A man’s heart is so wide that I was wrong
To dream that I could fill it with the sOng
A woman loves so well ;
A woman’s heart is narrow, but I filled
Mine brimming with your kisses—none was
spilled—
Farewell!
.So fierce your love was, I was half-afraid.
The roses blossom and the roses fade ;
The withered petals tell!
So high into your heart you lifted me,
So far I have to fall, since it must be
Farewell l
Now all the world I fashioned round me falls;
And from the past one memory calls and calls,
Grieving, and like a knell;
Now all the days like drear regrets shall seem,
And all the nights—the nights ! . . I dare not
dream!
Farewell t
But what if I can hold you, hold you yet
Till all else but my lips you must forget:
If love could but compel!
No ! all the mystic hopes our hearts have heard
Must droop and wither to this wistful word—
Farewell 1
My love was like a little child to me ;
Now in my heart’t is crying piteously—
Hush, dear ! all will be well!
My lips on yours for ever! again
You love me—though it be not true —and then —
Farewell /
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Saint Passion.
Only the midnight stars dimly divine
The hidden sanctuary of my desire,
The dreaming earth trails far beneath the fire
That overflows this throbbing heart of mine.
Teach me the secrets of thine inmost shrine,
O Saint serene of Passion—throned higher
Than highest praise of the celestial choir;
Thine every ecstasy a sacred sign :
Each tear, a tribute to the flying hour,
Each failing sigh, a sweetly answered prayer,
Each joy fulfilled, a freshly gathered flower
Flung on thine ever-fragrant altar, ere
Thine opening eyes make dim the dawn afar,
And flush with mortal dream, the morning star.
D. O'R.

whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 2, 1899.
Turner’s Income Tax.
In his budget speech, a few weeks ago, Pre-
mier Turner, of Victoria, announced that,
in view of the great improvement in the
revenue, he saw his way to reduce the in-
come-tax by one-eighth all round., and
thereby alleviate the excessive impost which
was preventing the investment of foreign
capital in the country, or something to that
effect. Victoria’s income-tax has hitherto
been built after this fashion : The first £2OO
of income goes free in every case. The next
£lOOO (from £2OO up to £1200) pays 4d. in
the £ if it is produced by personal exertion
only ; Bd. in the £ if derived from property.
The second £lOOO a year of income (from
£l2OO to £2200) pays 6d. if it is the result
of personal exertion; Is. if derived from
property; and all income above £2200 is
taxed Bd. in the £ if it is derived from per-
sonal exertion, and Is. 4d. in the £ if
it is revenue from property. The tax is
built on the strictly democratic idea that
the burden should press most lightly on the
poor, and that the man whose precarious in-
come is derived from his own exertions should
receive better terms than the one who has a
permanent revenue from property. Pre-
mier Turner, as already mentioned, pro-
mised, in his financial statement, to take off
one-eighth of these amounts (thus making the
tax on incomes derived from property 7d.,
and Is. 2d. in the £ instead of Bd., Is.
and Is. 4d. as at present) because he could
spare the money. And then in his Old Age
Pension Bill he announced that because he
couldn’t spare the money (because it was
wanted fgr the Pension Fund) he intended
to levy income-tax in future on all incomes
above £lOO a year (£1 18s. s|d. per
week), and to charge the tax on the
whole income when it was over £ooo
instead of letting the first £2OO go free.
So Turner is in the extraordinary position
of giving a very valuable concession to the
people with large incomes, because he doesn’t
want the money, and levying extra taxation
on the people who are making a bitter
struggle to live and keep their families on
£2 a week because he wants the money
something awful.
* * * *
The object of the reduction in the tax on
large incomes is not in any way disguised—
in fact, it is offered to public inspection as a
thing that the Turner Government is rather
proud of. Cornelius Job Ham, M.L.C.,
and Mayor M‘Eaoharn, and other large and
dreadful fossils, have found, and stated with
great violence, that the heavy tax on very
large incomes derived from usury tends to
“ prevent the investment of foreign capital
in the country.” And it is apparently
with a view to encouraging the influx
of foreign capital into the country
that part of the burden is to be
shifted from the man at the top to the
man at the bottom. Why the influx of
foreign capital should be encouraged while
the influx of small-pox and bubonic plague
is so much objected to, it is difficult for any
intelligent democrat to understand ; yet in
some especially drivelling quarters and
localities there is an intense desire to aid
and subsidise this disease —the worst com-
mercial disease that Australia has suffered
from up to date. The Turner Government
is grieved to see that there is a falling off in
the drain upon Yictoria for interest payable
to absentees (the inflow of foreign capital is
only a euphonious term for the outflow of
interest), and it feels called upon to encourage
the drain. It notes that the province hasn’t
been increasing its debts and embarrass-
ments of late with the vigour it used to
exhibit, and it proposes taking measures to
encourage the importation of debts
and embarrassments. Yictoria has been
exhibiting a lamentable tendency of late to
rely on its own exertions and on its own
capital, and to be self-dependent, and the
Government, actuated by the awful
M‘Eacharn and Cornelius Job Ham party,
feels that it is necessary to tread heavily on
this self-dependent spirit, and to take
measures for restoring the old tendency to
rely on Bull whenever a new industry is to
be started or anything important done.
♦ * • *
In Maoriland and South Australia, the two
most democratic provinces of Australasia,
the investment of foreign capital is specific-
ally discouraged, as a curse and a delusion,
by an absentee tax. In Ireland, which was
ruined by the influx of foreign capital
(largely the capital of English landlords who
bought up the soil) it was discouraged for
many years by shooting the agent, by the
famous Plan of Campaign, and by other
forms of agrarian agitation. And it was not
till the British Government interfered to give
the Irish tenant State aid to purchase his
farm, and thus, to a great extent, gave British
capital notice to quit, that some semblance
of peace came back to the distracted country.
The encouragement of the “ influx of foreign
capital for investment ” (as distinguished
from the influx of people who come with
their capital to be Australian citizens) is the
encouragement of absenteeism pure and
simple. Its other names are the en-
couragement of the outflow of interest,
and the increased importation of debts and
difficulties and foreign burdens. Yictoria
has already had a large experience of this
curse, for it was the glorious influx of foreign
capital which brought about its great down-
fall in 1893. A great inrush of money,
borrowed or otherwise, always forces up the
value of property, and its stoppage as in-
variably lets property down again. And
when the influx is very large prqperty goes
very high, as it did in Yictoria, and has a
fearfully long way to fall, and smashes
everything as it comes down.
* * * *
Old William Zeal, M.L.0., recently de-
clared that, to his own personal knowledge,
and in the narrow circle of his personal ob-
servation, fully £1,000,000 of foreign capital
that was sent to Victoria for investment had
been withdrawn . owing to that “vile
measure,” the income-tax. This is one of
the grandest testimonials that the income-
tax could receive, and should encourage Yic-
toria to hang on to it to the bitter end.
Owing to the income-tax, Victoria, to Zeal’s
certain knowledge, owes £1,000,000 less, has
£50,000 or £60,000 a year less interest to
pay, owns £1,000,000 of its country that used
to be owned by some foreigner, and has
learned to that extent to depend on itself
instead of being a slave and tributary to
Bull. And the withdrawal has done no
harm to the country at large. All Victoria’s
lands, crops, mines, buildings, cattle, sheep,
railways, and the like, are where they were.
The “ withdrawal of foreign capital ”
has shifted none of them. And Vic-
toria has actually more cash in its
banks now than it had in the glorious days of
1889, when the beautiful influx was at its
height ; in fact, the country at large has lost
nothing except an immense amount of debt.
But the Zox and Cornelius Ham and Zeal
push has lost something personally. The
absentee used to send his money out to them,
or people like them, for investment, and
they got commission on the investment.
They collected the interest to send to the
absentee usurer, and they got commission on
that. They lived to a great extent by being
the nigger-driver, and deputy-usurer, and
bailiff, and tribute-collector, and parasite of
the absentee, and if Yictoria decides that it
won’t be a slave to the absentee any longer,
it is their loss. So they preach the gospel
that the prosperity of Victoria depends
on its being mortgaged as deeply as
possible to the foreign capitalist, in
having as much as possible of its
lands and buildings and industries owned
by the foreign capitalist, on being in
debt, and on piling up liabilities, and on
having a ruinous interest bill to pay. And
the obvious reason is that the more their
country is in debt the more commission this
middleman push makes on collecting the
interest.
* * * *
One of the loudest arguments advanced
against the maximum income-tax of Is. 6d.
in the £ (on incomes of over £2,200 a year
derived solely from the investment of capi-
tal) was that the revenue derived there-
from was decreasing, though the total
receipts from income-tax have increased
steadily from £140,000 in 1895, to £186,000
in 1898. This means that since the great influx
was hit on the head by the present income-tax
less people are making enormous incomes,
and far more people are making moderate
ones, than there were a few years ago, for it
takes twice as much in small incomes which
pay Bd. in the £ to produce a given amount
of tax revenue as it does of large incomes
which pay Is. 4d. in the £. It is vastly
better for the country that there should be a
very large number of people earning moder-
ate incomes than a few with enormous for-
tunes, and so long as the total revenue keeps
up it is good news to hear that the people
who pay on the largest class of income are
growing fewer every year. It would not
be very bad news if they came to an end
altogether. Any individual who wants to
undo this commendable state of affairs is a
public enemy. And any Government which
reduces any tax because it discourages the
country from getting further into debt to the
absentee is ditto.
PLAIIM ENGLISH.
£5000 a Year.
There is one point which the people of Queens-
land would do well to remember till Saturday
is over : Every “ great ” metropolitan paper has
something like £5OOO a year to lose by Federa-
tion—therefore every big metropolitan paper
which is against Federation is probably a
sordid liar telling the public what it knows
isn’t the case in the interests of its own pocket.
At present the Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne,
Adelaide, Hobart, and Perth dailies are only
expected by their readers to report the doings,
each of its own local Parliament, which meets
in the same street, or the next street. But
when the great political centre of Australia is
in a town which is neither Brisbane, Sydney,
Melbourne, Adelaide, Hobart, nor Perth, these
dailies will have to report the proceedings of
the Central Parliament, or be cut out by more
enterprising rivals. That means that they will
have to keep reporting-staffs and correspon-
dents at the Federal city, and to have columns
and columns of the Parliamentary debates sent
along every night by wire. And the cost to
each paper of the size of Brisbane Courier or
Telegraph is estimated at £5OOO a year.
Therefore there are 5000 solid reasons per
annum why every metropolitan daily should
in its heart be shamelessly and horribly against
Federation, and there are 5000 good reasons
why the metropolitan paper which is against
Federation should be disbelieved from the first
line to the last.
♦
Taking the Bun.
Brisbane Telegraph has settled down to blast
Federation all it knows by the steady reitera-
tion of one foolish dull-brown lie—that the
Federal Parliament and the Federal Govern-
ment have resolved to put a tax of £5 a ton on
Australian-grown sugar. This is a sample of
the awful way the ox-like journal has been
going on for some weeks :
Why should the Parliament inflict on us any tax at all;
especially to raise so much more than we require ; and
more especially, why raise it on an article now practi-
cally free of duty to us? Words fail the mildest man-
nered man to express the indignation felt that sugar, a
product most peculiar to Queensland, should be seized
and taxed, the whole thing being treated by the Parlia-
ment as if it had a perfect right to tax this commodity,
as if it were a common product throughout Australia.
The whole continent, forsooth, is to be regarded as one
State for this purpose, although only Queensland can
produce sugar in large quantities ! Indignation breaks
all bounds, also, on seeing that at present rates Victoria
will save £50,000 a year on our sugar alone ; she will, in
fact, save very much more ; for it is certain that she will
buy our sugar at £5 per ton in preference to buying
sugar at £6 per ton.
The large stale bun for being the boss liar of
the anti-Federal cause is hereby awarded to
Brisbane Telegraph, and will be despatched as
soon as possible—carriage unpaid. There have
been great doubts about the final award of that
bun, but the matter is now settled beyond dis-
pute. The present bun-claimant assumes,
first, that Federation is completed; second,
that the Federal Parliament has been elected ;
third, that the Federal Government has been
formed ; and, fourth, that this loathsome com-
bination has proceeded to tax Queensland’s and
N.S. W.’s sugar and thus to do a horrible wrong
and injury. And as there is no Federation yet,
and the alleged Federal Parliament whose
alleged tax on sugar makes that alleged person,
the “ mildest-mannered man,” boil with indig-
nation, doesn’t even exist, and therefore can’t
have taxed or proposed to tax anything; and
as the alleged Federal Government Which is
alleged to have devised the alleged tax and
sooled the alleged Parliament on its evil way
hasn’t even begun to form, and no one
knows who will be in it when it
does form, the Brisbane organ’s howl
is a marvel of mendacity and uncalled-for pre-
viousness. The whole tale rests on the fact
that the Telegraph professes to know that the
sort of Parliament which the people are going
to elect will inevitably appoint the sort of
Government which will tax sugar—in other
words, it is weeping because its small son, when
he grows up, will have a son, who, when he
grows up, will assuredly marry the sort of
woman who will let the baby fall downstairs,
thereby causing it to be crippled for life. And
it asks Queensland not to federate in order to
save that infant from its pathetic fate. The
paper which invents a Government and a Par-
liament that can’t possibly be in existence for
another year, in order that it may allege that
these non-existent institutions have cieated a
tax which it has also invented deserves a bun
of no ordinary dimensions. In fact there is no
bun large enough to meet the case. And the
genius who, on behalf of Queensland, makes it
a bitter grievance that Victoria will certainly
buy “our” (Queensland’s) sugar if this in-
iquitous tax is imposed deserves 18 buns.
A Gridiron that Wouldn’t Float.
Pity the sorrows of the poor agent whose grid-
iron didn’t float. Great Western (Tas.) Land
Grant Railway Company’s promoter, W. H.
Palmer, wails up and down two columns of
Hobart Mercury in plaintive defence of his
failure to float the big grab in London.
Summed up, there seem to have been painful
differences between Palmer and his co-pro-
moters, and the general scramble for the boodle
wrecked the youthful prospects of the scheme.
But, “in the interests of those shareholders
who had throughout trusted me,” tne irre-
pressible Palmer went to London at his own
expense, and, despite the efforts of his co-
promoters to freeze him out, refused to be
frozen. By dint of bard work and great sacri-
fice he was able to assist the other chaps to
partially launch the co., but just then Brad-
don sent to England “ certain cables at an in-
opportune moment” (these were the cables
which reminded the co. that it had hung round
and quarrelled till its concession had expired,
and it had now nothing to float), and they
knocked a hole in what promised to be a magni-
ficent show. Finding they could not do without
Palmer the promoters agreed to become a
united family again, and now Palmer is back
in Hobart asking for a whole lot more conces-
sions, and he promises, on the honor of a syndi-
cate’s agent, that if Parliament grants the con-
cessions the float will, after all, eventuate.
Says Palmer :
We want to get the right to run a small branch line to
connect up with one or more of the lines that now con-
nect Mt. Lyell field with Macquarie Harbor, so that we
may set to work in constructing the line at three points
simultaneously, namely, from the Glenora end, the
Zeehan end, and from the direction of Lyell. We also
want the right to construct a short branch line to some
coalfields that we know of to enable us to supply the in-
creasing demand for fuel that exists on the West Coast.
And meanwhile Tasmania still waits. The
Great Western gridiron has hung fire for years
because the promoters wanted such a fearful
amount of plunder for themselves. Tasmania
has to do without its railway for years because
of the delays caused by the promoters wanting
so much plunder. And, when the line comes,
Tasmania is to give the private co. such an im-
mense grant of land that if it sold the land now
it could almost build the line itself with the
proceeds. Also, when it gets the railway, it
will have to pay about twice the freights and
fares that the State could have- done with, be-
cause the line will have to pay interest, not
only on its own cost (as in the case of State
railways), but on the promoters’ boodle as well.
And Tasmania is expected to grieve because the
gridiron didn’t float.
♦
More Sugar.
Beautiful specimen of anti-Federal effrontery
from Brisbane Telegraph :
When it was first announced in these columns that a
£5 per ton excise duty would be imposed on Australian-
made sugar, there were persons who wholly discredited
the statement. Some of them wired to their agents in
Melbourne to ask Sir George Turner if it was true. He
coolly said it was.
Now r as George Turner isn’t the 110 members
of the Federal Parliament—it isn’t certain
yet that he will even be one of them —he
couldn’t say that there would be any excise
duty on sugar. And, not being an absolute
fool, he didn’t. A man doesn’t climb to Tur-
ner’s position by being a prophetic ass of that,
sort. The Brisbane paper then proceeds to>
build a fearful and wonderful series of calcula-
tions on the imaginary <£s per ton excise, ami
the thing that Turner didn’t say, the most
fearful of the lot being this :
The above total amount of excise revenue does not in-
clude the output of sugar in N.S. Wales. The latest
return shows that the output for 1897-8 was 24,217 ton-..
At £5 per ton on that also the total revenue would be
£939,755. Our immediate neighbours having to pay Jth
instead of only £3 per ton on imported sugar, will enjoy
a protection of £2 per ton on their output; and Mr..
Fenton, Vic. Govt, statist, says that “of course it will
greatly encourage their sugar industry.” If they make
more on that accouut they may take less of us; and if so,
then for that reason also the new arrangement is against
us.
Fenton, being also a man of some horse-sense,
never said that there would be an excise duty
of <£s on N.S. YV. sugar, or that it would en-
courage the industry. He would probably have
been carried away to an asylum as a hopeless
case if he had. The astounding logic of the
Brisbane penny tatter is that a £5 excise duty
on Queensland sugar would ruin the Q. sugar
industry, and a £5 excise on N.S.W. sugar
would help the N.S.W. sugar industry in quite
a surprising fashion. Also that the N.S.W.
sugar-grower is to lose the protection of £3 a
ton which he now enjoys as against Q. sugar,
and he is to be taxed £5 a ton on his own
sugar, and thereby he will be £2 a ton
better off instead of £8 a ton worse off.
In other words, that he will be protected
against the Queenslander to the extent of £2 a
ton by losing his protective duty and being
himself taxed £5 a ton. Then it winds up
something to the effect that the scheme to tax
N.S.W. sugar at the same rate as Q. sugar
is a gloomsome conspiracy against Queensland,
and as sugar is food, and food and Federation
both begin with “f” and both have an “o”
and a “ d ” in them, the wicked machination is
proved beyond dispute. What kind of liquor
does that paper drink, anyhow ?
On the Importance of Being Well-
Connected.
Oscar Wilde once preached a sermon on the-
“lmportance of Being Earnest,” but recent
events have shown that, in Victoria at any


rate, it is much more advantageous to be well-
connected. The Bulletin recently commented
on the case of lawyer MTntyre —since sen-
tanced to two years’ gaol—who, being ordered
into “ confinement ” for non-compliance with
an order of the Court, hired sundry expensive
physicians and a costly lawyer to impress upon
his Honor the necessity of not sending him
there because of his bad state of health.
Whereupon he was released temporarily on
account of his bad state of health. And this
is just where the discrepancy comes in. In
Melbourne ill-health is a reason for letting the
influential person out of gaol. And it serves
equally well as an excuse for putting the unin-
fluential person into gaol. Compare MTntyre’s
case with that of Margaret Reilly. Mar-
garet was sent to gaol for six months, early in
March last, by the Melb. City Bench on a
charge of vagrancy—that is, for the iniquitous
act of being poor. Her term having expired
on 9th August, she was duly released, but
owing to an unfavorable report as to her
health made by the gaol medical officer,
the police were notified of her discharge and
she was promptly arrested at the gaol gates
and sent back for a further six months for
“ having no lawful visible means of support.”
Apparently this sort of thing can go on ad
infinitum, for it is impossible for any man
or woman to gain a means of support
while doing six months in Pentridge. But
observe the awful difference between the
treatment of Margaret Reilly and of Alex-
ander Grant MTntyre. The well-connected
Gent. One was simply to be “confined” —he
might provide his own food from outside, he
was allowed a fair supply of literature, and he
was not ordered hard labor. But, as regards
the woman, the Law, being notified that she
was in poor health, rushed around in great
haste and promptly arrested her, and sent
her back to the cold cells, and the hominy,
and the hour-a-day exercise, for six months,
notwithstanding the fact that a previous
half-year of the treatment had rather
impaired her health than otherwise. The
same law which wouldn’t let Alexander
Grant MTntyre undergo the mildest form of
detention because his health was weak sent
Margaret Reilly back into ihe coldest and
most rigorous form of imprisonment because
her health was ditto. And the principal dif-
ference between them (besides MTntyre’s
connections) appears to be that Margaret
Reilly had no property and MTntyre had
some, which the law has decided wasn t
honestly acquired. Victorian law has a great
and grovelsome respect for the man with <a’a-
honestly-acquired property.

The Inaccuracy of Chief Justice Madden.
Chief Justice Madden, enthroned as presi-
dent of Victorian Full Court, had something to
say about the beastly Bulletin last week, and
by this time he probably regrets his remarks.
By way of explaining why the verbose person-
age should be sorry that he spoke, a paragraph
from Melbourne Herald is quoted herewith :
The Full Court was stirred to the depths of its tradi-
tions to-dav, when a party to an application sought to
read from the Sydney Bulletin a report of some prior
proceedings in the matter in another court. The Chief
Justice, when he had sufficiently recovered from his
astonishment, said: “The reading of the Sydney
Bulletin will not assist justice in any way. It is of abso-
lutely no value at all, and the reading of it would be mere
waste of time. This sort of thing ought not to be
encouraged. The paper referred to does not profess to
report these cases in anything like an authoritative way.
Some person sends a paragraph, generally ridiculous, to
support a particular point of view. The paper makes no
pretence to accuracy, and is, in fact, generally inac-
curate.”
The Melbourne evening newspaper took care to
avoid any disclosure of the particular matter
brought before Chief Justice Madden, and
which bad been a matter for prior proceedings
in another court, as stated. There was an
interesting connection between the
discreet silence on this point and his Honor’s
indiscreet disparagement of “ the paper re-
ferred to.” J. B. Gregory was the “matter.
The “ report,” which Judge Madden refused-
very properly refused —to listen to was a letter,
dealing with said Gregory’s recent attempt
at convincing Justice Williams that he
(the said G.) had been improperly placed
in a lunatic asylum by his wife. A
letter to the editor is not a “ paragraph,
anyway. The Bulletin took no side with
respect to the arguments of the letter-writer,
but it did publish other letters contradicting
them, and putting what appears to be the
truth of the J. B. Gregory case for the first
time before the public. Herein lies the secret
of Chief-Justice Madden’s splenetic outbreak.
As a baldheaded dandy who waxes his mou-
stache and ofttimes makes himself conspicu-
ously foolish, the amiable Chief-Justice can’t be
expected to have any love to spare for this
disrespectful journal, although, as he nidi-
rectly admits, he makes a point of reading it,
just to see how wrong it can be from week to
week But it was the Judge, not the dandy,
who snorted last week. The Bulletin gathers
from his burst of annoyance in this hull Court
case that Judge Madden knew something of
the aforesaid Bulletin letters whereby poor
Gregory’s alleged grievance was brought to
light, and inasmuch as lie, together with all
legal friends of the “ leader of the Vic. Bar
(Mr. Topp), had been desirous of keeping
Gregory’s grievance dark, Chief Justice Mad-
den considered The Bulletin even more wrong
than usual in giving the unhappy man’s sympa-
thisers, however misguided they might be, a
chance to talk to the public All the same,
Judge Madden, friend of Bar-leader lo pp ,
was not justified in his suggestion that Ihe
Bulletin misrepresented the truth re barrister
J B Gregory, the husband of Mr. Topp s
sister. And when he said “ the paper referred
to does not profess to report these cases in any-
thing like an authoritative way,” his Honor
might have been honest enough to mention that
no other journal of Melbourne standing had re-
ported Gregory’s case in any way, authorita-
tive or otherwise. The Melb. press suppression
of the Gregory case was a flaming scandal.

An Appeal to Beef.
The hollow moan of a certain Brisbane paro-
chialist organ that “ if we federate there will
be a poll-tax on cattle ” is an eminently stupid
appeal to the sordid passions of Beef. And
The Bulletin, in the Federal interest, a so
appeals to the sordid passions of Beet by a
statement of where the interests or Beet le .
(1) There are four poll-taxes on Queensland cattle now.
The horned animal from Queensland PoH-taxed £1
when he g;>es into S.A. ; £2 when he goes mto fasmama
£llos. when he goes into Victoria ; and 15s. when he
goes to Westralia. . .
g (2) Considering that there are four provinces ,whic
believe in an import duty on cattle and only one
(N.S.W.) which doesn’t, the chances are that (sup
posing there are only N.S.W., Victoria, S.A., ,
mania, and perhaps W.A. in. the Co.n.nonweal h) there
will be a good solid majority in favour of a duty
on Queensland cattle when they enter the Common
wealth, and there will be a tax on them everywhere But
if Queensland is also in the Commonwealth there can be
no customs duty on its cattle, for the Bill provides f
absolute Freetrade within the Commonwealth. ...
(3) If Queensland stands out of the Commonwealth,
the Commonwealth can tax Queensland s cattle by an
import duty without taxing its own. So it probably will
tax Queensland’s cattle to protect and encourage its
bull-raising industry. , K .„
But, if Queensland comes into the Commonwealth then
the other provinces can't vote for taxing Q r-nsland s
cattle without also voting to tax their own ,-ix millions
of them) for all taxes are to be uniform inside the tedeial
territt) •K' ueens j and stan( j s ou t 0 f the Commonwealth and
its cattle have a duty levied on them at the Federal
border, that duty will help along the cattle industry in
the other provinces at Queensland’s expense.
(6) And if Queensland comes in and the Federal Govern-
ment, in a moment of impossible insanity, decided to levy
that utterly impossible and absurd excise duty on cattle,
it wouldn’t help along the cattle industry of the other
provinces at Queensland’s expense, because the other pro-
vince’s bullock and poley cow would be equally taxed.
It would only raise the price of beef all round.
In view of which things the Brisbane paper’s
appeal to the basest and most sordid passions
of Beef is rather a poor invention.

The Judge-cum-G-overnor System.
The worst possible use that you can put a Chief
Justice to is to make a Lieut.-Governor of
him, and at some future time or other Australia
will awaken to this important national con-
sideration. Recent official jubilations over
Baronet Chief-Justice Lieut.-Gov. Way, or
S.A., and Knight do. do. Madden in Vic.,
were only to be expected, for officialdom is ever
Tory in sentiment; but meanwhile the dull
silence of all the alleged radical politicians can
only be explained on the supposition that every
member regards himself firstly as a per-
sonal acquaintance of His Excellency the
Governor. It the member should happen
to get qualified for criminal prosecution, he
might, he thinks, feel the benefit of haying
been included in the Chief Justice s
social circle at Government House. Strange
that Australia, with its reverence for much
which is bad in Euglish institutions, should
submit to the distinctly un-English sys-
tem of associating the “fount of justice
with vice-royalty. For the comparative clean-
ness and impartiality of its legal administration
Great Britain is largely indebted to certain
rules of conduct for Judges in private life. I he
ermined potentates of the Law-courts are ex-
pected to keep themselves to themselves as
much as possible. They are not Society men,
neither do their wives run into the danger of
forming many friendships among the class that
has most reason for wishing to curry favor with
them. The result of this judicial exclusiveness
is seen at the occasional trials of influential
persons, whom the Judge is able to deal with
on their criminal merits, or nearly so. At any
rate, he isn’t in the embarrassing position of
being forced to admit that he has met them
and their relations outside of the Court, and it
is this power of social ignorance which makes
the British Judge a “ terror to evil-doers ” all
along the line. The Law often distinguishes
between persons up to a certain point,in the
allotment of sentences, but a criminal’s high
rank or position cannot preserve him from gaol
when once he has caught the British Judge s
eye. The eye that has never beamed upon the
prisoner at a social function does not hesitate
to glare at him in his hour of proven guilt. We,
in Australia, are encouraging a system where-
by the honesty of the Law is subjected to a
constant temptation a system which is
tightening the bonds of friendship between our
bell-toppered embezzling classes and the men
who sit in uncomfortable judgment upon their
sins. In all voung countries their Honors find
it difficult toavoid a personal knowledge of the
more “pushing” and gaudy citizens, though
they may try to hold themselves aloof. And
now we have allowed the British Govt, to
single out Chief Justices for billets in which
they are required to shake hands with, and
give entertainment to, every bounder who
schemes for invitations to Guv ment House.
A provincialist leaflet now circulating in
Queensland feels very bad indeed about this
matter :
The oloven hoof of military despotism crops out in
olause 119, wherein provisions are made that, during
domestic troubles, the Federal Government could shoot
down unarmed men, women and children (as the U.b.
Government did during the Homestead strike), and
which might occur with the strong feeling of the worker
population.
If Queensland doesn’t federate, the local striker
(who has no vote under the provincial system,
as a rule) can be shot dead on the mere order of
the Dickson Ministry, which is responsible
only to the local nominee Council and property-
vote-elected Assembly. And if the province
federates, the striker can only be shot dead
with the consent of the Dickson Ministry and
also of the Federal Government (which latter
will control the military, and which will be
responsible to a Parliament elected entirely by
the people —a Parliament where the striker
will have a vote). Under Federation, it will
reauire the consent of two Governments to
shoot the striker dead, because the Federal
Government can only move at the request of
the State Government; whereas now it only re-
quires that of one. And, at the worst, the two
Governments won’t shoot him any deader than
one. To the Queensland Fat Man, however, it
seems ridiculous that the Commonwealth should
make so much ceremony about shooting a mere
striker dead, so he implores “ all Democrats
to stand by the provincial system, under which
they can be shot quite dead enough for all
practical purposes with no ridiculous circumlo-
cution. _
“ Three Years,” to The Bulletin
Re your comment on the horrors of the Victorian penal
system, especially with reference to cutting off the gas at
Pentridge from all short-sentence prisoners, it may be
news to you to learn that not one of the umcountry Vic
gaols provides its prisoners with artificial light. When
that expensive lawyer pleaded so eloquently for the
fashionable first-offender, t’other day, and suggested, in
moving tones that, if his Honor could not see his way to
release the accused, he would at least order him to be
sent to Oastlemaine or Beechworth, he doubtless meant
well, but he didn’t know. Pentridge is a hell but most
of the country gaols are worse. No fires or heating appa-
ratus; no light through the long winter nights ; two
blankets only, on bare boards, in such a chilly winter
climate as Oastlemaine ; a library that would disgrace
a fortieth-rate mechanics’ institute —Pentridge has at
least a decent library ; church about once a month , the
rest of the Sunday, except for the hours promenade
round a 20ft. yardvegetating in the gloomy cell; the
final dish of hominy passed in at 3 p.m., and the door
locked at 3.30, and the inmate left to stagger as best he
can through the cold, dragging hours of darkness. -No
wonder the old hand prefers Pentridge, with all its
horrors, to a spell in a Victorian country gaol.

A really unique anti-Federal reason from the
amusing Brisbane Telegraph :
Here are the positions—
West Australia (square miles) ... 1,057,250
Queensland ... ... -•• 668,224
Total 1,725,474
Now take the others—
South Australia... ... 903,690*
New South Wales ... •••
Victoria
Tasmania ... ••• ••• ZD >' 3 ‘°
Total square miles ... ... 1,334,269
Take it this way—
Anti-Billite States ... -• 1*725,474
Billite States 1,334,269
Majority, anti-Billite ... 391,205
Add New Zealand •• • 66,5/7,160
66,968,365
How is that for an anti-billite territory ? .
And now add this, that we are to put ourselves behind
a “ wall against the world ” under the union.
If we stand out we have to fight the tariffs of our evil-
minded neighbors, which on the whole cannot be worse
than they are now.
Truly it is an awful thing to have three ex-
parsons editing a paper. Queensland has
already a more massive tariff wall against all the
world than any other province of Australia—in
fact, there are only about lialf-a-dozen countiies
on earth which wall out the world more enthu-
siastically than Queensland does ; and it is
solemnly assigned as a reason for voting
against Federation that it will involve a wall
against the world.” And the other provinces
are “ evil-minded ” because they have tantfs
while Queensland itself lias the biggest tanfi
in Australia. And, on top of all this, the Tele-
graph, in adding up the anti-Federal territory,
reckons Maoriland is containing 66,577,1bU
square miles. All Australia, Tasmania and
Maoriland put together are only a little over
3 000 000 square miles, and the whole British
empire is only about 9,000,000 ! In fact, there
are only 52,000,000 square miles of dry land in
the world, so the Telegraph, in order to make
its argument look impressive, represents
Maoriland as occupying the whole globe with a
bit hanging over at the sides. Also, it sets
that little country down as 100 tunes the size
of Queensland ! For utter foolishness a paper
run by three ex-parsons appears to lick
creation.
Bill Lyne, the restored makeshift who again
leads N.S.W. Opposition, is understood to be
planning another censure motion. But Make-
shift Bill has already had more chances than
any other leader in Australasia and made a
wreck of them all, and he will probably wreck
this one also. The point where Premier Reid
is mainly open to attack is his utter neglect to
pass the democratic legislation he has been
promising for the last five years. A man who
would rise up and hoot at George on account
of his failure to reform the Upper House, to
provide his promised State Bank, his State
note-issue, his Local Government scheme, his
promised system of resuming land for closer
settlement, and who would promise to do all
these things, also to the land and
income-tax, to establish an absentee-tax, a
minimum-wage law, a really large and service-
able State Land Bank, to pay for all unreproduc-
tive public works out of revenue, and to establish
Old Age Pensions, could sweep George Reid
away lfke a chip on a tidal-wave. For Reid
is a weak man ; only he keeps in power because
Lyne is much weaker. And when Parliament
has to choose between following the short,
broad man, who promises reform and doesn t
give it, or following the long, spectral man,
who won’t even promise it—well, why should
it trouble about a change ? If a really strong
democratic leader would rise on the Opposition
side he could leave George Reid with about
two followers in a House of 125. But Make-
shift Bill isn’t even the ghost of that kind of
leader.
Apropos of N.S.W.’s deep indignation because
the Transvaal Government requires the foreigner
to put in two years’ residence before he can be
naturalised and have a vote for the Lower
House, and will not acknowledge that he has a
right to demand complete citizenship, here is
the N S W. law on the same subject as summed
up in the “ Wealth and Progress of N.5.W.,”
page 649
An alien in N.S.W. may hold and acquire real and per-
sonal property, but shall not be qualified for any office,
nor enjoy either a municipal or Parhamentary franchise ,
shall not be qualified to be the owner of a British ship ,
and shall not have any rights and privileges except such
as are expressly conferred upon him. After a residence
in the cokmy of not less than five years a foreigner may
apply for letters of naturalisation, which the Governor
mayor may not grant at his option. . . . The naturalisa-
tion of Chinese is no longer permitted. Oeßiflcates of
naturalisation granted in other colonies do not confer the
right of British citizenship in N.S.W.
N.S.W. steps with a pretty large boot of its
own on the alien’s corns.

The Chief Justice of Victoria made the inevit-
able legal-fraternity speech before sentencing
lawyer MHntyre to two years’ imprisonment:
“ It gives me very great pain to perform the
duty I am called upon to perform towards your
father’s son, and towards one with whom I was
associated at the Bar when I was there. . . . •
You have received a long, laborious, and costly
education, and are practically losing jour
place in a profession which you have followed
with some distinction to yourself, and a social
position of a very prominent kind, which will
probably be lost for ever.” Somehow the
judicial mind seems incapable of taking an im-
partial tone where the punishment of a roguish
lawyer is concerned, especially when the lawyer
is a “well-connected” person who has spent
stolen money in keeping up social style. It
is enough for the public to know that law-
yers are to a great extent a mutual pro-
tection league without having the fact
proclaimed every time a member of the
league gets into trouble. Judge Madden
is paid a handsome salary to perform certain
duties, one of which is to deal with criminals as
he finds them in court without the least
regard for circumstances such as he spoke
of in the M'lntyre case. If he couldn’t
forget the M'lntyre and the father of
the M'lntyre he had known outside
the court, it was his duty to pretend to
forget them. They should be nothing to him.
In admitting himself moved by considerations
of social, not to mention professional friendship
for a convicted prisoner, he exposes himself to
criticism in his character of Lieutenant-
Governor, which brings him into touch with
the class who g i.in admission to Government
House. One may now expect to hear Judge
Madden maudling over the guilt of a young
man who has bumped against him at a vice-
regal ball. Why not ?

A praiseworthy attempt to modify, in one re-
spect, the insensate severity of N.S. W: penali-
sation is the Bill, now before the Legislature,
which provides that (as already the case in
England) time shall be allowed for payment of
police-court fines ; that said fines shall be pay-
able by instalments, and that a prisoner who
can pay a portion of the fine shall be released
after serving the part of his sentence
equivalent to the portion which he can t
pay. Considering that according to N.S.
Wales Comptroller of Prison, two-thirds of the
summarily-convicted persons in the province s
gaols are merely non-payers of fines, simple
economy has as much to say for the proposed
alteration as have humanity and common-
sense. Also, The Bulletin sees no reason
why, in this connection, there should not be
further adopted the eminently rational
though humorous German and Dutch ar-
rangement by which a man sentenced
to imprisonment for a minor offence is allowed
to do his “ time ” in instalments of a day or two
now and again—holidays and Sundays, to wit
so that his family shall not be penalised as well
as himself by continuous deprivation of its
bread-winner.
Considering the freehand N.S. W. public has
given its law-makers, and the decisiveness with
which it has pronounced that primary educa-
tion shall be compulsorv, it is scandalous to find
from official reports that 69,788 children be-
tween the ages of six and 14 failed to complete
the minimum attendance of 70 days during the
first half of last year, and 62,156 similarly
failed in the succeeding half. The Department
plumes itself on having taken legal action in
597 cases in the first half, and 973 in the
second ; but these figures seem only to intensify
the evidence of defective law or defective
machinery. The year shows over 131,000
breaches of law, while something less than
1600 have been brought to account. And the
offence is among the easiest detected, and, with
a proper administration, should be capable of
prompt legal proof. The community has a
right to protest against this feature of educa-
tional administration.
Briefing by the Crown of M.’sL.A. in any
Australian Parliament is a scandal both to the
Member who accepts the brief and to the Gov-
ernment which offers it, and the law which
allows it, if in letter it does, should be incon-
tinently altered. Lawyers creep in through
every crevice of the constitution where the
promise of advantage to themselves is dis-
covered, and, like Garland in N.S.W. Assem-
bly the other day, they will avow themselves
ready to do it again and again while the letter
of the law lets them. This was one of the
reasons why the English House of Commons in
1307 ordered the shires to return “no man of
law as a member,” and further resolved that if
“ a man of law ” was nevertheless returned
“he should have no wages.” James I. issued
instructions warning the people against sending
lawyers to Parliament, and Bacon, himself a
lawyer, said they w r ere an undesirable class to
sit in numbers in any Parliament.
Envelopes franked to the extent of <£ls per
year are now available to members of N.S. W.
Leg. Assembly. Over a score of members say
they won’t touch them, but probably, as was
the case with certain members’ salaries, they
will appropriate the grant all the same. <£lo,
however, leaves members who say they spend
£4O or <£so on parliamentary correspondence
with very small consolation. It tacitly re-
proaches them for toadying to constituents, or
insinuates worse. If the men who spend <£4o
or £5O really know that all their letters are in
accordance with parliamentary duty they
should, now the principle has been conceded,
reject the <£ls and move for <£4o or <£so. All a
member’s parliamentary correspondence should
go free, or no part of it should be franked. A
limitation is a general impeachment of a mem-
ber’s over-toadying propensities or a covert
accusation that if he were given a free hand he
would behave dishonestly.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
