The military drama which pounded the
kettle-drum of war and blew furiously on the
bugle of fratricidal strife at Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney) on Saturday has probably come to stay rather
longer than usual. “Secret Service ”is a drama
with many good situations, and the inevitable
love-story in it is of the kind which appeals to the
feelings of the multitude which has a best girl of
its own. The scene is laid in Richmond in the
last days of the unpleasantness which arose
between the Union and the Confederacy. The
Northern secret service man has got in with a
forged Confederate commission, and his purpose is
to do damage in a mean, underhand way. But he
mixes up love and business in the old ridiculous
fashion. A Southern girl who has learned to
grovel in ignorant faith at his feet gets poor
babbling Jeff Davis to place him in charge of the
military telegraph department ; and then he
doesn’t like to take advantage of his luck to send the
bogus orders which will break upall the Confederate
arrangements, because if he does the girl will never
look at him with the same expression in her bright
eyes again. He is called upon to assist in the
shooting of his own brother in order to preserve
his incognito, but he is worried about her bright
eyes all the time. When he is partially discovered
and shot at one end on spec, she saves him, and
thus he gets another chance to do his work, but he
keeps thinking. And in the end he crumples up
his precious forged despatch, and kicks it out on
to the sacred soil of the Confederacy, and doesn’t
send it after all. When he is captured and is
being taken out to be shot for sending the forged
order which he didn’t really send, his girl gets him
a last opportunity to escape, but she shudders at
him because she thinks he has ruined her country,
and he is too proud to tell her that he thought
better of it and didn’t ruin her country. He
is saved in an accidental sort of way, but by
this time the moral of the story is mixed beyond
redemption. The spy’s brother is dead—killed in
bringing in the forged orders which were thrown
away for a girl. Outside, the air is full of the howl
and clamor of war, and somewhere in the mud
and gloom the blue-coated Federals are dying in
thousands against the ramparts which ought to be
unprotected—they didn’t dream that their trusted
secret service man would turn dog on them for a
Virginian siren’s smile, and that made all the
difference. And Her Majesty’s audience goes
wild with applause when the Northerner decides
to go back on his salt and let his brethren in on
the ground floor at the shambles of Petersburg, all
because of the look in the Southern girl’s eyes.
This is a beastly illogical world.
* * * *
All the s une, “ Secret Service ” is a strong
drama—with a kind of strength that brings in the
cash at the door. The gallery always applauds the
hero when he forgets his duty to 100,000 men who
are not in sight, for the sake of one girl who is
visible. Williamson’s new star, Thomas Kingston,
makes a very successful first appearance as the
Yankee who turned such a gigantic dog (it is
large enough to be a bullock) on his friends ; he is
weak in some small situations, but in two big ones
he is excellent. Miss Henrietta Watson is also
wholly satisfactory, and as a side issue, her resem-
blance to sister Elizabeth grows more startling
than ever. Both sisters faint in the same way,
and when they faint they are so like that a police-
man who wanted one of them for assault and
battery would take either promiscuously. In fact,
there are moments when Henrietta is more like
Elizabeth than Elizabeth is like herself. Miss
Rose Musgrove does her best work up to
date as another Southern girl from across
the street; the company does good work all
round, but The Bulletin wouldn’t like to
swear that Miss Rose’s share isn’t the best
of the lot. Cecil Ward also pleases the
audience in a rather undersized part. Walter
Bentley has two very brief appearances, and
David Glassford only one. Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis
makes a fine old lady, and George Majeroni scores a
success as a mean Confederate in a long black coat
and a soul of the same color. There is nobody in
the long cast who can be described as a real
failure, but The Bulletin protests against the
excessive and unwholesome coffee-colored shine on
the bald nigger’s head. That bald nigger should
remember that he is a nigger—not a “ peerless-
gloss ” advt.
** * *
“The Gay Lord Quex,” by Pinero, was
not really written by Pinero, but by a much older
and more dispirited person whose shirt got mixed
up with his in the wash. At least The Bulletin
fancies so from internal evidence. It is mainly
the story of an old rake, and a young rake, and a
lady who was called out of bed suddenly and
hadn’t time to fully dress herself. There is one
very powerful act in it, and two slow acts
which lead up thereto, and one act of medium
velocity which tapers off from it. The velocity of
this last act is strictly medium. It is a good deal
faster than a board and not quite so fast as a cow
—if the cow is young and has an urgent appoint-
ment. And the really powerful act isn’t
especially healthy. The drama relates to a girl
who is engaged to the old rake (who has reformed)
and loves the young rake (who has succeeded in
keeping his rakishness dark). Her devoted friend
swears that she shall not be sacrificed to the
elderly garden implement, and for her sake
the friend demeans herself by listening
late at night at keyholes which the
elderly one should not be on the other side of,
though his presence there chances to be harmless
enough. And when the door opens suddenly the
devoted friend naturally falls head-first into a
bedroom where she shouldn’t be at that hour.
Then the elderly gentleman locks her and himself
in. His attitude is that, as his chances with his
own girl are blighted by the circumstances which
the friend saw through the keyhole, and which
she proposes to reveal, they will stay there for the
rest of the night and be found and blighted
together in the morning. The rest of that act is
mostly filled with the shrieks and wrath and en-
treaties of a partially-dressed lady whose character
is going to be Lost. They come to a compromise
at last after the friend has been dragged through
the mud of much abasement, and when all is over
she feels years older, and crushed and humble,
and goes back to bed with an impression that a
dray has gone over her.
* * * *
Mr. and Mrs. Brough are three-fourths of
the play—the former as Lord Quex, and the latter
as the devoted friend who listens at the key-
hole. Brough is a strong, clever, consistent
character. Mrs. Brough is probably ditto,
but the part she has to play is unsavoury
and leaves an unpleasant taste in the
mouth somehow. In fact “Lord Quex ”is more
or less unpleasant all round. It is called a comedy,
and the audience certainly laughs twice—once
when the lady pulls up her stockings in an exceed-
ingly public fashion, and once when she falls head-
long into the room in her yellow dressing gown.
The end is only moderately cheerful. Mrs. Brough
finds that she put in that awful night trying to
stop the marriage with the old rake to no purpose,
for the young rake is worse than the old one,
so she swears off eavesdropping at keyholes for
ever. Miss Noble has a colorless little part as the
girl who is between two rakes and nearly falls
to the ground. Miss Bessie Thompson, as a
casual lady who merely hovers round, is a bright
feature in the story; but Miss Thompson is
always bright and always pleasant to meet,
whether she is the whole show or whether she
merely looks in for a moment and hovers. Regin-
ald Dartrey, in another very casual character, is
also a notable success. W. T. Lovell fills a thin
part, and Mario Majeroni one that isn’t much
thicker, with about as good results as can be ex-
pected ; also Miss Temple does some good work in
places, and some good scenery gathers itself round
the unwholesome show like a valuable work
of art surrounding a serpent. On the whole this
writer doesn’t want to see many plays like “Lord
Quex. ” _lt is a clever production in its way—and
makes him feel as if he was wearing the day before
yesterday’s collar.
** * *
Nothing more than usually unusual has
happened this week at Sydney Tivoli. Sundry
new people have arrived, however, and the writer
hopes to go into further detail about them in next
issue.
** * *
True to its threat Sydney Lyceum called up
Boucicault’s ancient “Shaughraun” from the
vasty deep of by-gone plays. It is to run for one
week only ; the old whisky-and-treason play is a
time-worn stop-gap nowadays, and the manager
who tried to run it for a fortnight would get left.
The best part of the show is the scenery, and the
acting of Miss De Winton as Claire Ffolliott, and
J. P. O’Neill as Conn, the Shaughraun. The
latter brief fat comedian has a permanent lien on
the part now that poor Grattan Riggs has no
further use for it. “ When London Sleeps ” will
be revived on Saturday.
** * *
George Rignold re-opens at Sydney Cri-
terion on Saturday with the “ Indian Mutiny,” a
new drama in which the horrors of war and the
wickedness of the black person in rising against
his Queen will be set forth in conspicuous colors.
Therefore the “Kelly Gang” will leave off its
course crude career on Friday night.
** * *
“ Faust,” at Melbourne Alexandra, on
Saturday, turned out to be a tame, colorless, crude
performance of Gounod’s opera, but inasmuch as
it didn’t actually break down at any point, and
was going with more courage at the finish than in
the first act, the show made a fair appeal for
mercy. The average strength of the production
couldn’t have been lower, yet it bore the strain.
To keep “Faust” going right to the bitter end in
any sort of style is a creditable achievement, and
the Alexandra toilers are entitled to all the self-
satisfaction they can get from dabbling in a great
musical work. This “Faust” is consistently
feeble all round. The chorus doesn’t disgrace the
orchestra, and the orchestra seems not too bad for
the principals, who are no rougher than the
scenery. Perhaps the amateur gambolling of the
ballet is a little more painful than it ought to be,
but the sixteen uneducated legs can pass. Among
the principals, theprima-donna, Miss Bertha Bird,
cut the poorest figure on Saturday, for she made
no attempt worth mentioning to act, neither did
she look the part of Marguerite, nor sing it
pleasingly. Miss Bird’s high soprano voice,
topped by some curious trick notes, is wanting in
tone, and very apt to be out of tune. Louis
Luscombe’s light throaty baritone is lost when the
Devil goes down the scale, but Luscombe has a
bold Mephistophelian nose, and a good idea of
posing as the gentleman in red. The Valentine
of young Rebottaro is satisfactory in all respects,
and the new tenor, Ernest Sullivan, gives the
name-character a good appearance and a rather
sweet little voice. As a vocalist and an actor
Sullivan is about 40 per cent, of Henry Bracy
diluted with 60 per cent, of Lord Shaftesbury.
Two Siebels have been engaged, and if the alter-
nate Siebel is as pretty as the damsel who came
along on the first night, she will help to support
the opera up for a week or more. The question
whether “Faust ” will live longer than one week
was unanswerable at time of writing.
Cinquevalli the Great made an astounding
re-appearance at the Bijou on Saturday. Previous
to the cannon-ball turn, Cinque, introduced sundry
juggles which were new to Melbourne, and when
be reckoned it about time to give the audience
one last miracle ere retiring for the night, he
started to play catches with two plates. A full
description of how the juggler caught one plate
would require more adjectives than The Bulletin
can afford. Baby Powers is a feature of the
present “ first part ” at Rickards’, and the preco-
city of that bona-fide infant is increasing every
night.
At Melb. Athenaeum Hall a mixed and
various entertainment of the German-Reed order
is testing public opinion on the matter, and public
opinion is likely to be favorable to the experiment.
The programme on Saturday was about twenty
minutes too long, but the ingredients came up to
the expectations of a full-sized audience, and The
Bulletin will try to say something genteel about
Robert Young, E. M. Alexander and Co. in its
next issue, provided that the encore fiend has been
put under reasonable control meanwhile. Also
further reference to M‘Adoo minstrels and other
matters is held over for a week.
** * *
This is show-week in Melb., when the
solemn apostle of fat stock prods the bloated
porker in his tenderest feelings preparatory to
putting in an evening at the play. Old stuff is as
good as new for business purposes until Saturday
night, anyway, and every theatre witli a reputa-
tion to go upon hangs out its bill -of - fare,
and sings Gorsave the agricultural producer.
At the Princess’s, the last nights of “A
Stranger in New York ” are happening,
but the final appearance of that irresponsible
interloper will not take place before Wednesday
next. Till then he will continue to get outside
the cocktails that never were meant for him, and
the discomfiture of I. Collier Downe will proceed
as usual. Also, the festive Stranger will persist
in asking “What for?” when I. Collier Downe
states his name, and Mr. Wright Innitt will
desire his friend Carroll Sweet to carol sweetly, all
of which is probably quaint and funny if one has
made up his mind to be amused. A revival of
“ The Geisha ” will follow on Friday (Bth).
* * * -&
“The War of Wealth” retires from the
Royal this Friday, and for Saturday (2nd) warlike
Bland Holt promises “For England.” This is
said to be owing to the interminable crisis in the
Transvaal. Although the story has nothing to do
with President Kruger, it contains a good deal of
dead Zulu and British valor, and is likely to fill
the house for a fortnight. Bland Holt has only
two more weeks to travel at the Royal. The long
engagement of Miss Elizabeth Watson ends with
the present season.
** * *
Mdlle. Trebelli, for whom Australia has a
lively admiration of some two years’ standing, is
to sing again at Melb. Town Hall to-night (Thurs-
day). The first time Mdlle. Trebelli visited this
country she had to show cause for “starring” as a
great artist, but this time she brings an Australian
celebrity with her, and the Melbourne public will
welcome her as an accomplished fact. The night-
ingale intends to give only four recitals in Mel-
bourne, afterwards taking the provincial towns
en route to Sydney.
* * He *
If Dolly Castles is the Castles who is said
to have “a finer voice than Amy’’the confiding
public of Australia will escape a boom in this case.
Amy Castles’s younger relative sang “ Told in the
Twilight ” at a Melb. Royal matinee last week.
Preliminary puff paragraphs credited Dolly with
“ gold medals ” for warbling this same ballad in
Bendigo competitions, and a considerable percent-
age of the audience had come with an idea of dis-
covering another “future Melba ” who was to be
even more so than Amy. The disillusionment
amounted to positive cruelty. Younger sister
Dolly has no voice worth mentioning at present,
nor any means of persuading people that she ever
earned even a putty medal on her merits. There’s
no music, taste, or feeling in her warble. The
writer has heard “Told in the Twilight ” (other-
wise “ Close to the Threshold ”) sung many times
since it was brought out by a young Irish tenor,
named Newcombe, at London Gaiety Theatre,
some 20 years ago, but he never heard it so badly
treated before. The Melb. audience, strange to
say, realised the true character of Miss Dolly’s
performance, and didn’t trouble to give her a full
half-round of applause. In the light of those puff
pars., the fraud was too outrageous. Miss Dolly
had all the necessary confidence, also a short frock
and a rather natty pair of young legs. The dis-
concerted Argus merely mentioned her name' next
morning, but the Age gave her “ a sweet and clear
voice, with admirable enunciation ” (in these
respects she didn’t approach the little Ranger girl,
of Rickards’ co.), and said, “Miss Dolly is 14
years of age, but looks younger,” which means
that she is 15, childishly dressed.

A resurrection. Veteran Beaumont Read, now under

in Adelaide as one of the promoters of the movement to
send Meta Buring, a local warbler, ’Ome for training.
Peggy Pryde fairly let herself go in bidding farewell to
a Perth (W. A.) audience. “ Some blubbering idiots,” she
said, “circulated reports before I came here that my
business was smutty and that my songs were vulgar and
dirty. I’d gladly give 50 golden sovereigns out of my
pocket this minute to know who they were” she didn’t
say why, but she looked it. Then Peagydeliveredalectur-
ette on the purity of the modern music-hall stage. Poor
Peggy ! Certainly some people who had been East re-
turned with spicy yarns which they said were some of
P.P.’s gags. The result was that the house was full
nearly every night, but many men left—their wives at
home.
“ Albert the Good " : Countess Owbridge, in “ The Gay
Lord Quex,” is evidently a hardshell Baptist. One
marvels that she should invite ashe-manieurist to “ make
herself at home” at Faunce.v Court. Writer knows of
countessess of the Owbridge type (Bulletin is here per-
mitted a glass of water), and they wouldn’t allow a she-
manicurist within 10ft. of the garden-wall—in an unpro-
fessional capacity. Of course, the invitation is essential
to the plot, but this god jibbed when she took her young
man along—who made his uninvited self at home and
fired insults at the gay Quex. Writer also knows lots of
archbishops who invite the village blacksmith to the
palace, urging him to strike the grand-piano and make
himself generally musical. It would have been interest-
ing had Pinero put on a chiropodist’s establishment—the
spectacle of my lord’s salient feet being primed of in-
growing nails might have lifted the froth from the beer
of tragedy.
There is something guileless and calculated to make
the heathen smile in Bishop Wakefield’s explanation of
how he came to call “ The Gav Lord Quex ” the most im-
moral play that was ever put upon the British stage:
“ My statement before the Church Missionary Society
was exaggerated. I exaggerated purposely. It was the
only way to attract attention.” He then goes on to say
that he never saw the play in question, but thought it
was bad because “The Sign of the Cross” was bad. It
is observations of this sort that cause the modern sinner
to have hopes that perhaps Bishop Wakefield’s Deity
“purposely exaggerated” when He made those unpleas-
ant remarks about hell-fire.
They had queer ideas about the niceties of gentlemanly
behaviour in the time of Louis XIII., to judge by “ The
King’s Musketeer,” at Sydney Her Majesty’s. D’Artagnan,
ushered into the cabinet of the awesome Richelieu, ob-
serves a MS. lying Upon the table. Whereon he sits,
takes it up, and calmly proceeds to read it!! And
H miilton’s “Red Duke,” catching him in t he act, doesn’t
even pack him off to the Bastille for the liberty !
“ C. Clyde ” : “ Bendigo,” writing re Amy Castles, asks
why the Roman Catholics get “ narked” when their dis-
position to league together is commented on, whereas
Jews and the Presbyterian Scotch are rather compli-
mented by any observation on their clannishness?
“ Bendigo” forgets that, in the first case, it is a'-»fhestion
of religion, plus—very much plus—nationality. The
Jews look upon themselves as a race apart, not a cfeed
apart, as is evinced by the fact that they do not prose-
lytise, and rather hold the manufactured Jew in con-
tempt. In the case of the Scotch Presbyterian (the
modern Jew) the clannishness is for the Scotch, not the
Presbyterian, a Catholic Scotchman being nearer his soul
than a Presbyterian son of Erin. Hence the fact that
Jews and Scotchmen don’t get “ narked.” Patriotism is
recognised as a harmless imbecility ; sectarianism is a
disgraceful anachronism.

The local “Georgia Magnet”— a lady who earns a
living on the stage by letting the audience try to lift her,
and find that it can’t do it worth a copper—was sued in
Melbourne recently for board, lodging, washing, and
other sordid details. In evidence it was alleged that the
Magnet had egg-flip first thing in the morning, ham and
eggs for breakfast, stout and oysters at 11 a.m., much
steak for lunch, and a good deal of mutton for dinner. In
fact, the Secret of her Strength, it appears, is not a
scientific application of hitherlo-unknown forces nor a
combination of latent elements, but just Vittles.
Reported that various members of the
N.S. W. Govt, have led friends of their own away
to a lonesome spot, and there breathed it into
their ears under a dreadful oath of secresy that
the Governor has promised a dissolution if Lyne’s
expected motion of censure is carried. These
friends, being leaky and garrulous, have breathed
it to other friends, and some members who might
otherwise have voted against the Government
begin to wobble a little. Of course the Govern-
ment couldn’t tell such a tale openly, else
His Ex. would probably give it a flat contradiction,
but a lot can be done by breathing in strict con-
fidence on a carefully-selected friend.
The Bulletin makes it a rule not to pub-
lish jam from its friends, but as this mixed testi-
monial (Hansard report) is from an enemy it may
pass :
This great Sanhedrim of the press is headed by the
Bulletin. The Bulletin supports the Constitution Bill.
The Bulletin —an infidel paper—a notoriously infidel
paper—a republican paper—a disloyal paper—delights in
the Bill because it is disloyal. Australia asked the great
Sanhedrim of the press : why must we federate ? And
the great Sanhedrim replied: “For the blood, for the
destiny, for defence,” and it was a very good answer.
When New South Wales asked the press why should New
South Wales he sacrificed that this federation should come
about, the great Sanhedrim of the press, the Bulletin at
their head, replied : “ We have a law, and by our law
New South Wales must be sacrificed that Australia may
federate ! ” That is the position of things with regard to
the mighty and intelligent press. lam sorry, at this time
of life, to see these grand old papers following in the wake
of an infidel paper, a paper that is disloyal to its heart's
core, and republican in its very essence. They have fol-
lowed that paper throughout the whole of this fight. I
say, also, with all respect—although I am bound to speak
plainly—that the articles in the Bulletin have been
simply intellectual, magnificent, and apparently, from
their point of view, unanswerable. That is the paper
that has fought this battle for twenty years, and which
has brought this country to the condition in which it is
at present; and when the Commonwealth Bill has passed,
it may chuckle and be certain that the division of the
empire is not far off. —Hon. N. Hawken, in N.S.W.
Legislative Council, 16th August, 1899.
And after all this Hawken voted for the Federal
address.

• * • •
A painfully amateur financier, one Armstrong,
M.L.A., is trying to demonstrate in Queensland that the
alleged possible saving of interest by a Federal conversion
and consolidation of the public debt is only a weak inven-
tion. Yet any person of reasonable capacity ought to be
able to understand such a simple process. When Aus-
tralia is federated, and the new Government has had
time to settle down in its office and hang up its hat, it
will probably take the public creditor by the button-hole,
and fix him with its glittering eye, and address him thus :
“ My friend, you hold £21,01)0,000 of Queensland (or Vic-
torian, or N.S. VV.) 4 per cent, stock which has still, on an
average, say, 20 .years to run ; also a lot of 3J per cents.
You have only the security' of Queensland (or N.S.W., or
Victoria) alone for the money. That security
doesn’t look so good as it did, for these provinces
have given up the right to raise money at their
own pleasure by customs and excise to pay the interest
with. We, the Federal Government, have the
sole right to decide what the customs and excise revenue
shall be. We offer you our bonds with 3 percent, interest,
an extension to, say, 40 years, and the security of all
Australia and all its customs and excise revenue and all
its other assets, if you will surrender these securities. If
you don’t agree you don’t get the extra security." And
in 99 cases out of 100 he probably will agree, and thereby
save Queensland from £250,000 to £300,000 a year in-
terest. And if Queensland doesn’t federate all it can do
is to fix its creditor with a very much smaller and less
efficient eye and say : “You hold £21,000,000 of 4 per cent,
stock with, say r , 20 years to run, and a lot of 34 per cents.
Will you agree to take 3 per cent, with no better se-
curity'?” And in 100 cases out of 100 the public creditor
will reply by expressing the opinion that the province
has been drinking.
* * * *
Mr. H. S. Scott, general agent for the First
Sydney and New South Wales Tontine Investment Fund,
writes : —“ In view of an inference which might be drawn
by some reader > of your last week’s issue, will you permit
me to say' that the position of the Perpetual Trustee Co.,
Ltd., in relation to the Fund is solely that of trustees,
and they are not in any way concerned in the foundation
of the Fund. All moneys are paid, by members joining,
direct to the insuring companies or to the trustees; my
position is simply intermediary as broker for the proposals.
Any' incentive to “ drop a brick on the other fellow’s
head ” is avoided by a term of the Trust deed providing
that if at any time during the Tontine period the number
of members in the Fund should be reduced to five, such
five members at once become owners of the accumulated
fund, pro rata as to the amount and age of their policies.
With regard to the commissions—certainly I get them.
My principal business is that of writing life-assurance
business, aud the fact that policies are taken out with a
view to applying them to a special purpose such as this
fund, does not affect my right to the commissions. The
person insured has no more interest in the commission
earned than an advertiser might have in the commis-
sion The Bulletin allows to the representatives of its
advertising department. Of course the rotten system of
returning commissions or part of them has become so
common here (whether in advertising, insurance, or any
other commission business) that the idea of the agent,
and the agent only, being entitled to the full amount of
his own commissions may strike some people as quite
strange. Your suggestion that some company might
start a Tontine Fund, and form the commissions into a
nucleus for after-division among the members, practically
would amount to insurers drawing the commissions on
their own cases for their own benefit. In the same direc-
tion it might be suggested that if the Perpetual Trustee
Co., or some other co., were to start, say, a new Bulletin,
the profits might be distributed among the advertisers
and subscribers, instead of the said profits being as now
divided among what I heard a Domain orator define not
long since (apropos of one of your contemporaries) as a
‘ grasping and greedy proprietary.’ ”
* * * *
Pitt, Son and Badgery, Limited, is yet another
Sydney wool and produce concern which is suffering from
bad times, though its sufferings this year are not quite so
bad as they have been. The last three years’ profits of
this co. have been as follows :
1897 .. .. £1,841 I 1899 .. .. £4,043
1898 .. .. 2,731 |
There has been no dividend for a considerable time, the
earnings being monotonously “ transferred to a special
reserve to meet bad and doubtful debts.” How much the
bad and doubtful debts are supposed to amount to,"and
how far the special reserve has got towards covering them
the balance-sheet doesn’t profess to explain. But the co.
has a paid-up capital of £50,000 and bank overdrafts of
£65,437, so it is heavily burdened. Its assets include an
item called “ goodwill, plant, office furniture, &c., £25,000.”
How much of this is goodwill doesn’t appear, yet the good-
will of a concern which is unable to pay dividends is of no
visible present value, and this paper item ought to appear
by itself in order to give a reasonable idea of-the co.’s real
position. In the absence of any information as to how
much of the assets consist of goodwill and how much con-
sist of bad and doubtful debts not yet provided for, the
balance-sheet throws very little real light en the state of
affairs. The information which it supplies is mainly to
the effect that, when the bad and doubtful debts and the
goodwill (whatever they may be) are written off, and the
big overdraft is paid, the co. will be fairly entitled, on
last year’s earnings, to pay 8 per cent, dividends.

1894 and 1895, 5 per cent, in 1896, and 6 per
cent, in 1897. Also, it has paid pretty regularly a bonus
of 10 per cent, to policy-holders on their premiums.
This year it pays 6 per cent, to shareholders, the usual 10
per cent, bonus to policy-holders ; adds 500 to reserve
fund, making it £4500 ; and, including surplus from last
year, it, has £l2OO to carry forward. The Farmers Co-
operative is, in its way, the most remarkable insurance
office in Australasia, and one of the most remarkable on
earth. It does a very small business as yet; though
there is a steady increase the net premiums, so far,
haven’t g-ot beyond £5OBO for a year. In eight years it
has accumulated £5OOO reserves (reserve fund 14500 and
re-insurance reserve £1100), which is more than a year s
net premium income. Also, it has a paid-up capital of
£49,3ll—equal to nearly ten years’, net premium income.
The Bulletin doesn’t know another fire office in the
world which is founded on such a tremendous rock of
financial solidity as the Farmers’ Co-operative of Canter-
bury, or which has so vast a mass of funds in proportion
to the business done. If there is any joy in being about
the strongest fire office in the world this little Maoriland
co. can have it, for it has got there right enough.

In 1892 the Castlemaine Brewery had £BO,OOO reserve-fund

the reserves it has now are £3590, the rest, together with
ail the recent earnings, having gone into the bottomless
pit of “ depreciation.” Also, it seems probable that
depreciation will swallow the earnings for some time
longer, and there is a paper-asset of £5llO (debenture-
flotation account) to write-off before dividends come in
sight again. Still, there is a visible improvement since
’97, and the Castlemaine Brewery ought, to be one of the
survivors of the great beer-boom and the great beer-slump
that followed it.
* * » *
The Cascade Brewery Co. (Hobart) issues a
balance-sheet which also goes to show that beer isn’t
what it was. The half-year’s profit was £2668, and a 3
percent, dividend, and dividend-tax thereon, absorbed
£2658, so the co. only earned its 3 per cent, with £lO to
spare. The Cascade co. used to revel in 10 per cent, divi-
dends seven years ago, but since then it has gone the way
of most breweries —only it has gone not quite so far as
some of them. Under Federation there may be brighter
times ahead.

* * * *
Fitzgerald’s Brewing and Malting Co. (Castle-
maine, Vic.) is a conspicuous example of a brewery that
paid its 10 and 12.) per cent, dividends through the worst
time 3 of the Victorian slump, simply because it never
floated itself into a huge inflated co. in the booin-days, or
took to issuing £150,000 worth of debentures or similar
foolishness. It just stuck closely to its vats, and sold its
beer, and let the boom pass unnoticed. Last year’s profits
were £6456, and the 10 percent, dividend absorbs £4OOO
of this. The paper asset of “goodwill” is now finally
written off, and the co. finds itself with £40,000 paid-up
capital, £11,838 of reserves, and no liabilities worth men-
tioning.

* * * *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made lor testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not only give, a statement of metallic contents, but
owners also will be advised by an experienced, practical
metallurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in
bulk, whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation,
smelting, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should
not be less than lib. nor more than 10lb.
* * * *
Silverton Tram directors are becoming ab-
solutely reckless ; at one fell swoop they have reduced
rates so that, if traffic doesn’t as a consequence increase,
they will lose something like £4OOO a year— i.e., they
will only make £82,575 this year instead of the £86,575
which they made last year, on a paid-up capital of
£200,000. No less than 36 lines have been boldly attacked.
For the future Broken Hill will have its pearl barley
carried 35 miles for 15s. 7d. per ton, instead of 21s. 5d.,
and it rejoices in the tact that it can have either Bar-
celona nuts or split peas at precisely 2s. lid. per ton less
than it has been payinur. On the strength of this, and of
the further far-reaching' reduction in the freight of lime-
juice and maizena, Broken Hill proposes to give itself up
to a period of wild rejoicing, and has invited Gov.
Beauchamp up to join in.
There are, however, three or four more substan-
tial lines affected by the co.’s new tariff, which is to come
into force Oct. Ist; but even as to these, here are the new
charges as compared with the S.A. Govt, tariff for 85
miles (the Silverton line is 35 miles long) :
Govt. Co.
Flour, bran, potatoes 4s. lid. ss. lOd.
Fruit and Vegetables ss. sd. ss. lOd.
Chaff, etc 4s. lid. Bs. 9d.
And this which follows is a comparison of the S.A Govt,
freight for the last 35 miles before reaching Cockburn
(the border station) on the journey from Adelaide, and
the co.’s freight for the 35 miles past Cockburn :
Govt. Co.
Flour, &c. .. Is. sd. ss. lOd.
Fruit, Vegetables 2s. 2d. ss. lOd.
Chaff, &c. .. Is. sd. Bs. 9d.
Compare with Victoria and N.S.W. Govt, rates (still for
a single 35 miles): —
Co. Vic. N.S.W.
Flour, bran .. .. ss. lOd. 4s. 6d. ss. 3d.
Potatoes .. . ss. lOd. 3s. 2d. 4s. sd.

Fruit and Vegetables ss. lOd. 6s. Id. 4s. sd.
And of course any Govt., having carried goods 300 miles,
will carry them on a further 35 miles for approximately
one-third of the rates here quoted.

The Occidental G.M. (Cobar) annual report
shows that, taking into account gold in transit and work-
ing materials and stores, &c., on hand, the co.’s indebted-
ness (incurred for extension of dam, buying cyanide-
plant, &c.) has been reduced to but a little over £lOOO.
The balance of profit and loss for the year (operations
were suspended for five months for want of water) is
£5034. Development now proceeds vigorously and the erec-
tion of 10-head additional stampers, for which driving-
power is already available, is contemplated. As 60,000
tons tailings remain to be treated, there is still two years’
work for the cyanide plant ahead of the battery. Average
yield free gold per ton, 3dwt. 16gr. ; average extraction
per ton of tailings by cyanide, about 2dwt. Bgr. Which is
interesting as throwing light on the prospects of the
Cobar Gold Mines, now beginning wholesale operations
on same line of lode.
* * * *
The Mount Hope Copper Mine, N.S.W., floated
into a co. of-24,000 £1 shares in 1881, has been worked on
tribute for the past 13 years, and has paid regular half-
yearly divs. of 6d. per share. This co. had a surplus of
£2600 in July last, and from that paid a div. of (id. per
share (amount, £600), carrying forward £20..0 balance.
The machinery at mine, greatly out of repair at the end
of last year, was thoroughly overhauled and repaired at
commencement of this, necessitating closing of the mine
for about three months. For the old Cornish jigs there
have just been substituted Hancock’s jigs, a recent trial of
which was most satisfactory.
* • * *
The trick mine, Lake George, has shut down its
smelters, “pending the discovery of payable sulphides.”
The construction of a 1200-ton-per-week cyanide plant, to
treat the gossan, proceeds.

heard a great deal of the work done by the experimental
furnace, but it was only the other day writer was reliably
informed that; it was brought about by the use of
“ roaster” slags from the reverberatory furnace, which is
a horse of another color altogether in reckoning treatment-
cost.
* * * *
Carbolite, produced from powdered coke and
molten iron-smelting slag, is expected to supersede cal-
cium carbide as an acetylene-gas generator. Claimed
that carbolite of equal efficiency can be made at one-tenth
the price of carbide. It is to be made on a great scale at
Newhausen-on-Ithine, the power for the necessary elec-
tric current being derived from 20,000 horse-power water
motors. A company is being engineered, or talked of,
in Sydney to acquire the Australian rights of the inven-
tion.

Dredging for gold is indeed the order of the day.
On the Russell River, N.Q., all available area has been
pegged for dredging. The local syndicates reckon that
2 grs. gold to the cubic yard will pay.

Mt. Reid (Tas.) in London asserts
that there is no reason whatever for depreciation in
shares. The 100 tons sample has not yet been treated by
the new process, and if it fails other methods are
available. ’Twas ever thus. Evident that the mine is
not exactly what it has been cracked up to be. Shares
often ascend on gas, but seldom fall without good reason.

The Wilfiey concentrator has only been a short
time before the mining public, but has already demon-
strated its superiority, over 1200 being sold within the
past two years. This machine has a capacity of from
25 to 30 tons per day, and will save more sulphuretts,
float gold, amalgam or quicksilver, and make a cleaner
and better separation than any belt concentrator made.
It can be easily and rapidly adjusted while in motion, and
requires no expert attention. The Aus J ”al Otis Engineer-
ing Coy., South Melb, have been appointed sole manufac-
turing agents, and are now engaged constructing machines
for the colonies. The price of the machine is £ll5.

Mt. Lyell The Aiigus correspondent works
out the profit of last month at £1 13s. a ton. Value of
ore, £3 Bs. Each day’s profit £1272, or for a year £463,000.
To this must be added profit made from treating ores
from other mines, the chief of which is the Tharsis. Ore
value 3 195., made up of 5 per cent, copper with a little
gold and silver. Deducting Lyell’s total costs, 355. per
ton, and the amount per ton paid to the Tharsis, and
there is a big profit left. But that is not all. The Tharsis
pays for delivering and mining its own ore, for manage-
ment and other expenses, and supplies Lyell with a flux —-
silica.. On the basis of last month’s supply, 2317 tons, the
parent mine must make a profit of over £20,000 a year
from this mine alone ; and it is only a matter of time
when this source of profit will be much greater.
Woodlark Island, a New-Guiuea scheme lately
placed with the Sydney public, may be all right, but
there is far 100 much newspaper “yabber” about it.
Writer has read many paragraphs about 14cwt. of con-
centrates treated at Dapto with a result equal to 220 z.
gold per ton. Also, “ Numerous assays have been made
in Sydney yielding from 3dwt. to 71oz. per ton, and some
of the tailings have gone as high as 13oz. to the ton.”
Which is all very indefinite. The 14cwt. of concentrates,
for instance, may have come from one ton or from 50. In
one case they would be of great value, in the other pro-
bably worthless. Anyhow, it is a safe thing to be cautious
about all assay-mines.

A warning from British Columbia. Dear
Bulletin. —Let Australians think well before coming here
in search of gold. The officials here (Atlin City)—like
those at Dawson City—simply worship the dollar, which is
truly “almighty” here. I was born near Bendigo,
washed dirt in Ballarat, was a pioneer of Ooolgardie, and
I have never seen anything to come near the rotten laws
of this province. The so-ca led Government treats the
people like Indians instead of British subjects. At present
there are about 6000 men on the field, only about 1000 of
whom are working, as the great majority of the claims
are the subject of disputes which the Commissioner says
he has no power to decide. They charge you 2£ dollars
for recording a placer claim ; but, if said claim be jumped
20 different times, they will take the money from, and
issue records to, each person. Work can only be carried
on here 5 months out of the 12 —June to October, in-
clusive ; rest of the year the ground is frozen hard. We
are at present waiting for the judge from Victoria ; and,
as he is not likely to come before Aug., there can be little
mining done this year. There are a good many Austra-
lians here, but most of them are looking forward to getting
home again.—M.B.K.
* * *
Dear Bulletin.—Herewith some facts re the
much-talked-of Mambare River goldfields, British Papua,
which can be chopped-up and served as beer or gall to
intending New Guinea miners. In the first place, the
field is stone-dead ; most of the men are ditto ; and the
men who crack it up ought to be. Half of those going to
come will peg-out on sight, and be a nuisance to the men
living here, giving them much extra graft in burying
them. There’s nothing to warrant a rush, and never
was, the biggest pile ever leaving the field, after a
year’s extra lucky work, was not 200 oz All men here
now are fossicking, and poor at that, barely making
tucker. If a new gully breaks out it’s rushed by
the men on the spot, and is worked out in a week or two.
Then you poor benighted Australians hear the news six
months after, and reach out with both hands for Papua.
This scribe was one of them, and seven of his shipmates
out of 50, arriving six weeks ago, have died, and the other
43 are two days a week in their tents, laid-out with fever,
trying to scratch enough together to get out of the
accursed place. The country is a real old hard one to
battle in. It is healthy in a way, if a man would only
look after himself ; but he can’t. The place is only fit for
frogs to live in, it’s their paradise—incessant rain ; im-
penetrable wet scrubs, and rushing creeks everywhere,
with mountains to clamber over that would" wither
Kosciusko. Tucker is dear —lib. tin of meat. Is. 6d. ; tin
of milk Is. 3d. ; biscuits Is. 3d. per lb. ; rice Bd. per lb. ;
flour 365. a 501 b. tin ; and everything else in proportion ;
no vegetables, and plenty of good hard tack at a bob a
nip. About 120 men on the field, and most of them
possessed of a big rat. No man should come to New
Guinea with less than £lOO,, and never leave Samarai
without recruiting a few boys as native carriers at £3 10s.
per head ; without them he’s useless and ought to go
back home. If a man can afford to wait in this country
and keep his health he may make a rise.— Kiriwina.
Mambare Goldfield, Papua, 26th June, 1899.
Recently-returned Samarai (Papua) gold-tniner :
“If I owned Samarai and hell, I’d live in hell and rent
Samarai cheap.”

Said that the first Governor-Gen. of Aus-
tralia will be the faded Marquis of Lome, whose
spouse, the Princess Louise, is prepared to come
along and keep him in countenance for one year,
or longer, should she like the country. But she
is not expected to like it much, consequently
Lome, if he comes at all, will take the appoint-
ment for twelve months with the option of a re-
newal by his overshadowing Louise.

Under Federation and a sternly-reformed
military system the N.S.W. aristocracy may be
less easily able to quarter its connections on what
(so it is announced) is henceforth to be termed the
“Royal” Australian Artillery. The Common.
THE BE A TEN TEA CK.
NEROUS CONTRIBUTOR: “ I
want to leave these stories for
the editor's consideration. What is
the usual procedure?”
THE OFFICE BOY (briskly): “Oh, you just leaves
’em, an' then calls round in a week 'r two 'n'
takes ’em away again.”
wealth will presumably require something like the
Royal British Artillery standard in its commis-
sioned officers. The aspirant is there required to
pass a preliminary exam, as follows :—lst, mathe-
matics, including differential and integral ealeu-
lus; 2nd, three languages; 3rd, some of the
experimental sciences ; 4th, history and geography,
and some optional subjects. When he has passed,
he must spend three years in Woolwich Academy
at his own expense or that of his friends (unless
ne happens to be the son of an officer killed in
action, in which case he gets through free). He
is then subject to a discipline stricter than any-
thing known to a soldier in barracks, and learns
all the practical and theoretical parts of his work.
When he has pissed all his exams., and becomes
a lieutenant, he gets 6s. lOd. a day and quarters
for seven years. After that he get* a shilling
more, and, if he is good, becomes a captain after
12 or 13 years’ service. The Royal N.S.W. lieu-
tenant passes no exams, to speak of, except as to
this “birth and parentage,” and gets £240 a year
(from the jump, with a chance of becoming a cap-
itain in seven years, and a colonel in 15 !
* • • »
N.S.W., in addition to (partially) franking
the letters of the M’s.L.A., franks all the letters
of Government House. This was a concession
made to Gov. Duff by Premier Reid soon after
attaining office, and it has been continued till the
present. Parliament was not consulted upon the
matter ; in fact, members knew nothing about it
for over a year. No estimate can be formed of the
expense as it is included in a lump sum supposed
to represent the stamps supplied to the public
departments.

Melb. P.O. officials howled against neces-
sary work on the Sabbath, making “ conscien-
tious ” objections and pleading religion. Govt,
granted an extra half-day’s pay for Sunday work,
and a great and abiding peace fell upon the
department. After all, the breaking of the Sab-
bath is very often merely a question of damages. —
“ You broke the Sabbath ! ” Pet'-r cried,
“ Excuse yourself or you shall rue it.”
“You know,” the trembling soul replied,
“Dear saint, that I was paid to do it.”

Jersey Lily Langtry (aged 47) has entered
Into matrimony with Mr. De Bathe, heir to a
baronetcy, who was born in 1871. —
The people of Perth (W.A.) have lately
discovered, or think they have, that their narrow
Hay-street was originally laid out as a
beautiful wide thoroughfare, something
like Melbourne’s Collins-street. But the
old proprietors didn’t see the use of so
much street going to waste, so they
quietly put their buildings out to what
should have been the outside edge of the
pavement, and the Sandgroper Govt, of
the day didn’t think it mattered. Con-
sequently Hay-street was reduced to a
lane with a row of vested interests on
each side, and it will probably remain
a lane indefinitely.
D. : The Bulletin is busily engaged re-
pairing and improving the Australian lan-
guage. Well, why not Mondnight, Tuesnight,
Wednesnight, Thursnight, &c., &c., for thefuture?
It would be a literary short-out, and prevent con-
fusion in spots and places.
* * * i
Writes the Argus :
The millennium of the beer-drinker has arrived in Mel-
bourne. He can get a pint-and-a-half bottle filled to the
cork for 3d. in any hotel, and in the jug even more liberal
measure is given. In 1898 there was an increase in the
consumption of ordinary beer of 700,000 gallons.
This has been reprinted in Sydney with a view of
showing how bad things are commercially in Mel-
bourne ; but the paper which reprints it publishes
in the same issue an advertisement showing that
at a very decent Sydney hotel you can get a three-
course dinner with a glass of beer or wine for
sixpence.
* * * *
S.A. Govt, experimented with women
does, as medical officers in residence at Adelaide
Hospital; but after a twelve-months’ trial gives
it best and sends to England (why still to Eng-
land, by the way?) for three more male juniors.
And yet the female members of the Hospital
Board are not only among the hardest grafters,
but also do some of the best work. The reason
alleged for the change is that nurses do not get
along so well with women-doctors as with men-
doctors.
* * * *
Dr. Ramsay Smith, the brains of Adelaide
Hospital Staff during the recent dispute, has been
made President of the Central Board of Health,
and it is now prophesied that there will be a speedy
return to old conditions at the first-named institu-
tion. Patients have persisted in getting better
under the new regime much to the disgust of the
striking doctors, but probably this will be altered
when the University signs a treaty of peace, and
young medical students are again sent into the
wards. During the fight the Adelaide Medical
School has been moribund.

The climax of Victorian illegal-Sunday-
trading absurdity came with a meeting of publi-
cans at Brunswick recently, to “ consider the
advisability of refusing to trade on Sunday.”
* * * *
The experiment of employing female lock-
up keepers at N.S.W. police-stations is only in its
infancy, but so far shapes well. There are three
such women at Sydney stations and one at New-
castle. 4s. per day is the present rate of pay.
* * * *
At a certain town down South t'other day
the police raided a Chinese gambling-den. Among
the European devotees arrested was a well-
known Sunday School teacher. When brought
before the Bench the man of hymns and
prayers promptly pleaded guilty, where-
upon the Magistrate ejaculated : “ But
Mr. , I refuse to believe that you, a
Sunday School teacher, were found
playing fan-tan with a lot of Chinese.”
Hereupon the pride of the Sunday
School begged to withdraw his plea
of guilty, and assured the Bench
he was only teaching the Chows
English.
“Jack Shay”: Observations
in King-street, Sydney, reveal that
men are not the only free lunchers at
the pubs. Each drunkery has its quota
of “ dog ” bummers, who know when
11 o’clock strikes as well as the human
munchers. They play Lazarus to the
dossers’ Dives-snatching and nosing
after the scraps that fall on the
floor. Some barmen have what
they call “ permanent dog boarders,”
which they have given up trying to
“ shoo ” away.
» * * »
S.M. Herald’s art-critic ought to
carry a stable-boy under his wing whenever
he casts his trained vision on horse-pictures.
Mahony’s study, “ Indifferent Companions,”
at N.S. W. Art Society’s show, is referred to
as a wicked-looking bay about to bite its
companion. Now, the bay in question is
merely half-aroused—turning with a
whickering playfulness to the pony
in the next stall—a clever study in
_ horse-emotion-eyes back and listen-
ing, after the manner of a lover about
to nibble his girl’s diaphanous ear. The
calm indifference of the girl-pony in ques-
„ _ tion negatives the Herald’s aspersion of
vice in the picture—horses don’t rest on their
hind-quarters when they bite. Also, Australians
are entitled at least to intelligent horse-criticism.
The officers engaged in Sydney Bank-
ruptcy Court buildings should get another location
or be reassured by the Health authorities that the
sanitary arrangements are decent. Chief clerk
Carroll died the other day. Jones, another clerk,
died a couple of months ago. When Justice
Manning died animadversions on the Court build-
ings abounded. Now the Registrar and a messen-
ger. are ill. These facts excite apprehensions
which,_ if erroneous, should be promptly and
authoritatively dispelled.
A SPRING POEM.
Now comes the stealthy bull-dog anfc
Our picnic-legs to vex,
And things from chrysalises pant
To crawl down maidens’ necks.
The wattle lanes green with gold
And feels her sap grow warm,
Just like an Irish Rifle bold
In his new uniform.
And bridegroom Earth leaps, full of joy,
To clasp the Spring again,
Who hides herself, demure and coy,
In her chemise of rain.
A blissful married state
When the boy is twenty-eight,
And the bride is plump and charming,
Though no longer in her prime ;
Contentedly he smiles,
For there’s wisdom in her wiles,
And he dreams no dreams alarming
Of his Lily’s winter time.
When a dozen years have fled,
And he not so newly wed,
With the ardour of a iover
Will he gaze upon his wife?
Will the old girl fill the bill
When she’s creaking down the hill,
And his roving eyes discover
Younger interests in life ?

“ Shanghai ” : That paragraphist is wrong
in saying that Ohows put bad coins in the coffins
with their dead. Says my laundry man: “Put
urn bad money by-and-bye sometime come back
dead man and give plenty bad luck. No fear,
Oh inaman not fool.” And rally people who
know the Chin kies well say that a corpse is the
only thing a Chinaman has much real respect for.
* * * *
The traditional Chinaman who, when his
religious beliefs were magisterially investigated as
a preliminary to administering the oath, blandly
said, “ Kissee book ! blowee match ! killee loo-
stah ! allee same ! ” has a compatriot in Sydney
who at least equals him in happy-go-lucky broad-
mindedness. He has been “ welly good Clistian”’
for years and has evln taught in Sunday-school
and played at kiss-in-the-ring with “welly ni’ lil’
white gel,” but these facts did not perturb him at
all when he arose at a recent mixed gathering of
Chinamen and Europeans and recommended that
a Buddhist temple be built in Sydney with funds
provided by a self-imposed tax on opium and rice
to be collected by the Chinese merchants. The
speech covering this proposition was made in
Chinese. Then the speaker said in English, for
the benefit of the Europeans present, “ God bless
Quong Tart; God bless Gibbs Blight; God bless
Jim Philp ” (the Ingliseeman who runs the local
Chow paper).
* * » *
John Norton was last week sued for libel
by H. S. Prescott, a blind teacher whom he ac-
cused in Truth of divers disgusting offences upon
blind women committed to his charge. Plaintiff
did not go into the witness-box, or call evidence,
and eventually dropped the case and accepted a
non suit. Defendant’s counsel called evidence to
prove his allegations; and read among other
things a statement made by Prescott to a commis-
sion which enquired into his conduct. One of
Prescott’s admissions was that “ The girls had laid
their heads on his shoulder at times but they had
been warned to keep their distance in the hot
weather.” Also the frolicsome Prescott and his
ewe-lambs disported themselves much at “Sacks-
on-the-mill,” a truly arcadian game which appears
to have consisted of the piling-up of themselves
by the pupils upon their prostrate mentor, until
when crushed beneath too much accumulated
damsel, he would throw off the load and they’d
begin again. How charmingly idyllic !
* * * *
In the Prescott libel case, a blind witness
testified to certaih “criminal conversations ” —the
legal term for illicit love. She “ witnessed ” with
hands, not with eyes. Evidently, there is no
“blind side” to the optically-blind. The blind
are very sensitive—especially to the presence of
another lydy.

A recent Melbourne
divorce suit revealed the
fact that man and wife may
long live “happily” together,
although the gentleman is all
the time wearing horns of the
largest size. It was only re-
cently that the husband dis-
covered that his wife’s little
game had been going on almost
from the start of their married
life', and that the children he had
cherished as his own were really
the other fellow’s. But the
principal irony of the thing lay in the fact that
the unconscious cuckold had introduced to the
wife as a stranger, the very man who was already
too intimate with her. How that pair must have
felt as they bowed, and grinned, and were
“ pleased to make ” each other’s “ acquaintance ! ”
* * * *
The fresh batch of C.L. clergy landed in
Queensland from England recently accentuates
Bishop Webber’s determination, expressed two
years ago, that if Queensland did not provide a
University for the proper training of native
ecclesiastics he would fill every vacancy from
England.
* * * *
Last week a young lady appeared before
the. N.S.W. Chief Justice and asked for =£2,000
damages from a recreant lover who had married
his cousin after allowing her “to get her things
ready.” This outrage moved the jurymen to such a
degree of indignation that they arose and rent
their beards and gave judgment for the whole
<£2,000. During the trial it transpired that de-
fend nit, who had once owned some property, bad
become bankrupt, and the injured damsel could
only r. cover a dividend from the estate. So he
naturally didn’t defend the action. He won’t lose
anything ; it is merely a case of the other creditors
getting their dividend reduced.
A characteristic little thing has just hap-
pened in Perth. They have a Dental Board there
composed partly of all-round .sawbones and partly
of tooth-pullers ; and until a few months ago it
was a thorough-going Groper institution, which
frowned haughtily upon the interloper from the
East. But an aggressive person named Potts
struck it, applied for registration, and was
promptly fired out. Sailing into still greater
notoriety in consequence, he continued his fight
with the Board, and the Supreme Court ulti-
mately upheld his claim to registration. The
other day a new Board had to be elected ; but,
instead of accepting the new conditions and mak-
ing the best of them, the members of the old
Groper Board went into their surgeries and
tooth-rooms and sulked—not a single one of them
permitted himself to be nominated.

But there are exceptions. A Melbourne lady
who wanted to die at once the other day, skipped
quickly into a handy grocer’s-shop, seized the big
bacon-knife, and, spreading herself over the
counter, began to saw her throat. She had made
a decent-sized gash and was beginning to collapse
when the shopman noticed this unusual proceed-
ing, and proceeded to expostulate with her on the
vexatiousness of her conduct in thus spilling her-
self all over the fat bacon and the fresh store eggs,
and in mixing her life-blood with the prime
factory butter at 10|d. Just at this point a large
heavy constable happened along and rushed her off
to the hospital.
* * * *
Another instance of the superstition about
“waitingfor the police.” At North Melbourne,
recently, a man who had been dismissed from his
situation was discovered by his relatives in the act
of strangling himself with a strap attached to
his bedstead, but, instead of freeing his neck,
the foolish people ran off for the police, and
when a constable arrived the man was iust ex-
piring.
There is in Sydney a juryman who makes
no secret of the fact that he never consented to a
conviction, and he has been attending at Darling-
hurst for 30 years. There are jurymen in
Sydney, however, who never acquitted a
prisoner.

Hobart's most exciting institution, “Satur-
day night,” when the whole population turns
out and wanders up and down Liverpool-
street, is about to be abolished. An Early
Closing Bill, which has been approved by a public
meeting of the shopkeepers, transfers the last
business night of the week to Friday, and closes
all shops at one o’clock on Saturday. At present
the Hobart half-holiday is on Wednesday.
* * * *
Tas. Assembly has just passed a law
against incest. And, if report speaks fair, not
before it was badly wanted.
Melb. Fire Brigade is considering
the subject of old - age pensions for its
members. At present the young ana
vigorous firemen unfortunate enough to be
damaged in doing their duty, are provided
for by an accident-fund ; but the man who
grows old in protecting property and
saving lives has nothing before him now
but 12 months’ hard for vagrancy when
his day of usefulness is over. Strange, in-
deed, that the old fireman has never yet
been provided for ! He is without doubt
the one official in a community without
an enemy. Nobody ridicules him, he has
no nick-name, and by all sides he is
treated with respect; yet he is neglected
in his old age.

A piteous tale from “ One of the Crowd ” :
Dear Bulletin.— Recently an advt. appeared in a
Sydney paper: “ Wanted, a tnan to clean a house.
Apply 11 a.m.” I walked there at 9 a.m., and found a
dozen men before me. When the advertiser arrived at 11
o’clook, there were nearly 100 of us waiting (more than
one told me he had not tasted food that morning). The
advertiser was apparently so astounded at the crowd that
he took refuge in the empty house, and sent out word
that we should choose one man ourselves to get the job.
Some walked off in disgust; but 64 of us drew slips of
paper from a hat to see who would be the lucky one.
While we were balloting, however, a late-comer walked
in and secured the job for twelve shillings; and he and
his mate set to work, while the rest of us tramped back
to the city. This fact will tell you what is the condition
of unskilled laborers in Sjdney at present.
Are you listening, Jolly and Fat ?

A correspondent of a Sydney paper states
that—
Glasgow, Aberdeen, Edinburgh, the best sanitary kept
cities in Scotland, never dream of destroying a whole
carcase of valuable meat because about two inches of it
has a tubercular deposit. This same in London, Liver-
pool, and Manchester, Paris, Berlin, and the chief cities
of America.
Which, if true, goes to show that Australia is in
this matter very much ahead of old-world usage—
or very much behind it!

The scheme for consolidating the numerous
Sydney municipalities meets with much opposition
from paltry suburban aldermen who view the pro-
posed “ Greater Sydney Council” in thesame light
in which most of the anti-federalists in N.S.W.
Assembly regarded the proposed Federal Parlia-
ment. They know that in the larger sphere they
will have no place, and that their small voices, so
effective in their little Pedlingtons, will never be
heard or felt in a Council
which, it is hoped, will
attract a more intellec
tual class.

“It will be another Briggs affair,” said
Giblett, the Ultimo (Sydney) homicide, in
threatening his lady-love. In other words, as
pointed out at the time, the newspaper notoriety
given to Briggs’s crime nourished the criminal
germ in weak heads, including Giblett’s. And
the lurid accounts of Giblett’s “tragedy” will
pass on the infection.
THE GIRLS IN THE PARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She wears a gown,
Straight up and down,
That nowhere fits her bony form ;
She seeks the shade,
And seems afraid
To venture in the sunlight warm.
And, through her specs.,
The powers of X
Engross her till the sky grows gray ’
Though young she’s old,
And pale and cold,
The girl who’s in the Park by day.
With darkness fleet
A damsel neat
Comes out the hungry park to fill ;
She laughs and strays
O’er all the ways,
And, reckless, ventures further still.
Beside her boy
She’s thrilled with joy—
Her bosom heaves, her eyes flash bright,
The while his arms
Enfold her charms—
The girl who’s in the Park by night.
