A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 29, 1899.
My Dear MyEe,—-
With the wet last week everything got a little
mournful. For days there were no galoshes to be
had in Sydney. Inquiring at the shops you were
told, “There’s a shipful at the Quay but they
can’t be unloaded ; they might be damaged by the
wet. ” And one shopkeeper added the promise of
“anice pair, madam, as soon as it stops raining.”
Entertainments subsided. The Daffodil Show
postponed itself. Things that had to go on, like
the Fair and the Theatres, changed from places of
amusement to objects of sympathy. People began
to look for landmarks. “It began to rain the day
before Charlie was born. That’s four months
ago, ” and so on.
And the Fair is over now. The profi ts were nearly
£5OOO. Three thousand short of its anticipations.
The last davs were given up to raffles. You could
get everything from a grand piano to a tea-
pot in a raffle—or miss getting it. A sewing-
machine for sixpence. An eighty-guinea piano
for five shillings. Sydney is full of the people
who didn’t get these things. Plain, straight-
out buying was little indulged in. Every-
body bought by raffles, and as many as bought
were sold —or nearly. It wasn’t till the last day
that I decided finally on the ugliest thing in the
Exhibition. This was the curtain on the door of
The Bulletin’s Art Gallery. An artist chose it.
But he was evidently tired. It was the sort of
cretonne a bony female lies down on after a hard
day’s wash. The pattern is cruel enough to stick
into a cow, and the female gets up wearier than
over, after lying on those rearing lilies and angular
roses. But The Bulletin was not an active par-
ticipant in the Press Bazaar, so it ignores
and disowns that curtain. Saturday was the
record night of the Fair. Over twelve thousand
people came out, which points to the great success
the enterprise would have had in fine weather.
And on Saturday night Lord Quex made his
bow to Sydney. Let me say straight off that the
awful unspeakableness of the play didn’t decimate
local nerves as it did the English Bishop’s. The
D.C. breathed a big sigh and looked, “Is that
all! ” at each other. Certainly, Mrs. Brough
threw in a dangerous-looking bit of business —a
superfluous and stupidly vulgar action. It happens
in her manicure-rooms. She is going away on a
visit. Her assistants stand round. She puts on
her hat, takes her gloves and parasol, appears to
be ready to go, remembers something, puts up
first one foot, then the other, on a chair, raises
her skirts and pulls up her stockings. Why?
Unless to show that Sophie Fullgarney is
a vulgar person. Well, if Pinero cannot show
you that a woman is vulgar without requiring
her'to pull her stockings up in the face of the
house, his right hand must be losing its cunning.
The moral of the play turns on a chase after kisses.
Lord Quex, the bad old man, doesn’t kiss the
pretty manicurist. Captain Bastling, the bad
young man, does kiss the pretty manicurist. If
Lord Quex had kissed her, his fiancee would have
thrown him over. She throws up her lover,
Captain Bastling, because she finds him giving
Mrs. Brough one kiss. For neat little elemental
morals, commend us to a playwright who dips into
blue waters, raises risque situations, scatters
Plain Talk over every scene, and makes his name
out of the loudly accentuated dubiety of his play.
The frocks are lovely. From the programme I
learned that they came from our own David Jones.
Perhaps there’s a rather heavier to'uch about the
trimmings, a little too much braid, more strained
attempt at eccentricity than you will find in im-
ported frocks, but the local needle has done
remarkably well. On the whole, Emma Temple,
Duchess of Strood, gets the pick of the wardrobe.
There’s a clinging pale grey voile witli skin-tight
sleeves, and a clasping skirt that one has only time
to glance at. Voile is the material for our spring
frocks, evidently. The Duchess’ dinner dress is
another land-mark in yellow moire, with a little
guipure polonaise edged with dark fur coquettislily
drooping to one side both back and front. Miss
Grace Noble, who doesn’t come very close to her
portrait as a “Creamy English girl,” wears for
this dinner, in her own graceful way, a moonlight
satin and cunning little bodice of jewelled net
ending in coat-tails where the placket now is not.
But the much talked-of couleur-de-rose tea-gown is
not the most alluring rose-color robe one’s
imagination could picture. It is made in pink
brocade, with loose sides revealing green satin
lining and a draped front of pink chiffon tied over
with green ribbons. Every night the Duchess
cuts off one of those green ribbons and ties it out-
side her bedroom door as a signal for Quex to
enter, but even that doesn’t do away with them.
Now that paw-readings are so wildly indulged
in, Lovell’s “Valma”has extra interest. There
was cunning in his way of dressing himself, for a
fentleman palmist in a top-hat and frock-coat is
ound to resemble-a shop-walker on a fine Sunday
afternoon. Also, the manicure rooms are a moral
lessoii to everyone who bites his nails. They
ought to. give manicurists in Sydney a lift. The
girls in their long black frocks, all the scents and
soaps and manicure sets, and Mrs. Brough enun-
ciating professionally to Quex, “This is hacking,
not cutting. No gentleman should trust to him-
self where his nails are concerned, ” should bring
the- growing number of nail-nurses a rush of
business.
By the way, the biggest manicuring orders come
from bridal parties. The bride has her nails
clipped, painted and polished for an hour before
the ceremony, so that the finger that receives the
ring may strike the bridegroom and the clergyman
with its beauty. The bridesmaids also have their
fingers cleaned up carefully, so that the signatures
in the vestry may strike admiration into the hearts
of the best men.
Apropos of weddings. Miss Moss-Davies”
marriage with Mr. Robert Moss ran along ora
picturesque lines last Wednesday, with three tail,
slim bridesmaids in white flowered silk, flounced;
and inserted with fine white lace. A touch of
turquoise at the neck and waist, and huge sprays;
of pink roses held aloft by long white crooks,,
took off the monotony of the usual blank white:
Giving Away. The bride’s diamond necklace,
and long white satin train showed up well against,
the scarlet canopy wherein the wineglass is broken..
A reception went on at the Australia later,,
the lovely frocks were seen to advantage in a.
drawing-room setting. Mrs. Moss-Davies was ira
soft grey brocade with a little mauve toque. Mrs..
(Attorney-General) Isaacs, who is over from Mel-
bourne on a visit, was in a simple blouse-and-skirt;
costume, and her sister, Mrs. Harry Levy, wore a,
winter red, lightened with a white boa. Mrs.
Ernest Davies aired a fascinating confection of
pale tan voile, with a light tunic of guipure reach-
ing half-way to the knees, followed by a long suc-
cession of tiny tucks. The bodice had a smart,
little guipure sailor collar and square revers, from,
under which crept an artful moss-green tie. A
note of green at the waist belt and a white
chip hat with a high swathing of deep rose
silk and blades of grass set stiffly across the
front finished the costume. Mrs. “Dick” Teece
was also in tan, with steel trimmings and a flower
toque. A short white coat, worn with a black
braided skirt, deserves a word or two. For the
coat was of the latest design, scalloped all round
the waist-line, braided all over with white military,
and made doubly chic by a rolled collar of black,
silk braided in white. A white tulle toque with
black wings was the jacket’s lawful accom-
paniment. Miss George Michaelis wore a trained
frock of violet voile, with a yoke extending over
the tops of the sleeves —as all good yokes should
nowadays —of rucked chiffon. And after the
gowns in cunning came the immense wedding-
cake, transported from Maoriland. It was blessed
with a little door, that opened mysteriously, and
let the bride into the cake without the usual
desperate scrimmage with unyielding ice.
The Society of Artists started their “At
Homes ” with one given conjointly by Mrs.
Ashton, Mrs. Teece and the Mrs. Curnow. There
was music as well as tea for the big crush, Mr.
Beattie managing a nice little afternoon pro-
gramme. Between time 3 guests inspected the
Governor’s purchases, a young boy’s pencil-draw-
ings, a little green English Minns, and a small
picture of old Sydney by Watkins. Tastes differ
so much that it is no use taking notice of what
artists say whose pictures have not been Chosen.
The Art Society gives its popular Annual Con-
versazione on Wednesday, August 30th. Next
night the Society of Artists break into a ball at
Need’s Rooms. Other fixtures are The Coming
Woman Company in a play called “The Snow-
ball,” at the North Sydney School of Arts this
Thursday: Mrs. St. Hill’s lectures on palmistry
in the Equitable Building on Wednesday nights ;
and Miss Marie Narelle’s Concert in the Town
Hall, September 15th.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The new grand piano for Govt. House costs
the country £220. Cheaper, of course, than con-
tinued charge for hiring an instrument, which
charge also had to be disgorged by the national
pocket. Whenever Govt. House entertains any
section of the community, marquees, benches,
crockery, glass, &c., are supplied by the Publio
Works Dept, at the cost, not only of the guests,
but of outsiders who help to pay the piper for
vice-regal hospitality, without ever personally
participating.
“Gov. Brassey’s function to celebrate the
accomplishment of Federation seems to have been
the record quick and lively affair. All M.’sL.A.
were invited and turned up in carriages with due
solemnity to be ushered into one of the drawing-
rooms. Soon Uncle Tom (I am repeating the
story a statesman told to me) put his head in at
the door and muttered, ‘Um-um—glorious em-
piah—yah-yah gentlemen um-um—join me—
um—glass of wine, ’ leading the way to a distant
door. The first dozen that followed found them-
selves in a cold, bare corridor, at one side of which
stood a small four-legged table with about a dozen
glasses, three bottles of unlabelled fizz, one ditto of
whisky and one of soda-water. The leaders of the
bunch quickly closed on the glasses and sixty
legislators were still fighting their way out of the
drawing-room when the voice of the pro-consul
was heard: ‘ Gentlemee yah—yah—the Queen ! ’
The crowd at the back edged forward to grab a
drink, but—too late ! Gov. Brassey had proposed
‘Federation’ and s.secs, later he had grasped
Speaker Mason warmly by the hand and thanked
Parliament for its attendance. While 75 per cent,
of the Bourke-st. councillors were still glancing
wistfully at the table, the Baron gave the wink to
the waiter and the wine was whisked away.
People accustomed to attend his entertainments
call him Barren Brassey now.
“Young Gerald Macleay Browne, ‘ Boldre-
wood’s ’ younger son, whose engagement to
marry Gov. Jerry Smith’s daughter is announced,
is in business at Kalgoorlie—Kingston and Browne
is the firm. Like Everard, who married a Chirn-
side, Gerald (perhaps it was the name, pa’s name,
that captivated) is an assayer and mining man
generally ; and along on pa’s consent came an
appointment to the local management of Chaffer’s
mine. Sylvester, ‘ Boldrewood’s ’ big brother,
was one of the originals in the Broken Hill
Junction; and Everard, fresh from school—he
was one of Cosmo Newbery’s ‘boys’—was sent
up as assayer. That was the beginning of the
pile which mines have since brought into the
family.
“A melodramatio outcome of a recent well-
advertised law-case was witnessed by a small
audience at Elsternwick station, the other day,
when the wife of a clerk assaulted and battered a
heavy Melbourne magnate with her ’brella. The
angry matron had her husband lying in wait the
while to see that she got fair play at his ex-em-
ployer, but there was no call for him to come to
the lady’s assistance. The one-sided battle ended
in the victim appealing to a policeman to arrest
the assailant, but the myrmidon of the law told
him that his proper course —seeing as ’ow he knew
the lady’s name and address—would be to take out
a summons. After which she went her triumphant
way, threatening to repeat the same dose six
times a week until the victim fulfills his pro-
mise (whatever that may be) to her hubby.
“ A friend in London writes : ‘ Your Australian
beauty, Mrs. Maesmore Morris, has her photos all
about London, and is being boomed as a pro-
fessional beauty. She is engaged by Geo. Alex-
ander for St. James’ Theatre, where the leading
lady is now the beautiful Miss Fay Davis, beside
whom, I think, your Mrs. Maesmore Morris is
only pretty in a very weak, chocolate-cream-boxy
way. But she seems to have influential friends.’
“ At a recent wedding, celebrated at All Saints,
St. Kilda, between a keen yachtsman and a lady
whose name is a familiar word in every household,
an amusing contretemps occurred. _ The bride’s
gown was unusually handsome, but in walking up
the aisle something went wrong with the rear
works, and to the deep sympathy, but redoubled
interest of all present, her train hitched off.
“The one aspect of humor surrounding the
Melb. Hospital-doctors’election, t’other day, was
the arrival of Archbishop Carr. The kerb was
covered with the faithful when the Archbishop
stepped out of his carriage and as all respectfully
doffed their cadies, one of the candidates stepped
forward and, bending his knee within an inch of the
pavement, kissed the plump foot of Tom Carr
with a sounding smack.
“ Sophie Osmond’s benefit (Aug. 24th) overfilled
the big Theatre Royal. What with flowers,
thanks and a fat programme the crowd got its
money’s worth, and at the end of four tight-packed
hours trailed home in a chock-a-block com-
placency. There were all sorts and conditions of en-
tertainment, from ‘buck dancers’ to Miss Edith
Graham Berry, in quaint blue satin as ‘The
Vision of Youth.’ This young lady, by the way,
is only prevented by parental disapproval from
seeking her fortune on the boards. At same
matinee, Carrie Moore, in a little rub-a-rub skirt
and blouse, looked a work-a-day version of her
fas-and -glitter form. But her nigger lullaby, as
eretofore, was so convincing that infant hearers
in the gallery took it to themselves and gurgled
loud approval in the intervals of trying to swallow
lemonade-bottles.
“ After seriously observing a series of very cor-
rect and picture-frame figures of pretty girls in
the tableau at the Sophie Osmond Benefit, the
little, short-shirted boy who scratched his leg at
the critical moment when the flag was hoisted, set
the audience into roars of laughter. The subject
was ‘ His Only Pair ’ the mother (Miss Cora
Milne) sewing up a rent while the little fellow
stood waiting trouserless till the patch was
completed.
“ But the best joke of the benefit was the
frequent enquiry, ‘ Who is Sophie Osmond ? ’
heard in all parts of the house. A packed
audience, upstairs and downstairs, hundreds
of ss. ‘reserves,’ smartly-dressed women roost-
ing as high as the top gallery, and yet the
eternal question, ‘ Who is Sophie Osmond ? ’ Well,
Sophie is the buxom Salonite who worked up
the biggest ben. of the season ‘on her own.’ She
was the business-manager who scooped the pool.
Good old Sophie!
“Amy Castles’ ‘little sister Dollie, ’ another of
the matmee soloists, is, at present, a broad-built,
stout-ankled, baby-elephant type of girl, with
short, thick, tousled, auburn hair. Pa Castles,
’tis said, prophesies that vocally his younger
daughters will ‘wipe the floor’ with their senior.
The latter, however, with her refining halo of
dusky hair, holds more than her share of the
family’s available stock of looks. As a singer,
Castles 11. so far sports a boyish, unforced voice
and says her words distinctly.
“Coming Austral Salon shivoos are Miss
Osmond’s farewell tea to the Bland Holts, and
Amy Castles’ sing-song on Mrs. Saddler’s
afternoon. The Bendigo warbler volunteered
this attention as a mark of gratitude to the Salon,
her early and powerful ally.
“ Lady Bertha Wilbraham and co. kept them-
selves strictly to themselves during their recent
South Sea trip. And early in the trip they
despaired of the rough, rainy jaunt, and deserted
at Suva.
“It is suspected that the Melbourne Women’s
Franchisers carefully selected the feminine audi-
ence who packed the galleries for the debate on
their pet Bill in Vic. Assembly, Instead of
the awful crowds of hard-faced, spectacled hyaenas
in petticoats who have got in on former occasions,
there was this time quite an array of pretty
women. Consequently, the old argument of the
opponents of the Bill, ‘ look at the sort of women
who support this measure,’ was conspicuous by
its absence. The manoeuvre was clever and well-
worked. There were one or two beauties
amongst the fair listeners, and it was a remarkably
well-dressed show. Meanwhile, the old bald-heads
in the Council have been worried daily by agita-
tors, mostly young and pretty, who have been care-
fully chosen for the work. Every member of
Council has been waited on very many times, so
cow-seller Campbell cannot this time truly say he
has never met a woman who wanted a vote.
“Poor old ex-Crown-Prosecutor Walsh (just
dead) got his death-blow over that brief-lived
Judgeship. In the ancient’s then state of health
it was sheer cruelty to put him in such a position,
setting him up like Aunt Sally, just to be knocked
down again. Besides, his maunderings on the
bench were made the most of. None of their
honors would climb down smiling, maybe, if pil-
loried on the housetops by means of verbatim re-
ports of every chance remark in court. Deceased
was a voluble Irishman with a high-pitched Mile-
sian voice. ‘The old beggar that bothered
Kitty,’sa/id Deeming, in reference to the cross-
examination of Kate Rounsevell. Legal-sprig
Jacobs, brother-in-law of Attorney-General Isaacs,
and clever mimic of oratorical oddities, has lost
the best of his bunch of models by the death of
Mr. Walsh.
“Miss (Mars) Buckley, Mrs. George Chirnside,
and a host of Melbourne’s Naicest leave for
Sydney this week to join in the Sept, horse festivi-
ties.
‘ | The extraordinary stories of identification
which crop up occasionally make one wonder how
many innocent men, dead and alive, lie inside the
faol walls. A couple of weeks ago, Mr. Richard
little, an old man of 76, and perhaps the best-
known Melb. tailor, disappeared from his home.
Nothing was heard of him for three days ; then
an acquaintance came forward and said he
had seen and spoken to him only 12 hours before.
The clue was followed, and the relatives of Mr.
Little at last brought up against the old man who
had really been met. He was a complete stranger
to all, but his resemblance to the missing man was
so great that they completely excused the mistake,
adding that had they not been relations they
would probably have erred similarly. What about
the Suffield and other cases of the sort in view of
such facts as these ?
And in new music there is the “ Earl Waltz,”
by Montague Younyer, jun. (Paling and Co.), as
melodious a composition as its namesake; “ Gal-
way Bay,” by Sydney Hoben (Glen and Co.),
dedicated to Eva Mylott, in which Mr. Hoben.
takes advantage of H. P. Graves’ charming verses
to throw off his most ambitious and successful song-
setting up to date; and “ Mrs. Lofty,” by Sophy
Deloitte (Glen and Co.), a strange, inconsequent
little ditty, which tells you “her husband has
white fingers, mine has not,” and yet does not
profess to be comic.
“DeChaneet’s song, ‘First Love, ’ seems about
the most serviceable of several new publications
received from Glen and Co. Although ultra-
sentimental, it is a fetching thing, with a pretty
refrain, and altogether better than ‘As a Bird
from its Nest,’ by L. L. Lewis. The Castles-boom
was responsible for this cheap, twaddly ballad, and
artless Miss Amy is the high-soprano fledgling
who flutters from the nest. There’s nothing the
matter with the words of ‘Galway Bay,’a basso
ditty of the rollioking order, but the chief theme
is tiresomely repeated ; and Gillott’s Australian
Federal song (suggestive of ‘Death of Nelson’)
would, I think, be decidedly preferable to Mar-
shall Hall’s effort—if it were not so much longer.
‘ Narcissus,’a tuneful little piece for the piano-
forte, does duty as ‘ water - musio ’ at Dante’s
show, and does it very well.
“ The Marquis of Lome, the only Royal con-
nection who so far has accepted colonial office,
and who may possibly be the first Governor-Gen-
eral of Australia, is a dreary ‘Adonis of fifty,’
who writes awful poetry, and is at present
M.P. for South Manchester. The only other
Royalty apparently eligible is the Prince of
Wales’s son-in-law, the Duke of Fife. But some
people who are likely to know a little about the
matter say that undoubtedly Lord Hopetoun will
get the Governor-Generalship, if he cares to ac-
cept it.
Y ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULL.

out ? Every sensible person will answer “to
know.” Read on ; then you will know that there
has been another great invention discovered in the
manufacture of kerosene vapour stoves, which en-
tirely eclipses the Primus. Messrs. M'Lean Bros,
and Rigg, Ltd., of Melbourne and Sydney, have
just landed, and are exhibiting daily, the “ Jewel”
vapour kerosene stoves. This stove is constructed
on entirely new lines. The burner is connected
direct from the reservoir. It Kas no tubes to
choke up, and can be cleaned out in a moment by
the most inexperienced. With each stove are
supplied a duplicate set of washers and screw-
wrench, thus meeting the practical l’equirements
of those residing in the back-blocks and country
districts. For particulars write direct to M'Lean
Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., Melbourne or Sydney, the
home of up-to-date hardware novelties. Agents

PERSONAL ITEMS.
How would “ Cardinal Samoran” sound ?
Victoria is getting quite mature. J. M.
Rjed, its newly-appointed Surveyor-General, is
native-born ; he comes from Creswick.
Had Dan. O’Connor been caught young,
he might have made a rhetorician worth taking
seriously. He calls the recent copious rains
“ baptismal showers falling on the giant head of
Federation.”
When, at Dunedin, someone tried to in-
terview the late Prior Vaughan (the Archbishop’s
brother) concerning the relative merits of Protes-
tant and R.C. missions, he said: “Oh, no ; I
buried the vinegar-bottle years ago.”
Ex-Judge Foster, the only N.S.W. ex-
judge now on the Supreme Court pension list—he
has not been publicly heard of for years—awoke
to a spurt of activity last week in connection with
the formation of a new club at Newtown.
President Chaffey Baker, of S.A. Leg,
Council, is so anxious to be made President of
the Federal Senate that he is even trying to heal
his old breach with Kingston, so as, if possible, to
sweeten that influential opponent on the subject.
Died at Thames (M.L.), the other day,
Peter Anderson, a member of the first party to
peg-out a quartz claim in M.L. in 1856. Jerome
Cadman, father of Hon. A. J. Cadtnan, however,
was the holder of the first miner’s right issued in
Maoriland.
Has Louis Becke “found salvation”?
Once upon a time he apparently never lost a
chance of speaking contemptuously of South Sea
missionaries—nobody knew the tribe better. Yet
now it is cabled that he is championing them and
urging that Cardinal Moran be sued for libel.
Prof. Marshall Hall never misses a chance
of “getting one in” at the critics. The musical
scribe for one of the big Melb. dailies in an evil
moment confessed to a feeling of weariness during
Herr Scharf’s rendering of Brahms’ “21 varia-
tions on a theme of Handel,” (through no fault
of Scharf’s). At the final rehearsal for the Vic.
Hospital concert, chorus and orchestra were about
to worry Brahms’ “Song of Destiny.” Marshall
Hall, with that Mephistophelian grin of his—
“ Oh, yes, by the way, I see by the Age-gus
this morning that its critic got very tired during
this composer’s 21 variations yesterday. This is a
pity (sympathetically) as it might injure Brahms’
reputation.”
IN “PUSH” SOCIETY.—THE STRUGGLE
FOR SUPREMACY.
THE BLOKE: “So you’ve thrown me over for
Plunker Smith. Wot was yer blanky reason
for this? ”
THE DONAH: “ That's my bizness; but if yer
leant to know he’s got more ‘previous convic-
tions’ than you ’ave.”
THE BLOKE: “ Oh, that’s why, is it? ” (Sadly.)
“Well yer needn’t ’a been in sich a blanky
’urry; I’m sure I could ’a caught up to him !”
Apropos the first issue of the Sydney
Herald' (18th April, 1831), lately on exhibition at
the Press Bazaar in Sydney Exhibition Building.
Upon the day the bazaar opened there was to be
seen on Sydney Block the perennial George
Thornton (a native of the city and born in 1820)
who was Mayor of Sydney in 1857. Mr. Thornton,
then 11 years of age,'held a lighted tallow candle,
to assist the printer in the production of the first
issue of what is now the Sydney Morning
Herald.
Premier (and Attorney-General) Kingston
goes a step further and appoints a solicitor, J. H.
Sinclair, to harrow the affairs of people using the
Insolvency Court. Odd times will be filled in
Crown-prosecuting. So far S.A. has rubbed along
with one C.P., with occasional help from Glynn
and others in the country. The new man was
Kingston’s fellow-clerk; but while Sinclair took
to “local-preaching” and Christian-endeavoring,
the other stuck to politics and football-barrack-
ing. It would be hard to say which is the richer
—or the wiser. It is characteristic of S.A.’s
frugal mind, anyhow, that the new man’s screw is
only .£5OO a year.
Mortimer Menpes, writing recently, re-
ferred to the “ inartistic atmosphere ” he found
in his youthful days in Adelaide. Mortimer,
perhaps, was thinking of the unhappy day
when he was caught, as a mere boy, in an Adelaide
warehouse caricaturing the boss, and promptly
received the sack.

Art Society’s show, and being almost due at a
dog-fight, or something, he said to President Col-
lingridge : “ Sorry we have so little time —I wish
you would show Lady Oarington the gems.
“Your Excellency,” replied the Frenchman,
“they are all gems.”
Some Australian birthdays for Sept. :

‘‘ It appears to be thought necessary by
N.S.W. Govt, to purchase all the busts and pic-
tures of the late Henry Parkes, which are to
be had as ‘bargains.’ ” The irony of Fate ! Poor
old-man Parkes, the champion art-bargain hunter,
who as a buyer of expensive shoddy was without
a peer, is being commemorated by a gathering-up
of rubbish.
There are still living two nonagenarian
native-born brothers of the Mrs. Carey who
recently died, aged 99, at Collector, N.S.W.
Which suggests the notion that each person js
wound-up, like a clock, to “ go ” for a certain
maximum time, which cannot be exceeded;
though a “ stop ” may be easily caused at any
shorter period.
It is proposed that Tas. Assembly be re-
duced, in view of Federation, from 38 to 32 mem-
bers, also that the electorates be re-arranged by a
commission, composed of Chief-Justice Dodds,
Govt. Statist It. M. Johnston, and little barrister
Waterhouse, of Launceston. Two of the trio are
self-made men. Johnston (by far the ablest of
the three for the work to be done) was a railway-
man in Scotland, and, later on, at Spencer-street
Station, Melbourne. He is one of the ablest of
Australia’s all-round scientists, and refused a big
N.S.W. Govt, offer the other day. Dodds began
life in Hobart in a smockfrock-shop in Liverpool-
street. Waterhouse is son of a Wesleyan parson.
He tried to practise in Melbourne 10 years ago,
but didn’t succeed, and returned to Tasmania.
Then he was made Recorder of Launceston, but
threw up the post to take a good private partner-
ship in a law-firm.
Editor Windsor, of the Age, is
one of the least-known men in Vic-
toria. Until the other day, when
David Syme held a reception of pro-
minent people to see his new offices,
there were scores of M.’sP., city coun-
cillors, leading lawyers, and merchants,
and public men of all sorts, who had
never to their knowledge ever seen
Windsor ; and many of them didn’t
know there was such a person, think-
ing that Syme edited the Age him-
self. Yet Windsor said in his speech
that Syme lets him have a free hand in
the editorial policy of the paper,
scarcely ever interfering. Windsor is
short, slight, with a thin, refined,
gentle face, very keen eyes, white hair'
and beard; he has a pretty gift of
satire, and a fine sense of humor. He
also wields a brilliant pen. Indeed,
Alfred Deakin has been heard to say
that in his younger leader-writing days
he used to think there could be nothing
more flattering than to have one of his leaders
pointed out as “one of Windsor’s bits of fine
writing.”
Vic. Dancing-Master-General Duffy, when
on a business trip recently, struck a miserable
one-horse township somewhere back of Bendigo,
aud put up at the local neck-oil establishment.
After dinner Bung approached flourishing a bulky,
dirty document covered with signatures,
and besought the P.M.G. to add his
to the list. Glancing over it, Duffy was
amused to find that it was a petition ad-
dressed to himself, praying that he would
grant increased postal facilities to the
district. “Just shove your name down here,”
quoth Bung persuasively. “But I’m not a resi-
dent.” “That don’t matter a button, everyone
that comes along signs it. In fac.k, all the signa-
tyerrs but about 20 is travellers!” That com-
munity is still waiting for the post-office.
Perhaps the most ancient of S. Australia’s
political enthusiasts is a woman —Mrs. Jane
Brock, aged 86. Old, and a woman, yet she is a
radical; and she says she has never been anything
else. She has a vote for each House of Parlia-
ment, and exercises it every time. Also, she was
a staunch friend of late Labor-leader, J. A.
M'Pherson, and was one of the promoters of the
Labor-paper.
At the burial in London of the recently-
deceased Queensland “ Billy ” Smythe, M.P.,
amongst the wreaths upon the coffin was one
legended “ Queensland gratefully remembers her
public men” a large way of putting it,
which empties the tribute of all personal
value, and makes The Bulletin wonder which of
her malodorous “ public men ” Queensland thinks
of burying next.

Janies Heber Smith, who died in St. Vin-
cent’s Hospital, Sydney, last week, was grandson
of Bishop Heber, of “ Greenland’s Icy Moun-
tains ” fame. Cox was a law-clerk in Sydney for
many years, and, for a time, associate to the late
Judge Hargraves, but latterly devoted himself to
the commercial side of newspaper work. It may
be mentioned that Bishop Heber was also associ-
ated, in a sense, with Australia, as he was Bishop
of Calcutta when Australia formed a branch of
the Calcutta C.E. diocese.
J. H. Sinclair, just appointed S.A. assistant
Crown Solicitor, is president of the local Christian
Endeavourers, so that a new zest will be added to
tote-shop prosecutions. Sinclair was, 20 years
ago, a wild Bohemian ; he played football for
“ Sowth ” Adelaide with Kingston, and they were
clerks together in the office of Way (now C. J.) and
Symon. Nearly all the dare-devil companions of
Kingston’s irresponsible youth are now either
dead or rabidly pious, and, with the coming of
grey hairs, the S.A. Premier is, people aver,
rapidly becoming converted himself.
SOCIAL NOTE.
Prince Ranji gives a chance to the Australian XI.
A. J. Rose-Soley, formerly of S.M.
Herald, and husband of clever “ Rose de
Boheme,” is now doing dramatic criticisms for
’Frisco Argonaut. Soley, who might at any
time have been mistaken for Robert Louis
Stevenson, left Sydney to follow the dead English
writer in exploiting the islands for “copy,” but
his one book does not seem to have made any
stir to speak of.
Gilbert Probyn Smith, the Norton pam-
phleteer, assisted in a thrilling “situation ’’before
he was 12 months old. His father, Major Smith,
R.E., took part in the Indian Mutiny campaign,
and on the eve of the Cawnpore massacre Mr.
Smith’s ayah got the tip about the impending
slaughter, and concealed mother and child in a
dry well, where they remained for 11 weeks, the
ayah surreptitiously supplying food. At last the
faithful creature came across the Major in Cawn-
pore, and gave him the joyful news that his wife
and child were safe. Mrs. Smith remained blind
for six months, and the child’s eyes were long
affected. It was in afterwards accompanying his
father about India that young Smith acquired
his command of 12 dialects of six Oriental
languages.
The Dreyfus embroglio revives a story told
at the expense of Alex. Kethel, M.L.C., of
Sydney. During a visit to Europe he had seen
Paris in the throes Panama excitement,
and on his return was full of the scandal, the
bribery of the press, and the implication of the
leading French statesmen. He took his wealth of
information to a Sydney journalist, who, unknown
to Mr. Kethel, had also been" in Paris. The scribe
cut him short with : “ You can’t tell me anything
about it that I don’t know, Mr. Kethel.” Kethel
stared blankly and said: “ But I .have special
secret information about the awfu’ bribery of the
press!” to which the pressman .replied, with an
inscrutable smile, “Yes, and I know all about
that too ! ” and in a dramatic whisper added, “ I
had some of the money myself.”

running for a seat in the Federal Senate.
Ex-Police-Supt. Sadlier, recently appointed
a member of the Queensland Police Commission
of Inquiry, was a schoolfellow,
at En.nis, Ireland, of British
Commander-in-Chief Wolseley
and of “Redan Massey.” And
while Wolseley was fighting at
Tel-el-Kebir, Sadlier was be-
sieging the Kelly gang at Glen-
rowan. Amongst Sadlier’s
fellow passengers to Victoria in
the Great Britain (1852) were
“ Reggie ” Bright and George
Robertson (the publisher). Upon
arrival, Sadlier (then 19) joined
the police-cadets, of whom Vic.
Chief-Commissioner Chomley is
the last remaining in the force.
Sadlier, who was removed from
Ballarat just before the Eureka
trouble, always held that Attor-
ney-General Stawell was princi-
pally responsible for the outbreak
because he couldn’t realise that he
wasn’t in Ireland. When Sadlier
was sub-inspector at Beech worth
his superintendent was Burke, the
explorer, and when Burke was set-
ting out on his transcontinental
journey, Sadlier volunteered to go
with him, but Burke told him he
had better stay with his young wife
and children. The ex-super, tells
many stories about the cadet-corps
of the early 50’s. One day in later
years a seedy individual with
an adventurous eye dropped
into Sadlier’s office in Mel-
bourne. It was an old cadet
comrade of whom S. had lost
sight for half a lifetime. “Look
here, Sadlier,’’ says this damaged
swell, “ I owe you <£s for the last
30 years, and I’ve come to pay
you.’’ “The devil you are!” says
Sadlier. “ I don’t remember it, but
if you say you owe me the coin, I’ll
take it, for times are hard just now.”
Then the visitor bent down and whis-
pered in Sadlier’s ear, “Lend us a bob,
old man ! ’’ The next time Sadlier saw this genius
he was a man-servant at St. Kilda, sweeping the
front step one morning.
Bishop Byrne, of Bathurst, is said to be
the man in Card. Moran’s eye as successor to Dr.
Higgins, his former assistant in Sydney. It is
unlikely, however, that Dr. Byrne, who is dear
to the hearts of his Bathurst flock, will be induced
to make the change. Another dignitary in the
running is handsome Monsignor O’Brien, although
it is thought the jovial “ Dane ” Slattery will be
the man for the vacancy if Dr. O’Brien is content
to wait for something more comfortable.
Colonel Roberts, the first Australian
officer appointed A.D.C. to the Queen, was
A.D.C. to N.S.W. Gov. Young in ’6B. He was
also secretary to Young, and was again secretary
in London to Charles Cowper when that poli-
tician was Agent-General there. Roberts saw
genuine fighting under Lord Raglan in the
Crimea, being several times wounded. He re-
ceived commendations and medals for pluck and
daring. Colonel Roberts is an Englishman, and
lately entered his 63rd year.
John Tait (of Allan and Co.) tells an in-
teresting little yarn about “ Ben Bolt.” Meeting
Regina Nagel in Paris last year, he got her to
sing the song into a graphophone, and on his
return to Melbourne he went around to the house
of Mrs. Nagel, at Carlton, bearing news of her
daughter and—a graphophone. The effect of
Regina’s “ Ben Bolt ” upon her blind mother and
the assembled family was something to see and
remember. They couldn’t have enough of the
old song. When Tait was about to pack up the
machine and go, Mrs. Nagel asked him to leave it
with her till next day, and the family sat up half
the night listening to the sound of the far-away
contralto voice as it issued from the funnel.
Edison’s invention has given the world a wealth
of new romance.

Out-Back, as far as my observation goes, is simply

mean—not the big-footed splodger who stands at
.”, F ® j c
street-corners, and wonders what he 11 do for a
“case.” The trooper is mostly chock-full of in-

and the squatter, by taking notice of him occasion-
ally (at times he won’t know him from a rabbit)
gets just vvliat he wants, cheap. Riding once
with an English squattah (I was “ tutah ”) we met
“mounted man”—a decent fellow—who pulled up
for a “ yarn,” and offered us a cigar each. Jum-
buck, J.P., was indignant, and afterwards re-
marked, “ You know we have to tolerate these
fellahs—but we draw the line. If we didn’t,
they’d want to be riding out with our wives and
daughtahs, doncherknow.” Don’t think Robert,
as a rule, is open to tip, though I have heard one

the force who would just as soon scruff the
smiatter ns bis o-rnmn nnd who won’t be

tolerated. Why should they ?

‘ ‘ Alba ” : A drunken circus camped in
our paddock last night. Was ignorant of the fact
until I stumbled on to a warm, heaving mass that
was chained to a post. I apologised vigorously,
but the warm mass grumbled from its innards,
implying that it wasn’t taking any, and hit me a
oushion-like punch on the back. Struck a match
and faced a No. 1 sized brown bear rocking at the
end of its chain—tame, of course, but .... I
put in a lot of decent foot-work between the bear
and house. It appeared that everyone at home
had carefully walked into Bruin. Mother was
slightly hysterical. “ Never mind,” said father,
“ Bill ain’t home yet.” Nobody suggested hang-
ing out signals to warn Bill—we were all anxious
to see what effect Bill would have on the bear,
We listened and listened and we listened. Then
father listened. “ Hear that!” he said, suddenly.
The noise of a terrific struggle reached us, a weird,
three-cornered sound that was full of bear-growl
and our Bill. Father gob up and stated that
the bear was getting what for. “ And serve it
right, too,” said mother. Just then Bill appeared
through the back-window looking as though he’d
been fighting a man with nine arms and a gun.
“Fine night, Bill,” said father carelessly. “Yes,”
said Bill wiping his forehead; “splendid weather.”
Father lit his pipe. “Anything wrong, Bill? ’’
he asked steadily. No, said Bill. On y that
thear Brian Mullins is camped in our paddick—
drunk, as usual. Punched me in th back when I
passed him. Didn’t want ter scrap, so I kicked
him in th’ face. Gan t y hear him swearm to
hisself in Irish ? I hate fightin Mullins, he
crawls all over ye an stinks awful. Bill spat
into the fire reflectively. “Never mind, 111 go
down when he s asleep an’ pinch his overcoat.

“Curlew” (W.A.) : A
Clarence River darkey, up a
tree for honey, fell and was
badly smashed. The nigs,
gathered round, and after long
“yabber,” grabbed a young
fellow, who seemed to know
what was coming, and tried
to bolt. They gashed his breast
with a tomahawk and let the
blood drip on the injured darkey,
but he died, and the young black
was thereby disgraced : they reckoned
his blood had lost its healing power
when he flinched. This was in the
earlier seventies, and I have sought
parallel in Queensland and W.A. Nearest was
the Chinkie doctor at Mt. Morgan, Q., who bled
a fowl on his countryman’s broken leg. The
patient mortified and died.
A close-fisted commercial, staying at an
Orange (N.S.W.) pub., was questioning the land-
lord as to coach-fare to Lucknow. “Oh,” said
the publican, “ I’m going there in the morning ;
you can come with me, if you like; there’ll be
plenty room.” The bagman was profuse in his
thanks, but didn’t “fill-’em-up” either then or
while waiting for the buggy next morning. About
10 o’clock, seeing no sign of the horses, he got im-
patient. “All right,” at last said Bunghole,
stepping into the street and along it, “let’s get.”
“Yes, but—er—where’s the trap?” “Trap be
blithered!” answered the publican and sinner,
“ I’m going to walk. I told you there’d be plenty
room.”

“ Alia ” : Re water-cure for jib-
bing horses (B. 29/7/’99). A friend
of mine had a horse that used to
squat on his hind-quarters when left
tied up for any length of time, to
the great damage of harness and
shafts. Took him at last to the bank of a
waterhole, backed him close to the edge, and
tied him up to a convenient tree, the halter being
cut through except one strand ; and when, after a
time, the moke sat down, the rope parted, and he
did a 6ft. back-drop into the water. When he had
swum out on the other side he wore a pained ex-
pression of countenance—but he never sat down
any more.
Overheard from a N.S.W. coun-
try policeman : “You see old Groggins
reported me to the Inspector, and put
me to a lot of trouble, so I thought
I’d get even with him. The old fellow
used to visit a pub. every evening, and
generally reached a bridge on his way
home about midnight. Well, I waited
one dark night for him, and just as he
staggered on to the bridge I gave him a
regular woodener on the jaw and
stretched him. Then I gave him a
couple of kicks in the ribs, and left.
About four o’clock in the morning, I
crossed the bridge with another trap,
and we picked Groggins up, and took
him home. My mate wanted to lock
him up, but I wouldn’t have it. It
isn’t my dart to show spite. The old
fellow would do anything for me now.”

Paddy B , a fairly-lucky
N.Q. digger and hard-case in his time,
was asked by Warden St. George, of
Cooktown, to come and see him.
Paddy got drunk same evening and
was haled before the Warden. Next
morning latter said he was surprised
to see Paddy there. “ Surprished, is
it?’’said Paddy, “ shure didn’t ye ask
me to come and see yez ? ” He got off
with a caution.

“ Ben Sun ” : Close to spine
In cod and blackfish is found a worm
about thickness of sewing - cotton.
Larger proportionately in blackfish than in
ood. Don’t know of the worm’s existence in
any other Australian fish. There’s a bush
belief that only boiling fat will kill it, and that it
is very injurious to the human stomach. Anyone
enlighten ?
* V * *
“ Yar.” : Young bush school teachers of
both sexes need to be tough to bear the strain on
their constitutions. I knew one young fellow who
had to sleep in the dairy on a plank, 20in. wide,
fastened to the wall, and had to hitch his shirt-tail
to a nail in a slab to keep himself from falling
overboard. On other side of dairy on a corres-
ponding plank were the dishes of milk and cream.
In one corner was the “ harness ” cask, and in
another a heap of decaying onions and a hide or
two. He had also to sit down to eat with about
18 kids —mostly twins and triplets—and when he
turned his head to talk to the old man the eat-
ables would be promptly grabbed from his plate, so
that he got the name of being an incurable glutton.
Result was that when, after a row with the land-
lady about letting a sock fall into a dish of cream,
he left, he couldn’t find anybody to board him at
any price. Last time 1 saw him he was cawned
in the school-house living on tinned-dog.

One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney —who own the Willson patents for
Australasia —are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. This co. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
WATSON’S WHISKY.
“Bandicoot”: Some
of your correspondents seem
to infer, from a few glaring
exceptions, that immorality
is the rule in the bush.
Have been 15 years in it
and am well acquainted
with its denizens, from half-
clad scrub-fighters to the
affluent cookies on the
fringes of towns. Families
feed out of one dish, are
put to bed in sections on
washing-day ; are reared in
log-huts without partitions;
yet I can honestly credit
the bush with higher aver-
age morality than cities.
But lapses, if few, are all
the more glaring, and so are
made much of.
* * *
The N.S.W. detectives
who went hunting for the
notorious Clarkes (and were
all shot dead) took the rdte
of surveyors, and attracted
instant attention by moon-
ing around Clarkes' place
with a theodolite minus a
plumb-bob, which none of
them could adjust; also by
running lines across sur-
veyed roads and through
private property. The day
before they were murdered
a strange man rode up to
the bogus boss surveyor,
and, pointing to a mountain
peak near by, asked what
the bearing to it was. A
surveyor would have told
him in half a minute ; the
unfortunate detective didn’t
even know what he meant.
Next day, slaughter !
* * *
“R.A.”: He deafness
of white cats. I know of
a pure white cat (eyes grey) which oan’t hear a
gun fired-off alongside.
* » * *
Even now the battle with the bush is not
a walk-over for man. At Kiewa, on the Murray,
a father and son were making breakfast in the
bush. The boy was standing before the fire, and
the father was close by. Suddenly a tree crashed
down, and a branch struck the boy down on the
fire and pinned him there. The father was so
injured that he could not release his son, and
had to crawl away for assistance. By the time
help arrived—well, what the rescue party saw
when it arrived is better left unsaid. Both father
and son are now in Albury Hospital.
* » » *
lie big pigs (B. 12/8/’99). Pig recently
killed at Armidale. (N.S. W.) scaled 6151 b., dressed
—the head alone weighing 471 b. ! It was an-
nounced that this carcase would be given to who-
ever should guess its exact Weight; and, as four
persons struck the figure within 51b., they each
received a quarter. The two best guesses (one a
woman’s) were both 6201 b. In Maitland (N.S. W.)
was killed lately a pig weighing (dressed) 6401 b.
An Ulladulla (N.S.W.) man killed a pig which,
headless, weighed 6361 b. ; and a Melb. butcher
recently exhibited a porker scaling 7151 b. !
* * * *
“ P.T.F.” : Have heard it at last—the
Music of the Solitudes ! Was crossing, recently,
a bit of scrub-land in the dim light sent through
the rifts in.the low-flying scud by the lustreless
Miss LILI SHARP, the Bendigo Soprano.
Photo., T. Hunrphery, Melbourne.
Btars. Suddenly it came, soaring above the
rustling of the bushes and the moan of the turgid
creek near by. Booming like the surf on the
rocks ; vibrating every leaf till every leaf repeated
the melody with sweeter purity; swelling and
climaxing in a thrill of sound excelling the most
exquisite music ever drawn from a violin-string.
Miss ALICE CRAWFORD, a new “ Recitress” (from Bendigo).
Photo., Johnstone O' Shannessy & Go., Melbourne.
Humming, throbbing, lingering, dying—resonant
with a wail of human misery; appealing with a
note of the harmony one hears in dreams. Fas-
cinated, I stepped ecstatically around a clump of
obstructing bushes to get a better impression, and
—was struck across the breast by something tense
and vibrating. I had run into a tightly-stretched
steel-wire fence which was doing the aeolian-harp
business with the fitful west wind.

A white dingo was recently killed at Hurra-
jong (Windsor, N.S.W.). First of that color seen
in the district:
“ You may be a little tin-god, but you’re
damn badly soldered.”— Yankee election remark.
Grimwade, M.L.C. (Vic.), is bringing for-
ward his Bill to regulate crematoriums “as the
best method of disposing of the dead ” does not
seek to despoil the Leg. Council of any of its
functions. Dead politicians will continue to be
disposed of in the old way.
The Melbourne newspaper efforts to boom
Miss Lili Sharp as “the Bendigo soprano ” would
be funny enough if they were not unfair to other
and better singers who have to get along without
the aid of special paragraphs. Miss Sharp is just
a fairly clever artist, possessing a clear, pleasant,
unremarkable soprano voice of uneven quality.
Her top notes are not nearly as fine as Amy
Castles’s, but she has more taste and training than
the damsel in the more abbreviated frock.
The unexpected does happen
in a pleasing sort of way now and
then. At Prahran (Melbourne) a
ruffianly citizen was rebuking his
wife with a broomstick and a flat-
iron, when her screams attracted
the attention of two passing stran-
gers. They loafed into the premises
in a tired sort of way, hauled the
husband off, kicked him about 46
times, cut his face badly, broke one
of his ribs, and went away. The
gradual policeman arrived after they
had gone, and locked the justly-
damaged householder up. After
four days confinement the bench let
him off on his wife's entreaty, and
he departed saying he wanted to
find the two strangers who had
left their footmarks upon him—
and take a horrible vengeance.
But as they did their work well
and departed leaving no address,
and as Melbourne is a large place,
and even if found they may sim-
ply break another of defendant’s
ribs, these humble instruments of
Providence seem to have most of
the chances in their favor.
Henry Lucy writing from
London to S.M. Herald, tells a
story on the authority of General
Evelyn Wood (Mrs. Kitty O’Shea’s
brother) that casts a curious light on
Transvaal history. After Majuba
Hill, Wood succeeded the deceased
Colley in the South African cam-
paign. He now says he wanted the
Cape Ministry to cut the telegraph
cable so that he might have a free
hand in dealing with the Boers,
whom he could have smitten hip
and thigh—at least so he said at
the Authors’ dinner on July 2.
Curiously enough, the correspondent
says, the statement was only re-
ported, and then in a bowdlerised form, in one
London paper, and adds that Wood’s suggestion
to the Cape authorities was made “by way of
jest.” Not likely. The modern Major-General
doesn’t joke about such things. Had Wood been
a great soldier he would have found means to get
the cable out without “ suggesting ” it to anyone.

Mechanic (Bal.): It is the volatility of liquid
air that gives it commercial value by making possible its
use as a power-generator. It is 100 times more volatile
than steam. Your objection that it cannot be stored
also applies to steam, which has to be used immediately
it is made. Yet steam is extensively used in this and
several other countries. And liquid air can be preserved
for some time, which steam can’t be ; it is sent hundreds
of miles by rail in America Eucalyptus : The false
names don’t disguise it in the least —they would only
aggravate the libel-damages. Besides, it is no possible
use exposing a great swindle unless you are prepared.to
say straight out whom you are exposing. A thing like
that wants solid legal evidence behind it, else the paper
that publishes it merely gives the swindler a chance to
prove his spotless innocence in the law courts . D.K. :
“ Wouldn’t it have been as easy for the inventor of man

of the logical interpretation of concrete facts

The determination of the Boers “ to make Dutch the offi-
cial language of the Transvaal ” only follows British pre-
cedent, anyhow. When Britain annexed the Gape from
Holland an order was issued that proceedings in courts
and all official documents should be in English. The
Dutch speakers, who were seven-eighths of the population
at the time, remonstrated —but in vain. . A.J. : The
statement is probably rubbish. Save to a small extent in

K. : The old maxim that you can’t get more out of, a
machine than you put into it is nonsense. If you don t,
what is the good of machinery ? The use of machinery is
the use of a little force to set in action more potential
forces, which would otherwise remain latent. On a bike,
you turn your driving-wheel once with the same expendi-
ture of energy as you would have used for one stride;
the driving-wheel turns j our bike wheel four times and
carries you four times as far as you would have walked
by the same expenditure of energy .....Collins : If the

The Art of Letter Writing.
The business man of to-day recognises the value
of this art, as it naturally enters largely into his
daily life. The first essential is brevity; the
second, comprehensiveness; the third, absolute
accuracy. Perhaps our readers may be interested
in a sample letter (which we herewith attach) from
the ex-Mayor of Brisbane, B. H. Babbidge, Esq.,
which incorporates all the qualities necessary for
guidance, and indicates clearly how much thought
and fact can be conveyed in a few words.
“ I have been asked by some of my friends if I
have ever taken Warner’s Safe Cure. I replied :
* I have, and have found it a most excellent medi-
cine for general debility, and for toning up the
constitution, and I have always recommended it
for the same.’ ”*

POLITICAL
POINTS
S.A. Oppositionists, most of them poor
men and office-hungry, admit that the Kingston
Govt, will easily last out until Federation. Of
the present S.A. Govt. Kingston, Holder and
Jenkins will all be Federal candidates, and their
retirement from local politics will be certain to
disrupt the Ministry.
Premier Turner reckons that, after dis-
qualifying'as many of the old people as possible,
his Old-Age Pensions will cost J 1150,000 a year.
He provides £92, 000 a year by extra taxation and
“trusts to Providence ” for the rest. And that
weak, shiftless, half-hearted trusting to Provi-
dence is just where the trouble comes in.
“ To vote for Federation,” moans a Bris-
bane paper, “ is to make a humiliating confession
of our inability to manage our own affairs.” Put
it the other way : “To vote against Federation is
to make a humiliating confession of our inability
to help at managing the affairs of Australia, and
to acknowledge that we are only fit to potter away
in a corner by ourselves.”
One argument that really weighed a
good deal with Vic.' Leg. Council when it at last
consented to pass the One-man-one-vote Bill was
that long resistance to the measure had made
it the most—and most-deservedly—hated Legis-
lative body in Australia. “We are un-
popular,” said member after member, and sorrow-
ful “hear-hears” dittoed the wail.
Premier Turner, of Victoria, is carrying
the religious-instiuction-in-schools joke to the
bitter end. He has circularised the heads of all
the Beets in Victoria asking them to meet and
come to some agreement about what religious in-
struction they want, and then he'' will proceed
further. If they do meet they are much more
likely to come to a hospital than an agreement.
Having got the One Man One Vote Bill
through, the Victorian Government is making a
great effort to establish One Woman One Vote
also, so that the province can start its Federal
existence on a thoroughly democratic basis.
Despite the most enthusiastic opposition by that
pelican-in-the-wilderness, Murray Smith, the
measure was carried in the Assembly by a vote of
nearly two to one. Then the.ladies in the galleries
applauded enthusiastically, and the enraged
Speaker informed them that they would- be forcibly
thrown our into Bourke-street if it happened
fegain.
Whispered by a well-informed
M.L. correspondent that should the Aus-
tralasian Federation movement, initiated
in Auckland, prove abortive, the northern
province will seek to clip the wings of
Wellington by agitating for the removal
of the seat of government from Cook
Straits to a site on the railway line be-
tween Christchurch and Dunedin, the
cost of removal being provided for by the
retention by the State of the freehold
of the new metropolis. In the early
eighties Bathgate, an Otago member,
tabled as an abstract motion a very simi-
lar proposal, and it was only beaten by
one or two votes. The Aucklanders
complain that railways to connect the
northern city with her natural territory
are voted down consistently by the Wel-
lingtonians, who have, however, suc-
ceeded in tapping country topographically
belonging to Auckland.
Henry Chaplin, a subordinate mem-
ber of the British Cabinet who is probably
a straw thrown up to see how the wind
1 lows, suggests a small protective duty
on grain, proceeds to go towards an Old
Age Pensions Fund. Britain has lately
knocked another small hole in the Free-
trade fetish by subsidising the West
Indian sugar-industry; and the Indian
Govt, has lately begun fooling with pro-
tective duties. Britain still professes to
grovel to the clay-footed idol of freetrade,
but it grovels half-heartedly nowadays.
And the time is coming when tenders
will be called from marine-store people
r nd dealers in scrap iron for the complete
removal of the Joss.
Barton did the cleverest act of his life
when he resigned the N.S.W. Opposition leader-
ship after Federation was. assured. To stay on
and scramble with little wobbler Reid for the
little fag-end of local authority that remains till
the larger Parliament assembles, to quarrel about
culverts and grants to cow-shows, to try to pre-
vent Meagher calling Norton a liar—it wouldhave
been a woeful falling away for the Federal leader.
So the big lion, Barton, says to the small lion,
Lyne : “You can run after that mouse by your-
self if you want him —I don’t.” And the small
lion promptly replies that any mouse is large
enough for him to run after, and scoots for all he
is worth.
Queensland electoral-rolls at present date
contain 107,265 names of persons qualified to vote
on September 2nd. Of these Charters Towers has
4,863 electors (two members); Fortitude Valley,
3,907 electors (two members); South Brisbane,
3815 electors (two members). And little country
constituencies with only five or six hundred voters
return a member to the Bananaland Assembly.
Within a month after Victoria’s acceptance
of the Federal Constitution it secured One Man
One Vote in local Assembly elections —which it
had vainly struggled after for seven years —partly
because the anomaly of having One Man One
Vote at Vic. Federal elections, and a plural pro-
perty vote at Vic. State elections, was too ridicu-
lous to last. Queensland will also find that the
shortest way towards a democratic State franchise
is through Federation.
The vote on Federation should convince
the various “ labor-parties ’’ that they can no
longer command the support of the masses by
simply yelling that they are the Labor-party.
The people want up-to-date democratic legislation,
and if politicians calling themselves Fatmen or
Cast-Iron Tories offer the genuine article, and the
Labor-party opposes it, the workers will support
them, and the Labor-party can go hang—as the
N.S.W. Labor-party is going hang now.
Westralian Premier Forrest admitted in
Parliament, with the cynical frankness cf a big,
dull, ox-like man, that his Truck Bill was devised,
not so much because of the wrong done to work-
men by compelling them to take out their wages
at the boss’s store in goods sold at the boss’s price,
but because the storekeepers in his own town of
Bunbury found their trade injured by the Truck
system !! Bunbury is the beginning and end of
the universe to Big Parochial John.
Melb. Age gleefully records that Aus-
tralia’s first Governor-General is to be a member
of the Royal Family, if one of H.M.’s relatives
can be spared—which is doubtful. Here’s an
argument in favor of the old Egyptian system of
dealing with dead royalties.—
Q. Worker, which says it “isn’t against
Federation,” cartoons the Federal G-ovt. as a fat
man driving a half-starved Labor donkey with a
bunch of carrots under its nose. The leading article
in same issue (Aug. 26) is an appeal to Queens-
landers to vote against the Commonwealth Bill;
and later on a poet (who appropriately signs him-
self “G. A. 8.”) writes about “ The Federal Bur-
den” in lines like this :
After that, the Worker’s attitude re Federation
is like Paddy’s when he rode the-pig:—quite im-
partial, till he fell off. And the Worker has
fallen off on the wrong side. -
IN SUNDAY-SCHOOL.
TEACHER: “What is the chief reason for going to
church?”
BRIGHT PUPIL : “To put threepence in the plate, miss!”
John Darling, jun., S.A., Tory M.P., on
Household Suffrage :
Even if the country was not totally against the Bill, the
referendum showed that they were apathetic on the
question.
At the referendum referred to the “country”
yelled for Household Suffrage by a majority of
over 15,000 votes. If Darling saw 15,000 people
yelling themselves hoarse over a cricket century
scored by his brother, Australian captain Joe, he
would probably describe that as “apathy.”
After all the bitter yell raised in N.S.W.
provincialist circles about the alleged continuance
of the Victorian bounties under Federation, and
considering the further yell now being raised in
Queensland about the same alleged continuance of
the Vic. bounties, it is curious to note that
Queensland itself is the most lavish bounty-offering
province in Australia. I-t has a butter-export
bounty, cheese-export bounty, bounty on the
export of chilled meat, a bounty on wool sold by
auction in Brisbane, and a huge bounty on locally-
grown cotton, and it has helped- along the local
sugar-iuiclustry with assistance to the extent of
.£500,000. Yet Brisbane Telegraph is hurt in its
inmost soul because tho Federation Bill does not
immediately extinguish all bounties, and therefore
(so it alleges) Victoria will undue advan-
tage’over the guileless Bananalander !
STRANGE ANIMAL IN THE NOSE.
“Say, Bill, why didn’t Clem ’lllplay more? ”
“ Oh, he’s bin laid up with a platypus in ’is nose!”
The £350 disbursed by N.S:W. Treasury
for expenses incurred in Europe in obtaining in-
formation for a report on old-age pensions, chari-
ties, and State insurance, is not grudged. But
when the Treasurer was asked in the House
whether J. C. Neild’s fanfaronade mission abroad
would cost N.S.W. anything, why did he not in-
timate this instead of announcing that the mission
was wholly a voluntary adventure on the part of
the Major, ahd would entail no expense to
N.S.W. ?
The cable
August 24.
The Transvaal during the past three years has imported
via Delagoa Bay, 149,000 rifles, 6400 revolvers, and 398 tons
of ammunition.
The bold statement looks formidable till you pull
it to pieces. If all the rifles were military arms
they would be useless without cartridges, and if
all the ammunition was made up of cartridges for
those rifles, the 398 tons would only give, say,
14,250,000 cartridges for 149,000 rifles, or 95 per
rifle—hardly enough for one day’s fighting.
In Tas. Assembly a motion for a select
committee to inquire into the charge of bribery
preferred by Cameron, M.H.A., against Captain
Miles, in his capacity as master warden of Strahan
Marine Board, was rejected by one vote. Con-
sidering the stinkful things said against the gal-
lant master mariner whom Braddon lately took
into his Ministry, it might have been expected
that he would stand up in his large sea-boots and
demand the most complete inspection possible.
Anyhow, to escape from inquiry by one vote was
a rather tight fit.
How N.S.W. anti-Federalists must now
kick themselves for so loudly applauding their
Leg. Council for insisting, last May, upon those
impossible amendments on the Enabling Bill!
The new members then appointed to ensure the
passage of that Bill voted solidly for the Federal
Address to the Queen; but for them, Want-
MacLaurin would have succeeded in defeating the
Address. Things are getting so mixed in the
Assembly that the Council, by bringing about a
dissolution, might have found occasion for another
fight against the Bill.
Says the anti-Federal Toowoomba Chron-
icle as a reason why Queensland shouldn’t
federate: —
~lf our cattle are taxed it means an enormous advance
in the price of meat in the other colonies, and will the
working classes there be prepared to pay perhaps up to
one shilling' per pound for their meat or to tolerate a
Federal Government who support such a thing ?
And Brisbane Telegraph says Queensland
shouldn’t federate because—
There will be a poll-tax on cattle.
In Other words, anti-Federal rag No. 2 says
Queensland should stand out because otherwise its
cattle will be taxed, and anti-Federal rag No. 1
says it should stand out beoause it doesn’t matter
whether its cattle are taxed or not.
Toowoomba (Q. Chroni-
cle, as some kind of a lop-
sided reason against Federa-
tion, tells its readers that
“ the Victorian Parliament,
in order to encourage the
discovery of coal within the
colony, not only put on a high-
protective duty on imported
coal, but offered a splendid
bonus for every ton of coal
produced from Victorian coal-
mines.” It would probably
surprise this ill-in-formed
paper a good deal to know
that Victoria has no coal-
duty, and offered no bonus
for coal produced. And as
Victoria reckons it can face
N.S.W. competition under
these circumstances, Queens-
land, where the industry is
much longer established, may
contrive to do likewise with-
out the absolute and horrible
ruin which the Chronicle
thinks it sees ahead.
The very, very old
wheeze about “Melbourne
money being sent up to be
scattered broadcast in the
Federal interest,” which did
so much duty in N.S.W., has
now started in Queensland.
As usual, the identity of the
alleged sender doesn’t ap-
pear. The money is said to
come from “ a leading club ”
—name not given, either be-
cause there is no club or to
avoid hurting the club’s feel-
ings. Victoria, it seems, is
a poor, dead-broke place
whose only hope of salvation
is to join with rich and pros-
perous Queensland; and, at
the same time, it is a glori-
ously rich place where they
pour out gold with shovels to
corrupt the poor but virtuous
Bananalander.
Johnny Wallace, the old
gentleman with the terrible
walking-stick, had some more
to say against One-man-one-
vote in Vic. Leg. Council.
“ The measure,” pleaded
Johnny, “is brought in sim-
ply because it is a popular cry
at the present time, but the
legislator who suits his views
to the cries of the time is not
the one who inspires confidence and lasts
longest.” Which, coming from a man whose
sons lately expressed such a profound want
of confidence in his management of their
estate, was most impressive. Vet Johnny was
consistent even then. The popular cries of the
time on that occasion were the cries of the young
Wallaces for their money, and old Wallace didn’t
suit his views thereto till he could hang on. to the
cash no longer.
What really troubles Australian labor
parties in re Federation is the N.S.W. Con-
vention plebiscite which carefully left Labor
candidates among the rejecteds. That’s what
sticks in their crop. Yet, if they only had the
brains, they would see that massed Australian
labor, organised as it is, will probably exercise a
greater power under Federation than it can
possibly do when split up into sections and con-
quered in detail, as at present. But it will
certainly find greater men to lead it; and that
doesn’t please the ruling bosses. “D n the
people ! where will we be? ”
Attorney-General Pennefather, of Wes-
tralia, raving against Federation in the Sand-
groper Parliament, drew a horrible inference
from the U.S. civil war and the “ hundreds of
millions” of lives that were lost therein. The
East had no particular use for a man of such ex-
tensive ignorance, but Pennefather seems to be
good enough for Westralia. Vosper, Jones,
Tommy Walker, and Pennefather are four obscure
political scraps from the East who have risen to be
anti-Federal gods out West. Westralia imports
even its anti-Federal gods—like the African who
gets his brass deity from Birmingham.
If they’ve not got a living prince,
They need not worry. Truth to tell,
There’s no occasion for it since .
A mummied one will do as well.
Kneel for your Federal burden,
Receive from vulture bands
Fresh taxes for your sruerdon,
New gyves to bind your hands.
Renounce vour irlorious birthright,
Your children bind as slaves,
Enjoy your “ mess of pottage,”
Then seek dishonored graves.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Joe Darling, chief financier of the Australian
team, writes dolefully that the trip isn't going to pan
out anywhere near the £lOOO per man originally antici-
pated. “ More like £500,” says Joe, and the reason of it
is that the team doesn’t share in gates from the stands.
County clubs everywhere erected temporary stands and
collared hundreds of pounds.
• • • •
Home papers are bound to raise the question
whether M’Laren, with his policy of quick-change bowl-
ing, and his early nervousness at the wicket, is a good
captain for an All-England eleven. The first time Mac.
came to Australia he proved himself a cocktail batsman,
so much so that Stoddart pegged him back after a while
to fifth place on the batting list. At the outset he was
sent in first with Albert Ward to break the bowling, but
he funked visibly and rarely stayed there long enough to
get set. On his second visit M ‘Laren took charge of the
team in four (if not all five) of the test matches —Stoddart
standing out. And here, as in England this year, captain
Mac. was much too fond of remarking that the Aus-
tralians were the better men on a hard wicket. He
hasn’t a big enough heart for the position. Also on paper
he looks to be a poor manager of the bowling. Take the
figures for the fifth test. Lockwood, who had bowled
splendidly in the first innings, was not given a fair show
to recover his lost form in the second innings. He got no
wickets, hut only 33 runs were knocked off him, and
Bradley’s figures were 0 wickets for 32, whilst the second-
rate howler, Jackson, came out with 0 for 54, and A. O.
Jones with 0 for 43. The mismanagement in Rhodes’ case
was palpable. The Yorkshireman was chosen solely for
his bowling, yet because he had failed in the first innings
he was not given a reasonable show in the second. The
Australians scored 254 for 5 wickets (one of them a “ run-
out") and Rhodes’ average was 3 wickets for 27, two
clean bowled and the other o. and b.
* * • •
“A stale crust is better than no bread,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent; “ but a stale
cricketer isn’t worth a muffin, for he does—muff. This
sage deduction being the result of Surrey’s well-earned
conquest of the ‘ Darlings,’ and Ranji. and Co.’s big total
for Sussex in this present week (July 28). Four ‘tests’
and a hotly-contested tour up to date have tried some of
the cracks very hardly, and it would not come as a great
surprise to some peripatetic Australs (new name for
the Federated Antipodes) if another defeat or two turned
up before the tour is toured. M.C.C. Monday next
have practically an All-England XI ready—excepting
O. B. Fry, whom they never would or will accept as a
member. ‘ I can’t tell why,’ unless it be because
people will call him Charles Bounder Frj% though he is
the best run-getter here in ‘ top-price ’ cricket. At
the Oval on 14th prox., the woady Britons intend, by
‘ hooking ’ and * crooking,’ to get a ‘ little of their own
back.’ Iredale was so weak at the Oval that he had to
leave the ground, and couldn’t bat in the 2nd innings,
just when a man was wanted to stay with Trumper, who
got his 67 like a little hero, and well indeed has
he justified his last-moment’s selection. Clem Hill,
a pillar and buttress of batting strength, took the
field (after his illness) for the first time at Sussex
and under the strain of seeing 389 knocked off Jones,
Trumble, Noble, M‘Leod, YVorrall, Sid Gregory, and
Laver, retired in the afternoon, and the other sick man,
F.A.1., came out. Frank thought if he were well enough
to field he was well enough to be in the team, but the
selection committee rested (?) him. Clem looks thin, and
has undoubtedly had a bad time. The team missed him
muchly, for he is a universal favorite. Of the others, at
present there is a lack, in the field especially, of that
sparkle that dotted their work with frequent ‘ super-
human efforts.’ Their coats lack lustre, and the Titan-
action has disappeared in staleness. Certainly other
teams have had nearly as hard a programme,
but no other team has done so marvellously well
by sheer determination, grit, and hard labor. The
chances missed in the field of late have crippled
the team terribly, and although the catches, etc.,
have not been anything more than ‘possibles,’ yet up till
now these ‘ possibles’have been taken, and victory has
followed. Fry’s 181 at Brighton should have been 3,
o Worrall, b Noble, and nearly every ‘ Sussexor’ had his
‘life’ early. Killick 106, shouldn’t have had a tenth, and
there were five others. C. McLeod bowled splendidly,
getting Ranji at 5 with an off ball that came in 18 in. as
the batsmen tried only to‘knee if with his bat in the
air. Joe Darling has now lost the toss four times in suc-
cession, thus entailing a certain day or so in the field
under a blazing sun, and in the atmosphere of the ener-
vating English summer. At a general meeting the boys
decided to go straight back to Australia to peg out build-
ing allotments at Federation City. Perhaps a match or
two at Colombo, but no S. Africa or America for them.”
* * * *
Re young Collins’ biggest score on record (628),
curious to note that the first of the “gallant four hun-
dred ” was E. F. S. Tylecote, who made 404 in a House
match at Clifton College, the same school that the pre-
sent record-holder hails from. Tylecote’s score was made
on several consecutive half holidays, and Master Collins
took four afternoons to knock up “ his little lot.” The
cricket value of this mammoth score is nil, but as a
curiosity ’bis interesting in this age of records.
» * * *
N.S.W. Rugby premiership for ’99 was practi-
cally decided on Saturday, when Wallaroo beat the
hitherto invincible Randwicks by II points to nil. The
visit of the English team lost Randwick the game, for
whilst, during the suspension of club-football, the Rand-
wicks loafed and enjoyed themselves, the persevering
Wallaroos trained hard.
