Blundering parson L. M. Isitt has been putting
his foot in it as vigorously as ever. His last attempted
esclandre was the denunciation of the Wellington foot-
ball team for alleged drunkenness on the way down to
Otago to play. Considering that M.L. footballers have
reduced training to a fine art, this was a bit too steep-
even for Isitt 1
* * * *
The Newminster - Zuleika chestnut gelding,
Kobold, carrying 8.10 won Vic. Club Handicap at Caul-
field on Saturday by five lengths like a race-horse in
1.47. Previous Saturday he got away with the Show
Handioap (6fur.) at Flemington, having 8.3 up; time,
1.15 J. Young Foulsham rode him splendidly on both
occasions. Prior to the two victories mentioned, Kobold
(6 years old) had not won a race since l/l/’96, when he
showed Newhaven and Bright Eyes the way home in the
Normanby Stakes. Seems to writer that this nag is
worth considering for Caulfield Cup ; there is no reason
why he shouldn’t stay, and the weight (7.8) is not too
much. His brothers, Rebel and Arzim, were fairly good.
Quite on the cards, though, that Kobold, not being over
sound, may fail to see his preparation through.
* • * »
The late Caulfield and Adelaide G.N. races
beautifully illustrate the sweet reasonableness of Ikey
Mo, and the profits of the bookmaking indusi ry as com-
pared with the tote. For the six races at Caulfield there
were 64 starters against Adelaide’s 83 ; prize-money,
£2425, at Caulfield (including £l6OO for the big race),
against £1525 at Adelaide. Starting prices from Ikey—
2£, 8,3 J, 3,2, and 2£ to 1. From the tote—lBi, 4, 2£, 4,
16i and 3£ to 1. A S.A. punter, starting with £1 and
playing up his winnings on each race (supposing he
backed all six winners), would land £132,693 against the
Victorian’s paltry £5952. No wonder Ikey drinks fizz
and wears diamonds.
* • • •
“ Buckless ” : “Tattersall” will not lose any
sleep if the “ Special Sweep ” experiment is unsuccess-
ful. Writer held a ticket in “Special ’’ on Spring Handi-
cap. Sent in for one in “ Ordinary ” sweep on same race.
Received one in the G.N. Hurdles, with the usual red
slip : “ The sweep you applied for being closed, we have
taken the liberty of forwarding you a ticket, in next sweep
to be drawn.” As this was five days before the race, and
I did not want an interest in the Hurdles, I returned the
ticket, and requested that one in the “ Special ” on Spring
Handicap, in which I already had an interest, might be
sent me. By return post the Hurdle ticket came hack,
together with a letter, stating that the sweep I applied in
was closed, and “consequently we followed our usual
course, viz.: placed you in the one next drawn.” There-
fore, if the sweep one applies in is closed, applicant has
no right to state what sweep he would prefer as an alter-
native, but must accept whatever King “ Tattersall ” likes
to send along.
*' * * *
Adelaide Racing Club has carried a proposal re-
commending the committee to consider the wisdom of
paying two-thirds of the tote-div. to the first horse and
the other third to the second horse. The matter has been
pushed to the front through the wrong horse of two from
the same stable winning a recent biggish local event.
But it is feared that the Act will not allow it; and, while
the anti-tote party remains so strong, it is whispered that
there is wisdom in letting sleeping dogs lie.
• • * *
S.A. Jockey Club, now 11 years old, has paid
away £76,000 in stakes and £21,500 for course and
improvements ; and it now owes £5,500 besides on the
latter account. Without the totalisator its president, R.
C. Baker, declares this would have been utterly impos-
sible. It has improved the attendances, it has improved
the racing, and it has improved the conduct of the crowd ;
so that “ there is no place in the world where racing is
carried on more respectably and where it is freer from
what some people call * the evils of the course.’ ” Baker
is President of S.A. Leg. Council and Ex-Chairman of
Committees of the Federal Convention.
* * * *
Fitzroy (Melb.) pony races management (unreg.)
which lately re-opened that course, has struck an idea
that ought to do something towards persuading the
disingenuous mannikin with the strong arms to give his
moke whatever show it may possess, regardless of
orders. In a £5O race are offered prizes of £7, £2, and
£1 to the jockeys of the first three horses ; and,
seeing that a winning jock.’s fee never amounts to much,
and that most of the time it is a precious shaky debt,
this plan will not bring joy to the “ cronk” owner.
• * • *
“Cuscus”: The true inwardness of the A.J.C.
committee-bookmaker dispute, arising from that tribu-
nal’s refusal to allow layers who kept a shop in town to
bet in the sacred enclosure at Randwick, has not so far
been touched on. The A.J.O. committee may pose as a
mentor for public morals —smile here —but the milk in
the cocoanut is differently accounted for. Many owners
who are pals of committee-men bottle-up their mokes
for a big coup, and the inevitable stable hangers-on get
to know, and snavel all the doubles with the good thing
at the shop, and make it a favorite. Then the forestalled
owner uses vigorous language when the stable-money has
to go on at about half the odds it would otherwise have
secured. If the beastly shops were done away with
owners could go to Randwick with a lamblike expression,
secure in the knowledge that the riff-raff had not upset
their little pot, and that they had a mortgage on the
cream of the market.
* * • *
The moans of the “ books ” when Kelso’s bay
nag Dandy snared the Welter at Randwick on Saturday
were piteous. After first favorites getting home in four
out of the first five races, backers were out for a “ punt ”
on the last, and they accordingly let loose on Dandy,
with such effect that he started with odds on in a field of
14. They didn’t make much mistake, either, for he was
lengths past the judge when “ the field” arrived. Dandy
is by Martini-Henry out of Vain Glory, so is a near rela-
tive of “ Sam” Fielder’s favorite Vanitas, now champion
in India.
* * * •
Orlando, an average-looking chestnut gelding
by the Condobolin sire The Broker, had no trouble in
taking Rosehill Hutdle Race on Saturday. Whispered
about as a “dead bird” he started first choice, jumped
hard-held with the leaders all the way, and cantered
gaily in with his head in the air lengths clear of every-
thing.
* * * •
S. A.’s chances of winning V.R.C. Derby hasn’t
looked so bright for years. Gungadin, a colt greatly
fancied by Rupert Clarke, who offered a big sum for him,
has already swept the pool in Adelaide. The weight of
local money waiting to be put on him will probably make
him a leading favorite for the big Melb. event.
• * » •
The other day, with countless chances to one
against the happening, M‘Master, the Sydney sport,
drew his own mare, Cremona, in a Tatt.’s sweep on Syd-
ney Tattersall’s Club Spring Handicap ; hut the blind
goddess, having thus established a record, decreed that
the fortunate Mac should remain in blissful ignor-
ance of his good luck till after the race. So M‘Master,
the horse-owner, waited with muoh yearning for
M'Master, the ticket-drawer, to come to him and lay him
“ a bit out of the sweep.” But as he didn’t come to him-
self and use golden arguments with himself to induce
himself to start his own horse in the interests of his own
ticket, he finally came to regard himself as a close-fisted
person who wouldn’t come up to scratch and do the need-
ful, and laboring under that hallucination he drew the
scratching pen through Cremona's name. As the mare
just missed winning a fast-run Welter on the same day,
in ail equine probability she would, if started in the
Spring Handicap, have run into a place ; so M’Masterhas
now to bear the bitter reflection that he has done himself
out of from a thousand to a couple of thousand pounds.
* • * *
It would be hard to equal Vie. Mounted Rifle
Clulow’s bit of horsemanship at Sale, lately. Whilst
a horse harnessed to a buggy, in which was seared a little
boy, was bolting In the main street, Clulow raced up
alongside, and, whilst at full gallop, jumped out of the
saddle into the buggy, and pulled the horse up.
* * * *
Ex-jockey Jimmy Hayes seems to have gone
near to getting his precious iight put out for ever. Hayes
has so often been in trouble for assaulting somebody that
it comes as a pleasant surprise to find the nice young
man on the defensive. Jeremiah Heffernan was charged
at Caulfield (Melb.) court last week with the crime of
shooting at Jimmy Hayes and wounding a valuable race-
horse, but the lawyer engaged to prosecute on behalf of
the police felt bound to withdraw from the case, and
Jeremiah would have been discharged without the option
of a fine but for his misconduct in damaging the gee-gee.
On that minor count in the indictment the. shooter was
committed for trial. The horse had done him no harm,
whereas Jimmy Hayes had said things in public which no
right-thinking Jeremiah could be expected to patiently
endure. Therefore it is satisfactory to know that Jimmy
was more or less “stoushed ” by Jeremiah on two occa-
sions previous to the bombardment of his dwelling-house
by the same righteous foe. The stoushing may possibly
do Hayes a little good, and anyway Jeremiah had better
retire from the feud on his laurels lest he again vent his
fury upon an innocent quadruped instead of shooting
the other animal in moderation.
* * * *
Saturday last saw N.S.W. “cross-country”
championship a real cross-country race —for the first time
since ’92, so Metrop. Harriers (mostly harriers in name
only) found the task of running over rough roads, up
hills, and through sand-tracks and thick undergrowth
much too stiff. Very few of 60 starters finished the five
miles ; most dropped out en route, while others, barely
hanging on, helped to form the longest of long tails. R.
M. Gibson, winner in ’97, passed the post first, 40yds.
ahead of swimmer Harry Smith, thus annexing the indi-
vidual championship. Forrest Lodge Harriers won the
team-supremacy for the second year in succession ; War-
ringah (N. Sydney) second, one point behind. Re remark
that Metrop. Harriers are mostly harriers in name only.
Season is closing, and the championship is the first c.c.
event they’ve had ; of distance-races there have been
none, and paper-chases have been few and far between.
Why call themselves barriers ? Why not ordinary athletic
clubs, which they really are?
• A * *
Cable records another Australian victory “down
under.” Champion “Freddie” Lane, following up his
recent win of 220yds. championship, got away with that
of 440yds. last Saturday. Venue was Blackpool, Lanca-
shire, and course, the open sea, which accounts for slow-
ness of time (6m. 30 3-ssec.) as compared with world’s
best (5.43 1-5), and Lane's Australian top-time (5.54 1-5).
Last year England’s speediest amateur (Jarvis) appropri-
ated same event at Weymouth in 6.30, but conditions
were most favorable—perhaps they were not so on occa-
sion of Lane’s triumph. Description of race between
Jarvis and Lane for half-mile supremacy made the quar-
ter look a good thing for latter, as he led at that distance,
and had some weeks longer during which to prepare.
• • • *
The Cavills (Percy and “ Turns ”) will probably
be in Sydney for ensuing swimming season. They are
disgusted at their failure to arrange matches in England.
Percy challenged Tyers, July 22, whereupon latter
straightway got married and opened a pub., vowing that
he had done with the game. Champion of the world
“Joey” Nuttall has just quitted beer-pulling, and
doesn’t seem to take to water with the same zest as pre-
viously.
• * * *
“Old Timer”: Past-master L. Foley says, in
his opening lecture to every fresh pupil : “ Better stop a
blow with this than with this” —indicating right forearm
in first and nose in second instance. Writer saw a novice
fighter at Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney) on Saturday night
last who begs to differ from the great Australian maker of
top-notchers, for he (novice) fought six desperate rounds,
stopped every punch with his face, and didn’t mind the
gruelling a bit, though sent reeling dozens of times. A
gamer lad never stripped. In one round he swung his
right six times in succession on to his opponent’s kidneys
or ribs, and a deep-drawn “Ooh!” from hundreds of
throats accompanied each swing ; this in face of the fact
that the enemy kept jabbing the left home incessantly.
Name of pug referred to is Grono, and a better type of
the human bull-terrier has rarely been seen; built on
lathy lines, and sinewy from head to foot, he reminds one
more of a gamecock than aught else. The fighting all
through second round of G.G. Co.’s 8.7 novice competi-
tion was fierce and fast, and wrought ringsiders to
frenzy. One pair hung on to each other like catamounts,
furiously punching the while, and defied the referee’s
efforts to sunder them : ultimately all three (referee and
fighters) went sprawling in a heap. Writer has had. a
long experience, but never saw so much fury and fun in
a single night’s boxing.
* • * *
Crack ’rastler Harry Dunn and brother
“ Jimmy ” were in Sydney on Tuesday, en route to M.L.
The big fellow has had four months in Tas. mines, and
looks years younger and hard as nails. He’ll be back in
January to meet all comers at one or more, or the whole
seven, styles, and failing business means to visit Yankee-
dom and England again.
* * * ♦
“Wrestling” promises to boom in Brisbane.
Lorenz (middleweight-champion of N.S.W.) recently beat
Maximilian in Q. capital for £7O (purse and stake). Same
pair meet again shortly (£5O a-side and £5O purse); and
Lorenz has undertaken to make a match with Pagel after-
wards. Last-named is identical with “ Williams,” who
licked Harry Dunn at Sydney St Patrick’s Day sports
this year. Lorenz, Max and Pagel are pronounced
Teutons. A good Britisher like “Jack” Connots, ora
Yankee of the calibre of Dan. Stewart M‘Leod, who left
his mark in Melb. a few years ago, would go through the
lot on one mat, and think the effort nothing more than a
“ breather.”
* * * *
The mining boom, which has recommenced since
the Boer scare comparatively died down, has distinctly
benefited the Sj'dney Safe Deposit. Brokers and buyers
alike see the folly of keeping valuable scrip either in office
safes or private residences, and there have been large
numbers of visitors to view' the massive strength of the
Deposit vaults, with a proportionate increase in the
number of clients. We are requested to state that the
management freely invite a visit. The Deposit is situated
in Ash-street (back of Paling and Co’s), off George-street.*
At Sydney Press bazaar, Cardinal Samoran
won three raffles in a single evening. Now tell
us —was this the Devil’s luck?
At the trial of an Albury publican for
having music in his hotel at a social without a
police permit, the defence set up was that although
there was playing of instruments at that social
still it wasn’t music within the meaning of the
Act. The P.M. is taking time to consider the
point. A nice question for the Full Court.
A peculiar fact re the payments to General
Jawbone Neild is that he applied to N.S.W.
Treasury for his “expenses ” on January 16th of
this year, and was paid on the 18th ! Some un
fortnnates, contractors and others, are kept out of
their money for weeks and weeks, but, although
he emphatically asserts that he used no pressure
on the Premier, General Neild could secure hie
little whack in two days !
According to British small-arm experts,
the British military (Lee-Metford and,Lee-En-
field) rifles are not “ in the same street ” with the
Mauser (with which the Boers are armed) for
power, accuracy, and reliability of mechanism.
And when to this are added the considerations
that the Boer artillery is also superior to the
British, and that the British “ Mark IV.” ammu-
nition (from which so much was expected) is an
utter failure—the campaigning prospects of Bull
in Safrica are none too rosy after all. Britain’s
great advantage is the fact of the Boers being
utterly shut in and unable to get further war-
stores.
In reply to the charges made against him
in Tas. Assembly, Minister Miles made a capable
defence, but considerably weakened his case by
the stand-and-deli ver tone of his peroration. His
argument boiled down : “ If I said to the candi-
date for the billet (Morrisby, M.L.C.), ‘Oh, 1
don’t want the salary, you may have it,’ it was
not intended as a bribe for his vote, but was said
simply to show that I did not value the emoh
ments of office as much as the office itself. Mor
risby and I have been enemies since 1802. Was
likely to publicly give him a weapon to kill me
with ? If Morrisby took my words to mean at
offer of bribery, why did he not denounce me ti
the Board? Why did he vote for me as Chair
man ? ” His peroration, also boiled down : “Only
God and myself know what was in my mind when
the alleged offer to buy my opponent’s vote wa;-
made. God knows I have spoken the truth, and
I expect this House to believe me. If members di
believe me they will reject the motion for a specia
committee ; if they don’t believe me then .1 shah
have to resign my place in the Govt, and the
House. There is no other way.” Then the indig-
nant ex-sea-captain stalked dramatically out of
the Chamber declaring his intention not to re-enter
it until the motion was decided—which it was, by
one vote (16 to 15) in his favor. Bluff won that
time ! But the extreme narrowness of the major-
ity forces Premier Braddon to promise that the
Govt, itself will move for a special inquiry.

“From Brain to Keyboard.”
MISS WILKINSON,
A.R.A.M. (Rome),
NOW FORMING CLASSES
Tickets (which must be taken immediately ) and all
particulars at
GLEN’S, George-street, Sydney.
Miss Wilkinson, A.R.A.M., Rome, the organist of St.
Patrick’s Cathedral, Melbourne, announces in our adver-
tising columns that she will be in Sydney in the early
part of September for the express purpose of giving a
course of lessons in the Macdonald Smith system of piano-
forte technique, termed by its inventor, “ From Brain to
Keyboard.” The object is by a series of gymnastic exer-
cises for the fingers, wrists, and arms to do away with the
long hours of technical study which must be undertaken
by the modern pianist. Some years ago Mr. Macdonald
Smith, whose position in the world of music brought him
into dailv contact with students straining every nerve,
and literally working themselves to death to retain their
technique, set himself to the task of simplifying matters.
For years he devoted his spare time to the exhaustive
physiological study of all the muscles, sinews, and nerves
brought into play by pianists and organists. The result
of this was the invention of the practice of free gym-
nastics, which is now in extensive use. Many eminent
musicians have expressed great admiration for this exer-
cise, which has brought forth splendid results, not only
from the young and middle-aged, but from persons of
advanced age. The course of exercises attacks in turn
fingers, knuckles, forearm, wrist, &c., and all the various
exercises can be gone through in ten minutes, producing
a result not usually obtainable by hours of daily practice
at the piano. Sir Frederick Bridge, the organist of West-
minster Abbey, calls it “ the most valuable weapon in the
hands of the modern teacher,” and an eminent London
critic speaks of it as “ the most striking discovery of the
present century.” Full particulars regarding Miss Wil-
kinson’s forthcoming course may be obtained at Messrs.
Glen and Oo.’s music warehouse, George-street.
Town hall, Melbourne.

Peripatetic phrenologist to loving mother
of little girl standing at door: “Have the little
girl’s head read, mum ?” Little Girl: “ Carrots ?

What will Love not do? A German
leper, isolated in the hospital at Halle, escaped
lately with his nurse over the Dutch border, and
there they were married. The nurse had got
mashed on her leper and facilitated his escape,
and now she and her living death have got away
together to parts unknown.
“WVG.W.”: Have known severe beri-
beri amongst soldiers at Hongkong promptly
checked by the addition of pork to the dietary.
Medical authorities do not trace the disease
directly to lack of fatty foods, but consider that
said lack induces a bodily habit specially favor-
able to the b.-b. infection.
“D.J. ” : “ What’s in a name ? ” Lots !
Writer once required board and lodging, and, sick
unto death of the stereotyped ad. that “ young
man desired, &c., with a Christian family,” he
boldly advertised: “Pagan gentleman requires
board and lodging with a heathen family.” Coun-
ter-clerk looked at it sideways, and then referred
it to manager.. Manager, a humorist, said “Let
it go.” Called round for answers two days later.
Office full of them. Every Trilby-villa in the
city had replied, enclosing the choicest assortment
of photos, ever seen out of Sheol. On another
occasion wagered with companion that he was
not game to take the following personal advt. to a
Very Respectable big daily, and that if he was,
he wouldn’t succeed in getting it in: “Pagan,
male, wishes to correspond with brainy heathen
lady with view to making fortune.” Friend boldly
took it in, and set it down squarely on counter
with shilling. Clerk counted it, and was about to
grasp the shilling when he gasped and darted to
other side of office, and referred it to libidinous-
looking individual, who sniffed once or twice,
held it up to the light, thought hard, and then
whispered something. My mate was requested to
see manager ; manager not available ; see
clerk ; chief-clerk opined it was immoral. Friend
protested ; requested to wait and see manager.
Waited an hour. Saw manager, who read it care-
fully, put it down, wiped his specs., looked at
friend, who was wishing he was dead, and burst
out iri mingled grief and disgust that he was
.sorry to see such a promising-looking young man
taking to such horrible practices. He could not
hand him over to the police, but he would beg of
him to get out of the office as quickly as possible,
lest his presence should bring down upon the
whole holy show the wrath of an angry Deity.
My friend crept out.

London Daily Chronicle on Kipling :
The morbid novel is dead. Imperialism did the
really good service of killing it—lmperialism and
Rudyard Kipling. He came along with his
swinging step of the “ Road to Mandalay,” and
I the neurotic novel crawled through the first hedge.
It went out in a night like a bad dream.

10 Princes Street, Kew, 15th May, 1899.
Mr. S. A. Palmer, General Agent for “ Vitadatio.”
Dear Sir,—ln sending you this testimonial, I feel it will
no more represent my case than a St. Bernard pup
represents the size of his great parents ; still, small as it
is, it is the truth, and that’s the main thing.
You know I have been twice to England to try and get
ease from my sufferings, and was there treated by some
of the best m'edical skill in the world, but all to no pur-
pose. They told me at last that nothing could be done,
even to ease my pain (except to inject vnorphia), so I had
to go on suffering, with no hope of ever getting better.
No one but a man like yourself can realise the gloom and
misery, yea, agony, of such a state, but you, having been
in a similar plight, know how one in such a case longs for
death, and often feels inclined to do some desperate aot
to be rid of such an existence.
I had tried all different treatments. Allopathic,
Homoeopathic, Hydropathic and Electropathic. I have
also tried Russian, Electric and Turkish Baths, and
others too numerous to mention. One gentleman in
Sydney had me under the Crisis Treatment for six
months, and when I would not let him torture me any
longer, he declared that if I allowed my sore, raw body
to dry, I should soon be a dead man. I told him that
death would rescue me from suffering, so I would face it.
I parted, telling him that if Satan was hard driven to find
some special torture to punish his most hated victims, I
thought that he was the man that could teach him
(Satan) some wrinkles.
When returning from England three years ago, God
knows that I never expected to land in Australia. I had
then given up all doctors, and just meant to let myself
die. I thought, surely I have tried enough and spent
enough, so will try no more. Thus I was just suffering
on till death should release me. Everything I did,
whether eat, walk, talk, or sleep, was a burden to me.
Many who read this will remember how I then some-
times screamed with agony night and day. I went over
to Tasmania. Here I first heard of VITADATIO, and I
may tell you that (if it had not been for reading the de-
claration made by four gentlemen, who signed their
names, referring to the genuineness of your cure of
Hydatids, in Invercargill, New Zealand) all the people in
this world would not have induced me to again start
taking physic, as I had been taking medicines for 13
years, and was getting worse.
I could not get “ Vitadatio ” in Burnie (Tasmania), so
had to wait till I came back to Melbourne. I procured
my first bottle at the Mutual Stores, Flinders-street, Mel-
bourne. I took five large bottles. It certainly strength-
ened me, but it did not take away my severe pain, so,
like many others, I gave it up.
About four months afterwards, Mr. Button, of Oat-
lands (Tasmania), introduced me to you (Mr. Palmer), and
your remarkable case, being so much like my own, led me
to go in for “ Vitadatio,” if it should take my last penny.
1 drank, as you know, two bottles weekly, yea, one day I
drank a large-size bottle. I found it was food and every-
thing to me.
A well-known doctor, with whom I was friendly con-
gratulated me on my improved looks each time he saw
me, so I kept on taking “ Vitadatio,” and, as you re-
member, I came every other day to your Head Office ;
and so, by seeing you, got your encouragement, which
kept me taking the medicine. I endured the pain, and
drank the remedy, until the disease began to come away.
Soon it came away so fast that I have got bottles full of
the disease three or four times a week, which I brought
to you.
This, that had puzzled the doctors, when inside me,
now puzzled them as they saw it in the bottles ; one even
said that it was the “ lining of my bowels " coming away.
In fact, I understand one of the doctors sent a letter to
a leading morning paper to that efect. I should think he
would now withdraw it. I said, “I hope I shall soon be
free from all such * lining,’ as it is taking away the pain,”
and since the time 1 lost the “ lining of my bowels” (as
the doctors called it), I have put on a stone weight of
flesh, and, “ Hallelujah,” I can now sleep, eat, talk, and
run, and that with pleasure ; should the same doctor see
me now instead of wanting me to make my will, as he did
then, I think he would pass me for any life insurance
office.
I have been cured of the following :
1. Running sores in the legs, of over forty years’ stand-
ing. To show the thoroughness of the cure, a small dog
bit me on the very place five weeks ago, which caused me
some alarm, and two days of pain, but it is now
thoroughly sound.
2. Piles, that for years were a trouble to me, are now a
thing of the past—from them I am perfectly free.
3. Singing in my head, which was a constant nuisanc#
to me for seven (7) years, has completely left me.
4. Some small growths on the skin, which often
troubled me, have withered away, leaving only a stain on
the skin, which in every respect is as sound as can be. I
did not take the “ Vitadatio” for any of these complaints,
but only for the dreadful agony which I suffered, on
account of the growth in the pit of the stomach, and
which you now have in so many bottles, but I am glad to
say I can bring you no more, as it is ail gone, “ yes, all
gone.”
I intend to write a pamphlet on the different things the
doctors have called my disease, and which they could not
touch, but Vitadatio” has driven it from my poor old
body, which is daily getting stronger, as if going back to
y ° Uth ‘ THOMAS JOHNSON.
22nd May, 1899.

Neighbor Wolff and Others,
Almost every afternoon in pleasant weather I see
my neighbor Wolff sitting in an easy-chair in the
shade in front of his own door. Sometimes he
kills time by reading the paper, but oftener he
does nothing but watch the people and the vehicles
that pass. I frequently stop and talk with him a
few minutes, and he appears to be grateful for the
trifling attention. He is a man of about fifty,
strongly and sturdily built, and to look at him you
see no reason why he should not be up and about
as actively as the best of us. He is intelligent and
well-informed, has travelled considerably, and is a
man in every way worth knowing. His ideas on
finance and business seem to be clear and practi-
cal, and it strikes a stranger as odd he does not
grapple with his opportunities and pile up a big
fortune. Yet he never makes a motion in that
direction. He lets the chances all slip by, and
just sits there as though the world were no more
to him than the Klondyke goldfields are to the
resident population of a cemetery. Yet my neigh-
bour Wolff knows what money is, and appreciates
what can be done with it. Then why doesn’t he
“get a move on” as the Yankees say, and make
hay while the sun shines? Take a peep at him
about nine o’clock at night, when he takes his cus-
tomary exercise on the pavement—the way being
mostly clear of pedestrians by then—and you will
need no verbal answer to the question. Stocky and
strong as he appears when in his chair, he makes a
poor show on his legs. Slowly, heavily, leaning
hard on his thick stick, he goes to and fro a few
times between the corner and his house —a dis-
tance of 200 feet may be—and then with a sigh
sinks again into his chair like a man who realises
he is out of the race. A paralytic stroke two
years ago, and the constant fear of another—that
explains it. He is orie of the multitude who are
in the world and not of it—as this woman was a
while since:—
“From my girlhood,” she says, “I way always
oppressed and hindered by weakness; I was
habitually tired, weary, and languid. Like all
such persons, I lived a kind of life to myself. All
pleasures or pursuits which called for energy and
vitality were closed to me. I had a feeble and
fitful appetite, and what I ate gave me great pain
at the chest and back, and a sense of coldness,
deadness, and weight at the stomach. At times
there would be also a gnawing, sinking feeling.
“ As I grew older I was much troubled with
palpitation of the heart and loss of sleep. Year
after year I dragged along in this miserable way,
and, being tod weak to walk, was drawn from
place to place in a bath chair. Every effort was
made to obtain a cure. I consulted doctor after
doctor, and took many kinds of medicine and
treatment, but got no more than temporary relief.
One doctor said my heart was diseased.
“My sister urged me to try Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. Without any great hopes of its effect
in my case, I nevertheless procured a bottle
from the Stores in High-street, and began to
take it as directed. The result was a decidedly
improved appetite, and I relished and digested
what I ate without any pain or distress following.
As I continued to use this medicine the heart
trouble was less frequent and finally ceased alto-
gether. My nerves soon became stronger, and I
conld sleep well. In a few weeks more I was
letter and stronger than ever before in my life, and
have enjoyed good health ever since.' My friends
were almost as much surprised and pleased as I
was at this remarkable result of taking Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and you may make my statement
public if you think it will do good.”—(Signed)
(Mrs.) Mary Ann Welch, 1 Constitution Hill,
Luton, Chatham, February 6, 1895.
My neighbor Wolff may or may not recover
from his paralysis, but all cases ■like” Mrs. Welch’s
—indigestion, with consequent weakness and ner-
vous debility—should be curable by the remedy
which cured her. It opens and renews the diges-
tion, which is the fountain of life, and puts a
wholly new aspect on the world around us. From
having been laid aside as cumberersof the ground,
we become once more workers with others and
sharers of their pleasures.

Too Much Meat
Too Much Tea.
DYSPEPSIA
Arrives and Departs-
The Case of
OCTAVIUS L. COWAN, Esq.
(For the “ Kiama Reporter.")
Proceeding according to instructions in
the matter, ourreporter called at the “School
of Arts,” Kiama, N.S. VV. On Mr. Cowan
making his appearance, and being informed
of the business in hand, he gave the news-
paper man the following highly interesting
particulars.
“I suppose,” said Mr. Cowan (who is
Curator) “ there is no part of the globe where
indigestion and its various resultant troubles
are so rife as in these colonies.”
“ Yes—how do you account for that ? ”
“ Too much meat, and too much tea, too
much frying pan and generally too little
attention to the art of cooking. You see,”
pursued Mr. Cowan, in an explanatory way,
“ tea and meat don’t agree together. We
take them together it is true, but, as a
consequence, we Australians are the greatest
sufferers from derangements of the digestive
apparatus in the world. ”
“Did you ever suffer from indigestion
asked the newspaper man.
“Indeed I did, and very badly, too, and
I’ve little doubt its predisposing cause was
inattention to my diet. I received significant
warning of the approach of the complaint
by a peculiar oppressive pain which made
itself felt in the chest after meals. A kind
of a dull ache. It felt just as though my
food was sticking therein a lump, and would
not budge. I tried all sorts of things, but
nothing would rid me of this distressing pain;
and I was very careful what I ate and drank.
Very soon a great many other symptoms
showed themselves.
“ Tell me of them,” said the reporter.
“ Well— I was troubled very badly with
flatulence. Everything I ate took an extra-
ordinarily long time to digest. Sometimes
it wouldn’t digest at all, but fermented and
generated a sour gas. This would cause
distension and acute suffering. Then my
head ached very badly at times; indeed, I
always felt heavy and languid, and, though
weary, my sleep was much disturbed, and
always uneasy and unrefreshing. Many
nights I obtained no sleep whatever, owing
to the acute pain I suffered.”
“ Were you all right in other respects ? ”
“Oh, dear, no!I lost considerable weight,
and I was much pulled down in every way.”
“ What about the medicines you say you
tried.”
“ Oh, these much vaunted indigestion cures
are so much time and money wasted—they
only give the stomach so much more foreign
matter to dispose of, and they weaken one, too.
I got no relief till I found a powerful restor-
ative in Clements Tonic. This remedy had
a most wonderful effect upon my indigestion.
Even after half a bottle or so the hard lump
which I continually felt in my chest appeared
to soften and began to go away, and I com-
menced to get a decent appetite again.
During all my illness I could hardly eat
anything, but Clements Tonic as good as
forced me to eat. It made me quite hungry.
And sleepy, too, I believe, for I slept right
through the nights after the dose which I
always took at bed time.”
“ And the flatulence ? ”
“That went, too, in quick time, and I
started to put on flesh again, and you may
be sure it wasn’t long before I got as right as
anything, for Clements Tonic, naturally
enough, performed a thorough cure. After the
way Clements Tonic pulled me round I can
unreservedly recommend it.”
“I ask permission to publish your case ? ”
** You have that permission.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.

in the Colony of New South Wales, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of three folios and
consecutively numbered from one to three, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of my

Indigestion
RESULTING from
INFLUENZA
COMPLETELY CURED
The Case of
Mrs. MARY JANE HINTON.
(From the “ Evening News,” Sydney.)
[n response to a special request, our
reporter stepped up as far as Mrs. Hinton’s,
who owns a neat grocer’s shop in Darling
3t., Balmain, Sydney Mrs. Hinton was
behind the counter.
‘ ‘ Can you spare a few minutes to chat)
about Clements Tonic?” asked the reporter.
“ With the greatest of pleasure,” replied
the lady, and our reporter took out his note
book and seated himself.
“ You know I had influenza,” explained
that lady, * ‘ and that complaint pulls one down
terribly—not so much the illness itself, but
what it left behind, gave me the most
trouble. In my case it left me with indiges-
tion. The doctor said my liver was
deranged, and possibly he was right. On
many occasions I was seized with frightful
headaches, and continually felt so heavy
and sluggish that I was hardly able to
stand. At night I missed the regular sleep
I had been accustomed to, and so in the
mornings was very ill-fitted to commence
my day’s duties. My food was distasteful to
me, and many times I rose from a meal
unable to get down a single mouthful.
Somehow nearly everything I ate made me
feel sick, and an uncomfortable feeling of
tightness in the chest would remain for
hours after eating. On some occasions when
I was particularly bad this tight feeling
would increase till the pain was unbearable.”
“ I expect your bad health was a draw-
back in your business?” v
“You are right there. All the strength
was taken out of me, and I felt so low and
worried that I could take small interest in
anything.”
“ Did your liver affliction betray itself in
other ways ? ”
“ Well, sometimes directly after eatifi'g I
was attacked with severe cramps in the
stomach. It was a strange startling pain,
and was very terrible whilst it lasted. As
may be expected, I didn’t look my usual self,
but gradually got yellow and haggard. I
tried lots of things—all sorts of medicines,
but nothing had any action on my liver, or
did me any good in any way, till I obtained
a bottle of Clements Tonic, and that remedy
soon made a great change in the state of my
health. It had a fine bracing effect upon
my worn-out system, and took away that
heavy sluggishness in a very short time.
From that moment I could take exercise,
and mended remarkably quick. I could eat
my meals with a healthy relish, without any
suffering or inconveniences afterward, and,
thank goodness, I was able to get my regular
sleep again and wake up bright and cheerful
in the mornings ready to attend to my
business. Clements Tonic soon put me into
a splendid state of health. Nor am I the
only one in our family who has received
benefit from that excellent remedy. When
my husband was weak and ill we flew to
Ciements Tonic to restore him, and it braced
him up in quick time. I also gave Clement*
Tonic to my two growing girls with the
best effects, especially the younger, who
suffered from nervousness for a long tim&
after having had the measles. Clements
Tonic, however, strung up her weakened
nerves, as she can tell you herself, (here Mrs.
Hinton called her daughter—now a fine
strong looking girl—who was good enough
to confirm what her mother had said), in
fact, continued Mrs. Hinton, Clements
Tonic has always come successfully to the
rescue in our family, and, in all seriousness, I
really don’t know what we should have
done without it.” .
“ You will permit me, then, to put this in
the papers?”
“ Yes,” replied Mrs. Hinton, smiling.

A Blind Man’s Holiday.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" He who, outside of pure mathematics, pro-
nounces the word Impossible, lacks prudence.”
—Arago.
Dkuhmond was taking his young wife to Lower
Egypt, beyond the track of tourists. Men told
him that she was pretty ; and one day his dearest
friend hinted that she was something which only
one’s dearest friend is ever permitted to hint. Eor
three minutes after, Drummond spoke with his
shut hand under the friend’s throat.
“ But it’s the truth,” choked the dearest friend.
“You lie!” growled Drummond; and went
away aiter the manner ot men to brood if it might
be true. Was it indeed all for love that she had
wedded him—the lucky miner, but the blind
man ? . . .
* * * *
The Araluen’s search-light wheeled across the
lakes, and lit up the dingy levels of Port Said. A
heron flew out against the blinding glare, beat the
darkness overhead, and flapped into the near
reeds. A man on the upper deck laughed—at the
bird. Thereat a turbaned serang bobbed from the
funnel-stays, and addressed the open hatch.
“Didst thou hear him, Nana? He has not
laughed since the Leuwin, and that night a woman
died. Here, at the canal-head, he laughs again.
I have forgotten his name, but he was known to
thee over there. ” The serang’s fist briefly indicated
Asia.
“ His name is Yelarney, ” said Nana, in the hold.
‘T knew him at Benares; he was a chemist, and
there is not a fakir-wallah in India who can out-
trick him. He is a friend of the blind passenger.”
“The blind passenger has a wife, Nana? ”
The invisible Nana spat into the bowels of the
ship.
“Yes, and all men are her friends. Her feet
are smaller than your palm. She laughs, and by
Allah ! her breasts move up and down. The hus-
band was blinded in the gold-pits at Bendigo.”
A Jew-faced, ear-ringed man stole across the
saloon lights and listened. Three sing-song
words happened in the vernacular, and Nana in
the hold was silent. The ear-ringed man peered
down the saloon stairs : a half-naked woman was
screaming in the passage. He drew back.
“The heat has choked her whelp, and she will
low for a week, like a sulking calf.”
“ She is a mother,” said a near voice.
“ Exactly,” answered the Jew-faced man j “and
for that reason I’d gaol her for anarchy; her music
destroys—me, for instance.” He lit a cigar, and
prowled along the deck, exhibiting a cat-like
preference for the shade. Another figure was
groping near the gangway, groping silently and
with much craft. The Jew-faced man touched
his arm.
“Drummond ? I didn’t know you in the dark.’
Drummond laughed, and adjusted his pith hel-
met clumsily.
“ I’m looking for my wife.”
“ Looking, eh T * The Jew-faced man suppressed
a laugh.
Drummond straightened his bent shoulders and
sighed.
“I forget sometimes, and flounder into the
beaten tracks of language. Have you seen her ? ”
“ Yes, she has gone to the Quai Eugenie, with
other women, to hunt for the heathen with bric-
a-brac. The heathen with bric-a-brac is a snag.”
Blind Drummond turned his face to the saloon
and listened. A woman’s sob broke the stillness.
The punkahs whined monotonously. His lips
twitched :
“M’f! the place smells like a circus full of
Arabs. Let’s go up the Marino ; I’m tired of this
palatial hooker.”
A crowd passed them near the gangway; women
called to each other fretfully, and above the pre-
dominant hysteria came the drastic language of
sailor-men. The Jew-faced man took Drummond’s
arm, and swore through the crowded thorough-
fare until the wing of a terrace shut out the acrid
smell of the port. Lights flared across the open
water; a deep-freighted tramp headed in-shore,
muzzling the rime over her heeling bulkhead
light.
“ Let’s smell the world, Yelarney ; let’s take a
holiday from the sea.”
Velarney grinned, and his ear-rings trembled.
They had halted before an open door, and from
the fern-laden corridor came the soft wailing of a
violin. The blind man touched Yelarney’s sleeve.
“ Listen, a woman plays ; her touch is supreme.”
“Lotus enter,” said Velarney. “Oh, legion,
legion, thy name is bagnio 1 ”
The music ceased, but a voice took it up.
“ Come in out of the desert, messieurs ! ”
The musk-drenched tapestry heaved; a siren
beckoned from a pile of azure velvet.
“ Come in out of the desert! ”
She leaned forward, wrist thrown up to shade
her eyes. Velarney bowed, and the incandescent
lamp lit up his gipsy profile, its lank strength, its
trembling ear-rings. The siren’s hand flew down
to her unlaced wrapper, drawing it about her
naked shoulders.
“We have met before. Thou art—Monsieur
Meph., citizen of the Pit! ”
Velarney lit a cigarette, and the match streaked
flame-red from his fingers.
“ Your teeth are excellent, mademoiselle, and
you always make clean work of your chops. We
have met before—in Gehenna or Dan ; I think it
was in Dan. You were a bar-nymph then,
strong-armed and frothing the beer of human
kindness. What are you now?” He sighed.
“ Nothing but an oasis in the desert of morality ! ”
The “ oasis ” laughed softly.
“We met at Cairo, in ’79. You came as a con-
juror to make fun for the women of the palace.
You made apples grow on Arabi’s candelabra.”
Her brow creased ; she fell amongst the cushions.
“ But for those brigand war-ships I might have
been queen of Egypt. I wish all Britishers were
in hell! ”
Velarney laughed sneeringly, and linked his
arm with the blind man’s. The siren crouched
over.
“Arabi tried to pluck your apples from the
candelabra and burnt his silly fingers. You were
bastinadoed; I saw your wounded heels. Do not
laugh at me, Meph., citizen of the Pit.”
“I had forgotten those wounds,” murmured
Velarney, “ until your red lips recalled them.”
A short-legged Zouave stole forward from the
silken background.
“By Kismet! You shall not taunt Imogene.
Your business?” he demanded.
Imogene made a stiletto-thrust with her fan.
“He has come to smoke. Do not strike him,
Alberto, until the opium has quelled his spirit;
then I will watch you kicking his ribs.”
Velarney grinned, and threw down a sovereign.
“ There are cushions on your housetop,
Alberto; we would rest awhile. My friend here
is blind.”
Alberto, breathing apologies, led them along the
passage. He explained that Imogene had supped,
and Imogene was always volatile after a heavy
meal of English ham. Under his breath he said,
“I will hold this sovereign very tight. Velarney
is a conjuror-man.”
He left them on the roof, and felt his pocket for
a touch of the sovereign; it was there; he smiled.
* * * *
“Here are the pipes,” said Velarney. “Will
you smoke ? ”
Blind Drummond wriggled over the mat and
laughed.
“Anything for a spell. I suppose I shall see
dragons and hear the tread of innumerable
armies.”
“Don’t smoke if you are not inured. Don’t
smoke if you are happy ; and you are happy, aren’t
you ? You have a wife.”
“Oh, yes ; I’m pretty happy, thanks.” Drum-
mond’s breath came in sharp expulsions. “If it
hadn’t been for her I’d have snapped the thread
long ago. You don’t know what she’s been to me,
and ... I think I’ll let the pipe alone. ”
“H’m ! You are rich ; your wants are nil.
Lucky dog! ”
“I know it.” Drummond squirmed, and
brought his chin to the level of the coping. “ But
one can’t help a thought of the things around one
—the look in a woman’s face, the sun, and this
desert that reeks of camel and Pharaoh. A blind
man misses a lot of good things. ”
“And some nasty ones,” Velarney purred
softly. From his silver-mounted pipe came a
preliminary slaver. A faint drip , drip causrht
Drummond’s ear ; he leaned forward.
“ What’s that ? ”
Velarney put down the pipe and swore softl>
“ Water from the cistern ; what a damned
nuisance ! It’s enough to wear a man away. Can
stand most things when I’m smoking, but I bar
the sound of water just when I’m going down to
hell.”
He lifted himself painfully, and looked into the
street below. Drummond sighed, and groped to a
divan. A strain of Gounod’s “Faust” blew up
from the Casino, but the wind veered suddenly,
checking it in mid-octave. Silence again, and the
dripping in the spout became of surpassing inter-
e?t‘, Velarney spun round from the parapet, and
choked back a lunatic laugh.
“ What’s the matter?” said Drummond, queru-
lously. ror heaven’s sake, keep quiet.”
“I saw a woman on the pavement, and she
doesn t know whether to come in or not. There’s
a man, of course.”
-Drummond’s face was screwed-up in thought
and the words were only half-heard, but he felt
the strange man leaning over him, felt the hot
breath on his cheek. The drip, drip from the
cistern cut his qumme-laden nerves ; unconsciously
le began to count the drops. Chin thrust out, his
white bps followed the accumulating units until
each drop seemed like the peck, peck of a sparrow
at his brain. He whimpered feebly, closed his
ears, but the now terrific drip would not be shut
out; the pecking stroke became a clank that
vibrated in trip-hammer smashes on the zinc floor
below.
1 his is an enfilade !” he gasped. A silence
caine that stung him like whips, and sharp upon it
an explosion that seemed to test the limits of
sound. The blind man quaked, and clutched his
forehead. Suddenly a gleaming centre - piece
seemed to shiver the black roof above him, spilling
a myriad stars about the empty darkness. One
luminous disc shot down to his forehead; he
screamed, threw up his guard, but the flame struck
him where the pecking had been.
‘ ‘ Oh, God ! I can see ! ”
“What!” said Velarney. “No! .. . but it
is possible,” he muttered.
Drummond leaped upwards, but Velarney caught
the frail body and held it like a vice.
“Be still, thou jumping-jack, and . . . if it is
really so . . . be glad ! ”
Drummond clutched the strong wrists that
pinned him against the parapet.
“Let go! I can see— see!— do you hear ? I’ve
been shut up in darkness for ten years. I won’t
move a limb, I promise you. Let’s look up, old
man ; even through your fingers I see stars : look
at em !—look at ’em ! Was it the water—or you ?
The doctors told me the nerve had been simply
paralysed by that cursed explosion a shock
might ...”
He fought to catch a glimpse of skv, but Velar-
ney locked him mercilessly to the roof.
, “ Never mind the stars ! Poets call them stars ;
they are merely tears, frozen tears of the unhappy
blest. Be still! ”
Velarney shook him angrily, but the hand that
caressed his face thrilled him like a woman’s
touch.
... c ?' n se . e how strange!—l never knew you were
like this—it is not your conjuring, is it ? We’ve
been pals, Velarney, and I’ve helped you a bit.
Don’t take away the light; eh, you shan’t! my
God ! you shan’t! ”
It seemed to him as though an iron band had
been shaken from his eyes; the light had come
with a crash, and presto! the world took shape
again ; the formless void became spans of sea and
shore overhung by a luminous Eastern night. As
thirst-tortured men drink their fill, so he drank in
the sights around him. The near town was a
crimson flare in the desert. The Quai Eugenie
was aheave with black ships and lantern-lit poops
—white-beaked galleons smelling of grain and
fruit and alive with Norsemen, Lascars and bull-
throated Danes. Yesterday, all sense of color was
dead within him ; now his vision was crowded with
illuminations as sudden and bewildering as though
a myriad lamps had flashed across a rainbow.
. Velarney’s quick hands held his temples sooth-
ingly.

“It is true, he sees ! . . . But be still, they’re
talking below. Here’s the trap-door, take a peep.”
Drummond whimpered hysterically, and scram-
bled to the opening.
“It is blurred a little,” he murmured, “but I
see t ”
“ Sister Ann,” laughed Yelarney, “ what do you
see ? ”
Drummond’s whisper was sharp and clear.
“I see the woman Imogene, with a bottle of
wine. She is ”
A heel clinked on the tiles below. Imogene’s
voice followed the pop of a cork.
“Come in out of the desert, monsieur ! ”
‘ ‘ Look towards the end of the passage, ” whis-
pered Yelarney.
“ A ship’s officer, and a lady,” said Drummond.
“He is our skipper—skipper of the Araluen,”
droned Velarney.
Drummond rubbed his hands and chuckled.
“ The lady hangs back as if afraid. I cannot
make her out. Is she a passenger ? ”
“She is.”
Velarney’s voice was strangely calm. . . .
* * « •
The woman shrank from the painted siren, from
the wine-filled tumbler offered in such haste. The
siren’s peal of laughter filled the corridor.
“It is good wine, girl, see; tawny and beaded
like a nun. Drink ! ”
Her left eyelid dropped to the gilded officer.
He stepped forward and wagged his thick beard
after the manner of Bacchus m the cheap prints.
“ Blessed juice of the berry ! ” he said hoarsely,
and wagged it again.
The woman recoiled, frightened and irresolute;
her hands went back, groping for the tapestry. She
looked at Imogene, but Imogene dodged the
glance, and pulled the captain’s sleeve.
“ Sailor, thy mistress flees ! ”
The captain swore in surprise as the woman
turned swiftly in the passage and disappeared.
Imogene put the tumbler into his fist. He swore
again, but drank.
* * * •
Drummond, with shut lips, watched the comedy
below. Then he thought of the woman who had
checked herself on the threshold of the bagnio.
And she was one of the Araluen’s passengers,
some foolish woman who had become enamoured
of the gold-laced libertine commanding the' ship.
He wondered if she had ever fraternised with his
wife—Louise. . . . Louise !
His thought stopped for a period of six heart-
beats. He put up his hand to blot out the painful
light, to wipe the sudden moisture from his lean
temples.
Velarney had seen her over the parapet. He
would ask Velarney her name. He coughed.
Velarney turned and reached for the pipe.
“Feel queer, eh? You look as if you’d seen
God —or the Gentleman from Gehenna. Better
have a smoke ; it’ll steady you. Or, no; shut
your eyes and keep quiet. Mustn’t use them too
much ; or you’ll deprive the medical profession of
a curio. These sudden cures are deadly. ”
“ I’m all right, thanks. Things have been a bit
sudden—the light, I mean ; . it, seems to have
skewered the nerve—l can’t believe it’s true; I’ll
be very careful. By the way, who was the girl
that ran out? I was wondering if I knew her—
that is, by name. ”
Velarney pondered. Should he tell him, or
would it be better to let the end shape itself ? The
world had been miraculously re-created for this
writhing atom; but how booh came the pain i
He sighed; the problem was beyond his philo-
sophy ; this night was very wonderful.
Drummond spoke again nervously:
“I’m an awful cad to ask such a question ; but
one can’t help one’s animal curiosity. Perhaps I
can guess. How does Mrs. Minthorp hit you ?
She was always gadding with the officers.”
“ No,” said Velarney.”
“ The Creston woman, then ? ”
“No.” . ..
Drummond rattled off a score of names in
feverish haste, and Velarney answered sadly.
“No-no-no.”
• • - * •
Drummond lay back: a little repose might clear
his brain. He had named almost every woman in
the saloon, every woman except his wife. There
were the steerage-passengers, to be sure, but . . .
He must go back to the ship and meet her face
to face, thought Velarney ; even yet he is not
convinced, he is not sure: he will catch at any
straw to save himself from the truth ; there are
such men.
“And, if you won’t smoke,” he said, “we’d
better go.”
Drummond shivered under the desert wind.
One star showed clear against the dawn, and the
sea stretched like a floor of cloud all over the wide
hollow in the east.
“Look ! Day is creeping through Asia. Your
wife will be waiting: she may catch cold. ”
Drummond heard him as one man hears another
through a stone wall. The woman who had fled
the bagnio might be waiting to greet him—her
face would tell tales; and—he opened his eyes
cautiously to make sure—he could see; he would
know, know 1 He steadied himself against the
coping. . . .
“Velarney!”
Drummond’s voice was sure and full of strength.
Velarney trembled.
“ This light hurts, cuts me through and through;
put it out. I’ve got money; ask it, but put out
the light.”
Velarney thought—and approached him gently.
“It will hurtand ... if I can . . . remember! —
this time it will be out for always. Are you sure
it is best ? ”
Drummond never quavered. “It is best. Put
out the light ! ”
“Out it is!”
“ Heu /”
Drummond crouched under the shock of the
blow—the shock of the darkness that struck him
like a slamming door. “Wait!” . . . he mut-
tered. And, after a while, “I am blind, lead
me ! ”
“Friend, thou hast indeed taught me philo-
sophy,” whispered Velarney. “We will return to
the ship. ”
“I am ready.”
Velarney took the groping hand gently, very
gently. They passed down the stair.
ALBA DORION.
Lord Charles Beresfqrd asked Kinder, an
Englishman, Engineer-in-Chief of Chinese Rail-
ways, why he used American locomotives instead
of English. He replied that the English charge
was J 52500, the engine to be delivered in two
years, whereas the Baldwin Co., U.S., delivered
it in five months for .£lB5O.
After the Horses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Have they ever waked you early
While the shadows lingered yet,
Ere the gray light gathered fairly
And the drowsy stars had set ?
Have you heard the wagtails chirrup,
Felt the dawn-wind creeping cold ?
Till your foot was in the stirrup
And the whole world turned to gold !
Ah ! the joy of daybreak riding !
How it makes the warm blood start,
With a good horse striding, striding,
To the music of your heart!
And the morning’s welcome olden,
With the magpies all astir,
When the long wet grasses golden
Wipe the dry blood from the spur 1
How they gather from their feeding
In the white mists far and near,
Take the track with Sunflower leading
And old ’Possum in the rear !
How the bare hoofs rattle under,
For the Cowrie fillies know
When they hear the snake-plait thunder
It is time for them to go !
Ah ! the joy of it, ye riders !
When the old gray horse takes hold
Through the bushes where the spiders
Are spinning threads of gold !
Down the box-flats where the grasses
Dip silver with the dew,
And the last brown shadow passes
As the Fairy Dawn steps through !
On the sandhill hear them muster ;
’Cross the roadway see them swing;
In the lignums how they cluster !
Down the netting how they string 1
Rolling fat, yet lithe and limber,
Shoulders creamed with sweat and foam;
Head them at the river timber—
Rush them up the flat for home 1
Gone for ever yon glad hour is ;
When the sunrise floods the plain ;
I shall never run the Cowries
Down the blue-grass flats again.
Drop the slip-rails in their places,
Take the saddle from the gray;
Foamy shoulders, white-starred faces,
Fade and fade and fade away !
But if you have fought the forces
Of the Drought and Dust and Sun,
If you’ve ever tailed the horses
On a far-out western run,
If you’ve ever heard them neighing
As they gathered to the whip,
You will understand me saying
I would let a fortune slip
Just to take the old malacca
From its place upon the wall,
With a foot of silken cracker
And a yard of greenhide fall,
And be down those watercourses
Where the ten-foot lignums are,
Bringing up the station horses
On a reefing “KS Bar ”!
WILL H. OGILVIE.
A Vignette.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a middle-aged British citizen of the upper
middle class, and just like any other B.C. who
is ready on the slightest provocation to write to
the papers. But he was singular in that he had
no growing boys and girls to wonder what he
would do with.- Only a young and pretty wife.
Presently, far away down the long street, he
heard the tinkle-tinkle-tinkle and the call of the
muffin-man, and looking at his watch he saw it
was past 4 o’clock. “Gad, some crumpets would
go well. I shall tell James to get some, my dear.”
He called his young wife “my dear”—a fatal
thing- to do. She wasmqch too young and dashing.
She called Arm “a dear old thing,” and he told
her. all sorts of interesting stories. No doubt he
thought he knew all about the game of husband
and wife. But this is by the way.
Presently he strolled to the window, looked out
upon the street, and saw a bedraggled jnan and
woman trudging along the pavement, wreckage
from a sluinmier quarter. “Come here, my
dear,” he said, “and.have a good look at this
miserable couple, and I will tell you a story.”
“ Pooh ! About that wretched old woman?
Couldn’t be bothered listening, thank you ! ”
“ She was not always repulsive, my dear. She,
too, once, felt all the soft changeableness of the
olimate of Love.”
“ Which accounts for her weather-beaten ap-
pearance to-day, I should say; you dear, sentimen-
tal old thing ! But was she really ever pretty ? ”
“ Not merely pretty ; she was magnetic, and as
much sought after as a woman could wish.”
“Well, tell me her story ; or, rather, the first
part of it —since I know the end.”
“The beginning, my dear, was on her wedding
morn. She looked pale and irresolute, and they
gave her a stiff glass of brandy to give her courage.
She has sought courage in the same way ever since.
That is her story. The man
“ I don’t care about the man’s part, thank you.
Is that her husband with her ? ”
“ No, my dear. He is the interesting part of
the story. ”
